Introducing al-Mawaqif: A Landmark Work in Kalam
Defines the book's subject and lists commentators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subject and significance of the book al-Mawaqif and know the names of its first commentators.

Core Idea
Al-Mawaqif is a foundational kalam work with renowned commentators.

This lesson introduces the book al-Mawaqif, a major work in the discipline of kalam (theology). We will learn what subject it covers, who it was written for, and which early scholars wrote commentaries on it. This sets the stage for studying al-Mawaqif's content.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the statement that the book is in 'ilm al-kalam'.
2
Identify the commentators
List the three named commentators: al-Jurjani, al-Kirmani, and al-Abhari. Also note that others wrote commentaries and there are many glosses.
The commentators are prominent scholars.
3
Reflect on the book's status
Consider the description of the book as 'jaleel al-qadr, rafee' al-shaan' (great worth, lofty status). Why do you think it was so highly regarded?
This leads to the next lessons on its content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف بالكتاب : هو في علم الكلام ألفه لغياث الدين وزير خدا بنده وهو كتاب جليل القدر، رفيع الشأن، اعتنى به الفضلاء فشرحه السيد الشريف الجرجاني ومحمد بن يوسف الكرماني، وسيف الدين الأبهري وغيرهم، وعليه حواش كثيرة .
Definition of the book: It is in the science of kalam (ilm al-kalam). He authored it for Ghiyath al-Din, the vizier of Khuda Bandeh. It is a book of great worth, lofty status. The virtuous scholars attended to it, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, Muhammad ibn Yusuf al-Kirmani, and Sayf al-Din al-Abhari, and others, wrote commentaries (sharh) on it, and it has many glosses (hawashi).
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage defines the book 'al-Mawaqif' as a work on kalam (ilm al-kalam) and notes its patron, Ghiyath al-Din. It affirms the book's high scholarly standing and lists the first generation of commentators: al-Jurjani, al-Kirmani, and al-Abhari, stating that they, along with others, wrote commentaries (sharh) on it, and that it has numerous glosses (hawashi).

Key Terms
علم الكلام
شرح
حواش
الجرجاني
الكرماني
الأبهري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Mawaqif is a book on logic due to its title.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is in ilm al-kalam, not logic.

Checkpoint
What is the subject of the book 'al-Mawaqif' according to this passage?
A) Logic
B) Fiqh
C) Kalam
D) Grammar

Key Takeaway
Al-Mawaqif is a highly esteemed work on kalam, with early commentaries by al-Jurjani, al-Kirmani, and al-Abhari, among others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This book, al-Mawaqif, is described as 'of great worth, lofty status,' and yet you have probably never read a single line of it. What does that say about your own pursuit of knowledge? Are you content to remain ignorant of the foundations that scholars have labored over for centuries?
?
The virtuous scholars did not merely read al-Mawaqif; they wrote commentaries and glosses, engaging deeply with its arguments. In contrast, you likely skim translations or summaries. Will you commit to studying at least one original text like this with the same devotion?
?
Al-Mawaqif was authored for a vizier, a man of power. But its value transcends politics. What does that teach you about the priority of 'ilm over worldly status? Are you willing to sacrifice time and comfort to truly understand this science?

The Book's Fame and the Request for Commentary
Author describes the book's fame and friends' request for commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the context behind the writing of this commentary and how the author describes the impact of the original work.

Core Idea
Fame of the original work prompted a request for commentary.

The author begins by praising his own work, describing its vast fame and the profound effect it had on scholars. He then tells how his close friends, eager to understand its deeper meanings, asked him to write a commentary. This passage sets the stage for the entire commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the vivid metaphors like 'like the sun at midday'.
2
Identify the author's description of the book's fame
Look for phrases that describe the book's renown and its effect on scholars. The author uses strong language: 'became infatuated', 'bewildered'.
3
Understand the request and the author's response
Who requested the commentary and why? How did the author respond? This explains the purpose of the commentary.
The friends are described as 'noble beloved ones eager to access the secrets'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرره بنان التدقيق في ضمن عبارات رائقة معجزة وإشارات شائقة موجزة، فصار بذلك في الاشتهار، كالشمس في رابعة النهار، واستمال إليه بصائر أولي الأبصار، من أذكياء الأمصار والأقطار فاستهتروا بكنوز عباراته الجامعة ولم يجدوا عليها دليلاً، واستهيموا برموز إشاراته اللامعة ولم يهتدوا إليها سبيلاً، فاجتمع إلي نفر من أجلة الأحباب المتطلعين إلى سرائر الكتاب واقترحوا علي أن أكشف لهم عن مخدراته الأستار، وأبرز لهم من نقاب الحجاب هاتيك الأسرار ليجتلوها بأعينهم متبرجات بزينتها، متبخترات بمحاسن فطرتها، فأسعفتهم إلى ذلك متمسكاً بحبل التوفيق.
He polished it with the finger of meticulous revision within miraculous eloquent phrases and succinct enchanting indications, so it became in fame like the sun at midday, and it attracted the insights of those with vision from the intelligent of the regions and quarters; they became infatuated with the treasures of its comprehensive phrases and found no guide to them, and they became bewildered by the luminous symbols of its indications and found no way to them. So a group of noble beloved ones gathered to me, eager to access the secrets of the book, and they proposed that I unveil for them the veils of its hidden meanings, and bring forth for them from the screen of the veil those secrets so that they might behold them with their eyes, adorned with their beauty, strutting with the virtues of their nature. So I responded to them, holding fast to the rope of success.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an author's self-praise of the original work (likely the Mawaqif) and a description of how it captivated scholars. The author states that the book was polished by meticulous editing, resulting in eloquent and miraculous phrases and concise indications. Its fame became like the sun at midday. It attracted the insights of intelligent people who became infatuated with its comprehensive phrases and bewildered by its luminous symbols. A group of noble friends, eager to uncover the book's secrets, asked the author to write a commentary that would unveil the hidden meanings. The author agreed to fulfill their request, holding fast to divine success. The passage sets the stage for the commentary's creation.

Key Terms
اشتهار
استمال
بصائر
استهتروا
استهيموا
مخدرات
أسرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is merely boasting about his own work.
Correction: The author is not merely boasting but emphasizing the need for a commentary, as the original work's depth required explanation for eager scholars.

Checkpoint
Why did the author decide to write the commentary?
A) He was ordered by the Sultan
B) A group of friends requested it
C) He sought fame
D) The original book was incomplete

Key Takeaway
The commentary was written in response to friends' request to unveil the secrets of the highly acclaimed original work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a profound Islamic text, do you feel the same infatuation and bewilderment as those scholars? Or do you quickly move on, content with surface understanding? What step will you take today to seek a guide to its deeper treasures?
?
The author held fast to the rope of success when asked to share knowledge. If a sincere seeker asks you to explain something of the deen, do you respond eagerly with reliance on Allah, or do you hesitate out of fear or pride? What holds you back?
?
The book's fame was like the sun, yet its secrets remained veiled. Is the faith you profess a sun that illuminates your actions, or are its deeper realities still hidden behind a veil you have not dared to remove? What cost are you willing to pay to have those veils lifted?

The Opening of the Commentary: Basmalah, Hamdalah, and Lexical Glosses
Commentator begins commenting on original text's basmalah and hamdalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator introduces his work, the purpose of the basmalah and hamdalah in kalam texts, and the lexical glosses that follow.

Core Idea
The opening of a kalam text is not mere formality; it sets the stage for the entire discourse.

This passage marks the beginning of the commentary on a major work of Islamic theology. The author first seeks divine assistance, then explains why the original author began with the basmalah and hamdalah. He then provides lexical clarifications and ends with a humble plea for the reader's forbearance. Understanding this opening helps us appreciate the scholarly etiquette and methodological concerns of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the commentator's transition
Notice the phrase 'وها أنا أفيض في المقصود' and what it signals about the beginning of the commentary.
This phrase marks the shift to the core commentary.
3
Examine the purpose of basmalah and hamdalah
According to the commentator, why did the original author begin with basmalah and hamdalah? Look for the term 'براعة الاستهلال'.
The basmalah is not just a blessing; it alludes to the purposes of kalam.
4
Study the lexical definitions
The commentator defines several key terms from the original text. Find at least three and note their meanings.
These definitions show the commentator's attention to precise language.
5
Reflect on the closing plea
The commentator asks the reader to be forgiving of errors. What does this reveal about scholarly humility?
This plea sets a tone of modesty and cooperation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وها أنا أفيض في المقصود، متوكلاً على الصمد المعبود، فأقول : قال المصنف : بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. ضمن خطبة كتابه الإشارة إلى مقاصد علم الكلام رعاية لبراعة الاستهلال، فبسمل أولاً تيمناً، ثم قال: (الحمد لله العلي) شأنه أمره وحاله في ذاته، وصفاته. والمكارم جمع مكرمة بضم الراء فعل الكرم ضد اللؤم، والمآثر جمع مأثرة وهي المكرمة لأنها تؤثر أي تذكر أي ما يؤثرها قرن بعد قرن، المدى الغاية، الصوارم جمع صارمة من صرمت الشيء قطعته، الفلول جمع الفل بالفتح وهو الكسر في حد السيف، الرباع جمع ربع وهو الدار بعينها، يقال : روى وروى ورواء كغنى وإلى وسماء كثير، مرو، والبهاء الحسن. والمأمول من الأذكياء المتحلين بحلي الإنصاف المتخلين عن رذيلتي البغي والاعتساف، إذا عثروا على شيء زلت فيه القدم، أو طغى به القلم أن يستحضروا أن لكل جواد كبوة، ولكل صارم نبوة.
And now I pour forth into the intended purpose, relying on the Eternal, the Worshipped, and I say: The author said: In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Within the preface of his book, he alludes to the purposes of the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) in observance of the excellence of the opening. So he began with the basmalah for blessing, then said: 'Praise be to God, the High'—His majesty, His command, and His state in His essence and attributes. And 'al-makārim' is plural of 'mukramah' with ḍammah on the rā', the act of generosity, opposite of baseness. 'Al-ma'āthir' is plural of 'ma'atharah', which is a noble deed because it is transmitted (tu'aththar), i.e., remembered, that which is transmitted generation after generation. 'Al-madā' is the goal. 'Al-ṣawārim' is plural of 'ṣārimah' from 'ṣaramtu al-shay'a' I cut it. 'Al-fulūl' is plural of 'al-fall' with fatḥ, which is the notch in the edge of the sword. 'Al-ribāʿ' is plural of 'rubʿ', which is the house itself. It is said: rawā, rawā, and rawā' like ghinā, ilā, and samā' meaning much. 'Marw' [a type of stone?]. 'Al-bahā'' is beauty. And the hope from the intelligent who are adorned with the jewelry of fairness, divested of the two vices of transgression and oppression, that when they stumble upon something wherein a foot slips or a pen oversteps, they bring to mind that every steed has a stumble and every sharp sword has a bluntness.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage marks the author's transition to the core commentary. He says 'وها أنا أفيض في المقصود' (And now I pour forth into the intended purpose), signaling the beginning of the explanation. He then quotes the original author's opening: 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم' (In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful). The commentator explains that the original author placed the basmalah in his preface to indicate the purposes of ʿilm al-kalām, following the convention of excellent opening (براعة الاستهلال). The original author then said 'الحمد لله العلي' (Praise be to God, the High), and the commentator glosses that 'العلي' refers to His majesty, command, and state in His essence and attributes. Next, the commentator provides lexical definitions of several terms from the original author's passage: 'المكارم' (noble deeds) plural of 'مكرمة' (act of generosity), 'المآثر' (traditions of noble deeds) plural of 'مأثرة' (a noble deed remembered and transmitted), 'المدى' (goal), 'الصوارم' (sharp swords), 'الفلول' (notches in sword edges), 'الرباع' (dwellings), and 'البهاء' (beauty). Finally, he expresses his hope (المأمول) from intelligent readers who are fair and free from oppression: if they encounter errors (a slip of the foot or pen), they should remember that every steed stumbles and every sharp sword has a bluntness—a plea for leniency in judging the work.

Key Terms
المكارم
المآثر
المدى
الصوارم
الفلول
الرباع
البهاء
براعة الاستهلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The basmalah is just a formal opening with no substantive connection to the content.
Correction: The commentator explains that the basmalah is placed to allude to the purposes of ʿilm al-kalām in observance of the excellence of the opening, showing its substantive role.

Checkpoint
Why did the original author begin with basmalah according to the commentator?
A) To seek blessings only
B) To indicate the purposes of ʿilm al-kalām
C) To follow the sunnah
D) To praise the ruler

Key Takeaway
The commentator's opening reveals the purposeful placement of basmalah and hamdalah, defines key terms, and humbly requests the reader's understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone corrects your mistake, do you respond with humility like the commentator requests, or do you react with pride? Will you commit to accept correction gracefully, even from those you consider less knowledgeable?
?
Do you treat the basmalah and hamdalah at the beginning of Islamic texts as mere ritual, or do you pause to reflect on their deeper purpose—that they set the stage for the entire discourse? How can you change this habit starting today?
?
The commentator asks his readers to be fair and forgiving of errors. Is there a person you have been harsh or unfair towards because of their mistakes? Will you forgive them sincerely before you finish this lesson?

6 min
Defending the Fa' in the Basmalah
Commentator defends the fa' in 'fabsamala' via objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the fa' in 'fabsamala' is correctly placed, despite the basmalah being a generic opening.

Core Idea
The basmalah's blessing sought first makes the fa' appropriate as a marker of simple order.

This passage delves into a seemingly minor grammatical point—the use of the letter fa'—but it reveals a key methodological principle: how a scholar frames the beginning of his work to signal his intentions. The commentator defends the placement of the fa' by explaining that the basmalah, while a standard opening, plays a crucial role in setting the stage for the specific indication of kalam purposes. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent claims that the basmalah is generic and cannot indicate specific purposes, so the fa' is misplaced. Notice the logic of the objection.
The basmalah appears at the start of every book, so it doesn't signal anything specific about kalam.
2
Understand the first defense
The commentator says the basmalah is for tayammun (seeking blessing), and only after this blessing does the inclusion of indication become effective. Thus the fa' denotes mere sequence, not causation.
The basmalah is temporally later than the intention of inclusion.
3
Cite the authority
Ibn Hisham in Mughni al-Labib states that fa' can indicate mere arrangement, not just consequence. This legitimizes the fa'.
There are many examples in the Quran.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
If the basmalah is part of the preface, then the indication occurs within it, not at the very beginning. This also justifies the fa'.
The basmalah would then be included in the preface alongside the indication.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
Even if basmalah has no role in brāʿat al-istihlāl, it contributes to the inclusion (tadmin), and the fa' remains correct as indicating mere arrangement, with detail following summary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وها أنا أفيض في المقصود، متوكلاً على الصمد المعبود، فأقول : قال المصنف : بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم ضمن خطبة كتابه الإشارة إلى مقاصد علم الكلام رعاية لبراعة الاستهلال، فبسمل أولاً تيمناً، ثم قال: (الحمد لله العلي شأنه) أمره وحاله في ذاته، وصفاته وأفعاله... قوله: (فبسمل أولاً تيمناً) فإن قلت: ليس للبسملة مدخل في الإشارة المذكورة، لأن البسملة مما يطرد في أول كل كتاب من كل فن فلا تحصل بها الإشارة إلى المقاصد الآتية، فلا للفاء . قلت : تضمن خطبة كتابه الإشارة إلى مقاصد علم الكلام إنما يستحسن ويعتد به، ويعد تفوقاً في ابتداء الكتاب بعد رعاية التيمن ببسم الله فكأنه قال : أراد التضمين المذكور، فبسمل أولاً تيمناً ليعتد بذلك التضمن فالفاء حينئذ أصاب موقعه على أنها قد تجيء لمجرد الترتيب كما ذكره ابن هشام في مغني اللبيب وله أمثلة كثيرة في القرآن المجيد، والظاهر أن البسملة متأخرة زماناً عن التضمين الذي أريد به سببه أعني الإرادة، وقد يتوهم أنه أراد بالتضمين المذكور الإيراد في ضمن الخطبة أي أثنائها فللبسملة مدخل في ذلك حينئذ إذ لو لم يبسمل أولاً لكانت الإشارة في أول الخطبة لا في أثنائها، وتقدم جملة الحمدلة لا يكفي لأن قوله العلي شأنه الخ سواء اعتبر بدلاً عن لفظة الله أو نعتاً له من متمماتها، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف، نعم يمكن أن يقال : على تقدير كون البسملة جزءاً من الخطبة لفظ التضمين يشعر باشتمال الخطبة على شيء آخر سوى الإشارة المذكورة، فللبسملة على قصد التيمن مدخل في التضمين وإن لم يكن لها مدخل في براعة الاستهلال وبهذا يظهر حسن موقع الفاء إذا حملت على مجرد الترتيب أيضاً، ولو بالنسبة إلى نفس التضمين لأن مرتبة التفصيل متأخرة عن مرتبة الإجمال.
And I now proceed to the purpose, relying on the Eternal, the Worshipped, and I say: The author said: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful,' having included in the preface of his book an indication of the purposes of the science of kalam in observance of the art of an elegant opening. So he began with the basmalah first for seeking blessing, then said: 'Praise be to Allah, the Exalted in His status' (His command and state in His essence, attributes, and actions)... His statement: 'So he began with the basmalah first for seeking blessing.' If you object: The basmalah has no entry into the mentioned indication, because the basmalah is customary at the beginning of every book of every discipline, so no indication of the forthcoming purposes is obtained, and thus the fa' is not appropriate. I say: The inclusion of the indication of the purposes of the science of kalam in the preface of his book is only considered praiseworthy and regarded as an excellence at the beginning of the book after observing seeking blessing with 'In the name of Allah.' So it is as if he said: He intended the mentioned inclusion, so he began with the basmalah first for seeking blessing, in order that that inclusion be considered. So the fa' is then in its proper place, since it can come for mere arrangement, as Ibn Hisham mentioned in Mughni al-Labib, and it has many examples in the Noble Quran. Moreover, it is apparent that the basmalah is temporally later than the inclusion intended by its cause, namely the intention. And it might be imagined that he meant by the mentioned inclusion to bring it within the preface, i.e., during it; then the basmalah has an entry in that at that point, for if he had not begun with the basmalah first, the indication would be at the beginning of the preface, not during it. And the prefacing of the hamdalah segment is not sufficient, because his statement 'the Exalted in His status,' etc., whether considered a substitute for the word 'Allah' or an attribute of it, is part of its completions. And it is not hidden what strain there is in this. Yes, it can be said: On the assumption that the basmalah is part of the preface, the word 'inclusion' suggests that the preface contains something else besides the mentioned indication. So the basmalah, for the purpose of seeking blessing, has an entry in the inclusion, even if it has no entry in the art of elegant opening. By this, the good position of the fa' becomes clear, even if it is interpreted as mere arrangement, and also relative to the inclusion itself, because the rank of detail is posterior to the rank of summary.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the use of the letter fa' (ف) in the commentator's statement 'fabsamala' (so he began with the basmalah). The objection claims that the basmalah is a generic opening used in all books, so it cannot specifically indicate the purposes of kalam, and therefore the fa' (which often indicates a result or sequence) is misplaced. The answer defends the fa' on several grounds: first, the basmalah is present for tayammun (seeking blessing), and it is only after that tayammun that the inclusion of the indication is considered effective; thus the fa' signifies simple temporal succession. The commentator cites Ibn Hisham's authority that fa' can denote mere arrangement. He further argues that the basmalah is temporally later than the intention of inclusion, so the fa' is appropriate. He also considers an alternative interpretation where the basmalah is part of the preface, and then the indication occurs within the preface, not at its very beginning; this also justifies the fa' by making the basmalah's role in the inclusion more direct. The conclusion emphasizes that even if the basmalah has no role in brāʿat al-istihlāl, it still has a role in the inclusion, and the fa' remains correct as indicating mere arrangement, with the detail being posterior to the summary.

Key Terms
براعة الاستهلال
بسمل
تيمناً
الفاء
تضمن
ترتيب مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The letter fa' always implies causation or immediate consequence.
Correction: The commentator shows, citing Ibn Hisham, that fa' can denote mere temporal or logical sequence without causation.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the fa' in 'fabsamala' is correctly placed?
A) Because the basmalah is the cause of the indication.
B) Because the basmalah is temporally later than the intention of inclusion, and fa' can indicate mere arrangement.
C) Because the basmalah is part of the hamdalah.
D) Because the basmalah is not related to brāʿat al-istihlāl.

Key Takeaway
The fa' in 'fabsamala' is justified because the basmalah seeks blessing before the inclusion of indication, and fa' can indicate mere arrangement, not causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly seek Allah's blessing before your daily tasks, or do you rush into them without consciously invoking His name? What would change if you deliberately paused to say 'Bismillah' before each major action today?
?
The objection in this passage doubts the basmalah's relevance to the content. Have you ever treated the basmalah as a mere formality, reciting it without reflecting on its meaning and its connection to the task at hand? Commit to reciting it with awareness the next time you start something.
?
The commentator defends the fa' by showing it indicates mere order, not causation. How often do you assume a causal link between your actions and their outcomes, forgetting that Allah is the true cause? Reflect on an area where you attribute success to your own effort without recognizing Allah's role.

The Use of 'Thumma' Between Basmalah and Hamdalah
Commentator defends the use of 'thumma' via grammatical rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a subtle grammatical rule about the conjunction of singular nouns resolves an apparent contradiction regarding the use of 'thumma' in the author's arrangement.

Core Idea
'Thumma' indicates delay only when conjoining singulars, not full sentences.

When the author wrote 'thumma qāla al-ḥamdu lillāh' after 'basmala', it seems odd because there is no delay. The commentator anticipates this objection and resolves it by referencing a nuanced grammatical principle. We'll follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection points out that 'thumma' implies delay, but there is no delay between basmalah and hamdalah. Read the passage and identify the problem.
2
Find the grammatical key
The commentator's answer relies on a distinction: 'thumma' only obligatorily indicates delay when conjoining singular nouns. Notice how he references verified grammarians.
3
Apply the rule to the text
Now consider that 'basmala' and 'qāla' are singular verb forms, so the rule applies. Why does that justify 'thumma'? The delay is a grammatical manner, not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ثم قال : الحمد لله إن قلت : ثم للترتيب مع التراخي ولا تراخي للحمدلة عن البسملة لا زماناً ولا رتبةً كما هو الظاهر، فما وجه ثم ؟ قلت : بعد تسليم عطف مدخول ثم على بسمل قد ذكرنا في حواشي المطول أن المحققين من النحاة نصوا على أن دلالة ثم على التراخي وجوبا مخصوصة بعطف المفرد.
His statement: 'Then he said: Praise be to Allah.' If you object: 'Thumma' indicates arrangement with delay, and there is no delay of the hamdalah from the basmalah, neither in time nor in rank, as is apparent. So what is the justification for 'thumma'? I say: After conceding that the word governed by 'thumma' is conjoined to 'basmala' [as a single word], we have mentioned in the glosses on al-Mutawwal that the verified grammarians have stated that the indication of 'thumma' for delay is necessarily specific to the conjunction of singular nouns.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the use of the word 'thumma' (then) in the phrase 'thumma qāla al-ḥamdu lillāh' (then he said: Praise be to Allah). The objection points out that 'thumma' implies a delay or lapse between two actions, but the basmalah and hamdalah are uttered in immediate succession with no temporal or rank delay, so how can 'thumma' be used? The answer relies on a grammatical rule: 'thumma' can indicate delay necessarily only when conjoining a singular noun to another singular noun. In this case, the word 'thumma' is conjoining the single word 'basmala' (as a verbal noun) to the single word 'qāla'? Actually, the construction is 'fabsamala ... thumma qāla' where the verbs are conjugated, but the grammarians consider the verbal nouns as singulars. The commentator states that verified grammarians distinguish between 'thumma' conjoining singulars (where delay is obligatory in meaning) and conjoining sentences (where delay is not necessarily required). Here, since 'basmala' and 'qāla' are both singular verb forms, the delay indicated by 'thumma' is taken as a manner of speech, not a temporal delay. Thus the use of 'thumma' is appropriate because the conjoined items are singular words, not full sentences implying temporal sequence.

الترتيب مع التراخي
عطف المفرد
بسمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'thumma' always implies a temporal delay between two events.
Correction: In conjunction of singular nouns, the delay is a grammatical feature, not necessarily temporal.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator justify the use of 'thumma' between the basmalah and hamdalah?
A) By claiming there is actually a temporal delay.
B) By arguing that 'thumma' indicates rank delay, not temporal.
C) By citing that 'thumma' indicates delay only when conjoining singular nouns, not sentences.
D) By saying the basmalah is not a complete sentence.

Key Takeaway
The use of 'thumma' in 'thumma qāla' is justified because when conjoining singular nouns, 'thumma' indicates a grammatical delay, not necessarily a temporal one.

The Basmala and the Letter Fa in the Introduction
The commentator explains the role of the basmala and defends the placement of 'fa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the placement of the letter 'fa' in the phrase 'فبسمل أولاً تيمناً' and the role of the basmala as a blessing rather than a content indicator.

Core Idea
The basmala is for blessing, not content, so the 'fa' is justified by mere order.

This passage addresses a subtle objection about the opening of the book. The author begins with the basmala, preceded by a fa (meaning 'so' or 'then'). An objector questions the connection. The commentator provides a multi-layered defense, showing that the basmala serves as a blessing, not a content indicator, and that the fa can indicate simple sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection and the commentator's response. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
Notice the structure: objection (فإن قلت), answer (قلت).
2
Understand the main defense
The commentator argues that the basmala is for blessing (tayammun), not for indicating the content of the book. This answers the objection that the basmala has no role in indicating the purposes of kalam.
The fa is justified by mere order (tartib), as shown by Quranic examples.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator also considers that 'inclusion' (tadmin) might refer to the introduction itself, in which case the basmala is part of that inclusion. This further supports the placement of the fa.
This shows the depth of the defense, considering different readings of the author's intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فبسمل أولاً تيمناً) فإن قلت: ليس للبسملة مدخل في الإشارة المذكورة، لأن البسملة مما يطرد في أول كل كتاب من كل فن فلا تحصل بها الإشارة إلى المقاصد الآتية، فلا للفاء. قلت: تضمن خطبة كتابه الإشارة إلى مقاصد علم الكلام إنما يستحسن ويعتد به، ويعد تفوقاً في ابتداء الكتاب بعد رعاية التيمن بسم الله فكأنه قال: أراد التضمين المذكور، فبسمل أولاً تيمناً ليعتد بذلك التضمن فالفاء حينئذ أصاب موقعه على أنها قد تجيء لمجرد الترتيب كما ذكره ابن هشام في مغني اللبيب وله أمثلة كثيرة في القرآن المجيد، والظاهر أن البسملة متأخرة زماناً عن التضمين الذي أريد به سببه أعني الإرادة، وقد يتوهم أنه أراد بالتضمين المذكور الإيراد في ضمن الخطبة أي أثنائها فللبسملة مدخل في ذلك حينئذ إذ لو لم يبسمل أولاً لكانت الإشارة في أول الخطبة لا في أثنائها، وتقدم جملة الحمدلة لا يكفي لأن قوله العلي شأنه الخ سواء اعتبر بدلاً عن لفظة الله أو نعتاً له من متمماتها، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف، نعم يمكن أن يقال: على تقدير كون البسملة جزءاً من الخطبة لفظ التضمين يشعر باشتمال الخطبة على شيء آخر سوى الإشارة المذكورة، فللبسملة على قصد التيمن مدخل في التضمين وإن لم يكن لها مدخل في براعة الاستهلال وبهذا يظهر حسن موقع الفاء إذا حملت على مجرد الترتيب أيضاً، ولو بالنسبة إلى نفس التضمين لأن مرتبة التفصيل متأخرة عن مرتبة الإجمال.
His saying: 'He began with the basmala for blessing.' For you say: The basmala has no entry into the mentioned indication, because the basmala is something that is repeated at the beginning of every book of every discipline, so the indication to the coming purposes is not obtained by it, so there is no [function] for the fa. I say: The inclusion of his book's introduction of the indication to the purposes of the science of kalam is only deemed good and considered, and is counted as an excellence in the beginning of the book after observing blessing by 'In the name of God'. So it is as if he said: He intended the mentioned inclusion, so he began with the basmala for blessing so that that inclusion would be considered. So the fa then has its proper place, in that it can come for mere ordering, as Ibn Hisham mentioned in Mughni al-Labib, and it has many examples in the Noble Qur'an. And the apparent is that the basmala is later in time than the inclusion whose intended cause is the will. And it may be thought that he intended by the mentioned inclusion the presentation within the introduction, i.e., during it. So the basmala then has entry into that, because if he had not begun with the basmala first, the indication would have been at the beginning of the introduction, not during it. And the precedence of the sentence of praise (al-hamdalah) is not sufficient, because his saying 'the Exalted in station, etc.' whether it is considered a substitute for the word 'Allah' or an attribute of it, is among its completions. And it is not hidden what is in it of strain. Yes, it can be said: on the assumption that the basmala is part of the introduction, the word 'inclusion' indicates that the introduction contains something else besides the mentioned indication. So the basmala with the intention of blessing has entry into the inclusion even if it has no entry into the excellence of the opening (bara'at al-istihlal). And by this appears the good position of the fa if it is taken for mere ordering also, even with respect to the inclusion itself, because the level of detail is after the level of summary.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the function of the letter 'fa' (ف) in the author's phrase 'فبسمل أولاً'. The objector argues that the basmala is a standard opening and cannot indicate the specific purposes of kalam, so the 'fa' (which often indicates a cause or sequence) is unmotivated. The commentator answers that the author's inclusion of kalam's purposes in the introduction is already praiseworthy; the basmala is added for blessing. The 'fa' then indicates mere temporal order (tartīb), as in many Quranic examples, not causality. He further explains that the basmala is temporally later than the intention to include those purposes. Even if one takes 'inclusion' to mean 'in the midst of the introduction', the basmala still plays a role because without it the indication would be at the very start, not within. The commentator concludes that the 'fa' is well-placed whether for order or inclusion, because the detailed mention follows the summary.

Key Terms
البسملة
التيمن
التضمين
الفاء
براعة الاستهلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The basmala itself indicates the content of the book.
Correction: The basmala is a generic blessing, not a content indicator. Its function is devotional, not informative.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say is the primary function of the basmala in this context?
A) To indicate the topics of the book
B) To seek blessing (tayammun)
C) To replace the praise (hamdalah)
D) To show causality with the following content

Key Takeaway
The basmala serves a devotional purpose, not a content-signaling one, and the 'fa' is justified by temporal order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Use of 'Thumma' in 'Then He Said: Praise Be to God'
The commentator defends the use of 'thumma' despite no temporal delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'thumma' can be used without temporal delay in Arabic grammatical constructions, based on the distinction between conjunction of individual words and sentences.

Core Idea
'Thumma' does not always imply delay; its meaning depends on the grammatical context.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle grammatical point about the word 'thumma' (then) used in the phrase 'ثم قال الحمد لله'. An objector argues that 'thumma' implies delay, but the hamdalah follows the basmala without any delay. The commentator responds with a distinction from Arabic grammar that resolves the objection. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its significance for understanding the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objector's point: why is 'thumma' problematic in this context? What does 'thumma' normally imply?
The objector says 'thumma' implies delay (tarākhī), but there is no delay here.
3
Understand the answer
The commentator cites the verifying grammarians. What distinction do they make? How does it resolve the problem?
The key is 'عطف المفرد' vs. 'عطف الجمل'.
4
Apply the rule to the text
Explain why 'thumma' in 'ثم قال الحمد لله' is correct despite no delay, using the rule from the verifying grammarians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ثم قال: الحمد لله) إن قلت: ثم للترتيب مع التراخي ولا تراخي للحمدلة عن البسملة لا زماناً ولا رتبةً كما هو الظاهر، فما وجه ثم؟ قلت: بعد تسليم عطف مدخول ثم على بسمل قد ذكرنا في حواشي المطول أن المحققين من النحاة نصوا على أن دلالة ثم على التراخي وجوباً مخصوصة بعطف المفرد.
His saying: 'Then he said: Praise be to God.' For you say: 'Thumma' is for ordering with delay, and there is no delay of the praise from the basmala, neither in time nor in rank, as is apparent. So what is the aspect of 'thumma'? I say: After conceding the conjunction of what is governed by 'thumma' upon 'basmala', we have mentioned in the marginalia of al-Mutawwal that the verifying grammarians state that the indication of 'thumma' for delay necessarily is specific to the conjunction of individual words.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the word 'thumma' (then) in the author's phrase 'ثم قال الحمد لله'. The objector argues that 'thumma' implies a delay, but the hamdalah (praise) follows the basmala without any time or rank delay. The commentator answers that, after accepting that the object of 'thumma' is conjoined to 'basmala', the grammarians of verification (muhaqqiqun) have ruled that 'thumma's obligatory indication of delay is only when conjoining a single word (mufrad), not a whole sentence. Since here it is a sentence, 'thumma' can indicate mere sequence without delay. This is a technical grammatical point from the science of Arabic syntax.

Key Terms
ثم
التراخي
عطف المفرد
المحققون من النحاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'thumma' always implies delay
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is context-dependent in Arabic grammar: 'thumma' implies delay only when conjoining individual words, not sentences.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator accept the use of 'thumma' despite no apparent delay?
A) Because the hamdalah is actually delayed in rank
B) Because 'thumma' can indicate mere ordering when conjoining sentences
C) Because the basmala is not part of the introduction
D) Because delay is not required for 'thumma' according to some grammarians

Key Takeaway
The word 'thumma' does not always indicate delay; its meaning depends on whether it conjoins individual words or whole sentences.

The Temporal Meaning of 'Thumma' and the Objection
Discussion of 'thumma' and resolution of an objection about temporal delay.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the particle 'thumma' is interpreted in its original temporal sense and how the objection about the delay of the command to reflect is resolved.

Core Idea
The temporal delay of 'thumma' applies only to qualification, not actual attainment.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection regarding the use of the word 'thumma' (then) in describing the stages of intellect. Critics argue that if 'thumma' implies a sequence over time, then the command to reflect would be delayed unreasonably. The author offers two replies, clarifying that the delay is limited and poses no theological problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part: the objector claims that using 'thumma' in its original temporal sense would delay the command to reflect until after all four stages are attained, which is not the case.
2
Examine the first reply
The author replies that the delay is only until the first two stages and the qualification for the last two, not until their actual attainment.
This is a nuanced distinction.
3
Consider the second reply
The author adds that even if the delay were full, there is no harm, because according to Ahl al-Sunna, a sane child is not held accountable until maturity.
This shows flexibility in interpretation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Note how this response protects the integrity of the text while maintaining rational consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وكلمة ثم على معناها الأصلي قيل عليه: يلزم من ذلك تأخر الأمر بالتفكر عن حصول المراتب الأربع، وليس كذلك ورد بأن اللازم تأخره عن حصول المرتبتين الأوليين، وعن التأهيل للمرتبتين الأخريين لا عن حصولهما بالفعل ولا محذور فيه وقد يجاب بأن لا محذور في الأول أيضاً على تقدير تسليم اللزوم، إذ المذهب الحق عند أهل السنة أن الصبي العاقل ليس
His statement: 'And the word thumma is upon its original meaning'. It was objected to him: That entails that the command to reflect is delayed until after the attainment of the four stages, but it is not so. The reply is that what is necessary is that it is delayed until after the attainment of the first two stages and after the qualification for the last two stages, not until their actual attainment, and there is no harm in that. And it may be answered that there is no harm in the first [interpretation] also, assuming the entailment is accepted, for the true doctrine according to the Ahl al-Sunna is that the sane child is not...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage discusses the meaning of the word 'thumma' (then) in the author's text. The author states that 'thumma' is used in its original temporal sense. An objector argues that if 'thumma' implies a temporal delay, then the command to reflect (al-amr bi-l-tafakkur) would be delayed until after all four stages of intellect are attained, which is not the case because the command comes earlier. The author replies that the delay is only until the first two stages (material intellect and intellect by disposition) and the qualification for the last two stages, not until their actual attainment. There is no problem with that. A further answer suggests that even if the command were delayed until after all four stages, there would be no harm, because according to Ahl al-Sunna, a sane child is not held accountable until he reaches maturity. The text cuts off at 'the sane child is not...', but the sense is that the command is not applicable until certain conditions are met.

Key Terms
ثم
أمر بالتفكر
تأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'thumma' must indicate a long temporal gap.
Correction: 'Thumma' can indicate a sequence without significant delay; the author shows it indicates only a logical order.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is delayed by the word 'thumma'?
A) The command to reflect is delayed until all four stages are actually attained.
B) The command to reflect is delayed only until the first two stages and qualification for the last two.
C) The command to reflect is not delayed at all.
D) The command to reflect is delayed until the Hereafter.

Key Takeaway
The temporal sense of 'thumma' does not force an objectionable delay; the command to reflect is properly sequenced with the stages of intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Purpose of Tafakkur and Tadabbur
Commentator explains purpose of tafakkur and tadabbur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine command to reflect on creation and how this reflection leads to knowledge of the existence of a Maker.

Core Idea
Reflection on creation is the commanded path to knowing the Creator.

The commentator begins by explaining the meaning of 'then' in the Quranic context and clarifies that God commanded us to reflect on His creatures and contemplate His made things. This lesson covers the purpose of such reflection as a means to arrive at knowledge of a Maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator explains the command to reflect and its goal.
Pay attention to the words 'تفكر' and 'تدبر'.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'تفكر' (reflection) and 'تدبر' (contemplation) and understand their meanings in this context.
3
Connect reflection to knowledge
Understand how the commentator connects reflection on creatures and made things to knowledge of a Maker. This is the logical flow from creation to Creator.
This step establishes the groundwork for the proof in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مقدمة المؤلف وكلمة ثم على معناها الأصلي الذي هو المهلة، والمراد أنه تعالى أمرهم على ألسنة الرسل بالتفكر في مخلوقاته وأحوالها والتدبر لمصنوعاته وأطوارها وفي قوله : ( ليؤديهم) أي : التفكر والتدبر فيها مع ما في حيزه نوع تفصيل لما أجمله مباحث الإلهيات والاستدلال عليها بالممكنات في قوله العلي شأنه وما يعقبه (إلى العلم بوجود صانع، لأن المخلوقات حادثةٌ ولا بد للحادث من صانع ( قديم ) لا أول لوجوده إذ لو كان أيضاً حادثاً، لاحتاج إلى صانع آخر، فتسلسل أو دار (قيوم) قائم بنفسه مقيم لغيره، فإن ذلك لازم لكونه صانعاً حقيقياً
In the introduction of the author and the word then upon its original meaning which is delay, and the meaning is that He, exalted, commanded them through the tongues of the messengers to reflect on His creatures and their states, and to contemplate His made things and their phases, and in His saying: (to lead them) i.e., reflection and contemplation in them along with what is contained in it is a type of detail of what was summarized in the discussions of the divine matters and inferring them from contingent things in His saying, exalted is His status, and what follows it (to the knowledge of the existence of a Maker, because created things are originated, and the originated must have a Maker (Eternal) with no beginning to His existence, for if He were also originated, He would need another Maker, thus leading to infinite regress or circularity (Self-Subsisting) standing by Himself, establishing others, for that is necessary for His being a true Maker.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage explains the purpose of tafakkur (reflection) and tadabbur (contemplation). The commentator states that God commanded humanity through messengers to reflect on His creatures and their states, and to contemplate His made things, leading to knowledge of a Maker. The proof for the Maker's attributes follows: He must be Qadim (eternal) because if He were originated, He would need another Maker, leading to infinite regress or circular reasoning. He must also be Qayyum (self-subsisting) because He is the true Maker.

Key Terms
تفكر
تدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Proof of a Maker: Qadim and Qayyum
Commentator proves necessity of a Maker with attributes Qadim and Qayyum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the existence of a Maker and why He must be eternal (Qadim) and self-subsisting (Qayyum).

Core Idea
The created world points to an uncreated Maker who is eternal and self-subsisting.

Having established that reflection leads to knowledge of a Maker, the commentator now provides the rational proof: created things are originated, and every originated thing needs a maker. This maker must be eternal, otherwise infinite regress would occur. He also must be self-subsisting, standing by Himself.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'لأن المخلوقات حادثةٌ' through 'مقيم لغيره' along with its translation. Note the logical structure.
2
Understand the argument
Identify the key premise: created things are originated. Then, apply the principle that every originated thing requires a maker. Consider what happens if that maker were also originated.
The argument uses reductio ad absurdum (infinite regress or circularity).
3
Derive the attributes
Understand why the Maker must be Qadim (eternal) and Qayyum (self-subsisting). Qadim avoids regress, Qayyum is necessary for being a true Maker.
Remember that Qadim here means 'eternal without beginning', not just 'old'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مقدمة المؤلف وكلمة ثم على معناها الأصلي الذي هو المهلة، والمراد أنه تعالى أمرهم على ألسنة الرسل بالتفكر في مخلوقاته وأحوالها والتدبر لمصنوعاته وأطوارها وفي قوله : ( ليؤديهم) أي : التفكر والتدبر فيها مع ما في حيزه نوع تفصيل لما أجمله مباحث الإلهيات والاستدلال عليها بالممكنات في قوله العلي شأنه وما يعقبه (إلى العلم بوجود صانع، لأن المخلوقات حادثةٌ ولا بد للحادث من صانع ( قديم ) لا أول لوجوده إذ لو كان أيضاً حادثاً، لاحتاج إلى صانع آخر، فتسلسل أو دار (قيوم) قائم بنفسه مقيم لغيره، فإن ذلك لازم لكونه صانعاً حقيقياً
In the introduction of the author and the word then upon its original meaning which is delay, and the meaning is that He, exalted, commanded them through the tongues of the messengers to reflect on His creatures and their states, and to contemplate His made things and their phases, and in His saying: (to lead them) i.e., reflection and contemplation in them along with what is contained in it is a type of detail of what was summarized in the discussions of the divine matters and inferring them from contingent things in His saying, exalted is His status, and what follows it (to the knowledge of the existence of a Maker, because created things are originated, and the originated must have a Maker (Eternal) with no beginning to His existence, for if He were also originated, He would need another Maker, thus leading to infinite regress or circularity (Self-Subsisting) standing by Himself, establishing others, for that is necessary for His being a true Maker.

Commentary
This passage explains the purpose of tafakkur (reflection) and tadabbur (contemplation). The commentator states that God commanded humanity through messengers to reflect on His creatures and their states, and to contemplate His made things, leading to knowledge of a Maker. The proof for the Maker's attributes follows: He must be Qadim (eternal) because if He were originated, He would need another Maker, leading to infinite regress or circular reasoning. He must also be Qayyum (self-subsisting) because He is the true Maker.

Objection on Command to Adam and Its Resolution
Objection about the command applying to Adam and its resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the command addressed to the children of Adam through the messengers is properly applied to Adam himself.

Core Idea
The command applies to the human species as a whole, not each individual.

In this passage, a potential objection is raised about the earlier interpretation of the command to the children of Adam. The questioner points out that Adam himself could not have been commanded through later messengers, since he was the first prophet. The commentator resolves this by clarifying the collective nature of the command. Let's see how this logical objection is handled.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the problem: the commentator said God commanded the children of Adam through the messengers, but Adam was not addressed by later messengers.
Notice the questioner's phrase 'فكيف يستقيم ما ذكره؟' (how can what he mentioned be correct?).
2
Focus on the answer
Identify the key phrase: 'المراد أنه تعالى أمر النوع على السنة الرسل لا كل فرد فرد' – the command is to the species, not every individual.
3
Apply the principle
Consider how this resolution also applies to other prophets, as the commentator mentions.
This shows the consistency of the interpretation across all prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الشارح قد فسر بني آدم بنوع الإنسان، وآدم منهم، وليس مأموراً على السنة الرسل إذ الظاهر أن المراد رسل البشر، فكيف يستقيم ما ذكره؟ قلت : المراد أنه تعالى أمر النوع على السنة الرسلُ لا كل فرد فرد، وإلا لم يستقم في بعض من سواه من الأنبياء أيضاً .

I said: The meaning is that He, exalted, commanded the species through the tongues of the messengers, not every individual, otherwise it would not be correct regarding some other prophets as well.)
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The questioner raises a problem: the commentator earlier said God commanded the children of Adam through the messengers, but Adam himself was not commanded by later messengers because he is the first prophet. The answer clarifies that the command is directed to the human species (النوع) as a whole, not to each individual. Thus, Adam is included as part of the species, even if he did not receive the command from a later messenger.

Addressing Circularity in the Proof from Quran 15:9
Objection-and-answer regarding circularity in proof from Quran 15:9.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves the potential circularity in using Quran 15:9 to prove the Quran's preservation.

Core Idea
The verse's own preservation is proven by tawatur, not by the verse itself.

This passage tackles a methodological concern: if we use a Quranic verse to prove that the Quran is preserved, doesn't that assume the verse itself is preserved? The author addresses this objection by distinguishing between the evidence for the verse and the evidence for the rest of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and the response.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the author mentions the potential circularity (شائبة مضادرة). The objection is that using the verse to prove preservation requires the verse itself to be free from alteration.
3
Analyze the response
The author explains that the verse's freedom from alteration is established by tawatur (mass transmission) from the Prophet, who is confirmed by rational proof via miracles. This avoids circularity.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument is valid because the verse's authenticity is independently verified. The author also notes that one does not need to provide separate evidence for the preservation of this verse itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لقوله تعالى : وإنا له لحافظون صحة الاستدلال بهذه الآية على ما ذكر موقوفة على أن لا تحريف فيها نفسها، ففيه شائبة مضادرة ويمكن أن يقال : انعدام التحريف في هذه الآية نفسها ثبت بتواتر نقلها عن النبي عليه السلام الصادق المصدق بالتصديق العقلي الذي هو إظهار المعجزة على يده، والاستدلال بمنطوقها على انعدام التحريف فيما سواه، ولا يقدح فيه جواز الاستدلال عليه بالتواتر أيضاً، وهو ظاهر، نعم يلزم عدم التعرض لدليل عدم التحريف في هذه الآية نفسها .
His saying: 'For His saying, "Indeed, We will preserve it." The validity of using this verse as evidence for what was mentioned depends on there being no alteration in it itself, so there is a hint of circularity. And it can be said: The absence of alteration in this verse itself is established by the tawatur (mass transmission) of its transmission from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who is truthful and confirmed by rational confirmation, which is the manifestation of the miracle at his hand. And using its wording as evidence for the absence of alteration in other than it, and the possibility of also using tawatur as evidence does not harm it. That is apparent. Yes, it is necessary not to address the evidence for the absence of alteration in this verse itself.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the proof from Quran 15:9. The objection is that using this verse to prove the Quran's preservation might be circular because the verse itself would need to be preserved to be valid evidence. The author responds by saying that the absence of alteration in this specific verse is established through tawatur (mass transmission) from the Prophet, who is confirmed by rational proof through miracles. Thus, the verse is reliably transmitted without alteration. Using its text to prove preservation of the rest of the Quran is valid, and the fact that one could also use tawatur for the rest does not invalidate this argument. The author notes that it is not necessary to provide separate evidence for the preservation of this verse itself.

Key Terms

المصادرة
التواتر
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that using a Quranic verse to prove the Quran's preservation is circular because the verse itself would need to be preserved to be valid evidence.
Correction: The author clarifies that the verse's own preservation is independently established by tawatur from the Prophet, confirmed by miracles, so the argument is not circular.

Checkpoint

D) He uses a different verse.

Key Takeaway

Definition of First and Second Perfection
Defines the two perfections and their subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between first perfection (the specific differentia) and second perfection (attributes and actions), and the two divisions of second perfection.

Core Idea
Perfection has two aspects: what makes a thing a type (first), and its attributes and actions (second).

Sanusi begins by defining the two types of perfection that every entity possesses. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on divine attributes and causality. The passage lays the groundwork by distinguishing the type's essential differentia from its inherent attributes and outward effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of perfection
Start with the sentence defining perfection as the possession of attributes and issuance of effects after the type is realized.
Notice that 'first perfection' is the differentia, and 'second perfection' is what follows.
2
Identify the two divisions of second perfection
Look for the phrase 'قسمان' and note the two categories: attributes inherent in the essence (like knowledge) and actions proceeding from it (like writing).
The examples clarify each division.
3
Reflect on the relationship between first and second perfection
Ask yourself how the first perfection (the type's differentia) relates to the second perfection (attributes and actions).
The first is what makes a thing that type; the second is what flows from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كمال كل نوع يعني أن كماله بعد تحصله وتكمله نوعاً بمنوعه المسمى كمالاً أول على الإطلاق إنما هو ( بحصول صفاته الخاصة به، وصدور آثاره المقصودة منه ) ويسمى هذا الكمال كمالاً ثانياً وأشار إلى أنه قسمان: أحدهما: صفات تخصه
قوله: (ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً) قيل : حمل الكمال المذكور على الكمال الثاني، ليحتاج إلى تقييد النوع المذكور بقوله : بعد تحصيله وتكمله إلخ لا يحتاج إليه، بل لا طائل تحته لأن تمثيل الكمال المقصود بالنسبة إلى الإنسان بالقوة النطقية وما يتبعها من العقل مناد بأعلى صوته، على أن المراد بالكمال مطلق الأمور المختصة سواء كان أولاً أو ثانياً، وكأنه فهم ذلك من لفظ الصفات فإنها تكون خارجةً، وقد غفل عن اصطلاحهم أن الذاتيات تسمى الصفات النفسية، وأقول : أصل هذا مأخوذ من كلام الأبهري حيث حمل قوله بحصول صفاتها على الكمالات الأولى المنوعة وقوله بصدور آثارها على الكمالات الثانية، ووجه إطلاق الصفة على الذاتيات بأن أجزاء الماهيات الحقيقية يتعذر الاطلاع عليها، أو يتعسر فأخذوا الأثر القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار المختصة بالنوع، وسموه فصلاً والأثر العام القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار العامة له، جنساً تسهيلاً للطالب، وصح تسمية الذاتي بهذا الاعتبار صفة له. قال : وهذا هو السبب في إطلاق المتكلمين الصفة على الذاتيات وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء المحمولة المسماة بالذاتيات وأما الأجزاء الخارجية فلا يطلق عليها الصفات النفسية، ولا يتعسر الاطلاع عليها، نعم قد يحمل الصورة المنوعة في بعض الأنواع كالإنسان على ما عرف، حتى قيل : إن النفس الناطقة ليست صورة منوعة له لأنها مجردةٌ فكيف تكون صورة منوعة
قائمة به به غير صادرة عنه، كالعلم للإنسان مثلاً . والثاني آثار صادرة عنه مقصودة منه ،بخصوصه، فتختص به أيضاً كالكتابة الصادرة عنه وكالمضاء للسيف
For the perfection of every type means that its perfection after its realization and its becoming a type through its specific differentia, called the first perfection absolutely, is only through the possession of its particular attributes and the issuance of its intended effects. And this perfection is called the second perfection. And he indicated that it is of two divisions: one of them: attributes specific to it. He said: 'and it is called the second perfection.' It was said: interpreting the mentioned perfection as the second perfection makes it necessary to qualify the mentioned type by saying 'after its realization and its becoming a type' etc., but it is not necessary; rather there is no benefit in it because the example of the intended perfection with respect to man as the rational power and what follows from it of intellect proclaims loudly that the intended perfection is absolute specific matters, whether first or second. And it is as if he understood that from the term 'attributes' because they are external, but he neglected their convention that the essential properties are called psychological attributes. I say: the origin of this is taken from the speech of al-Abhari where he interpreted his saying 'by the possession of its attributes' as the first perfections that differentiate, and his saying 'the issuance of its effects' as the second perfections. And the justification for applying the term 'attribute' to essential properties is that the parts of true essences are difficult or impossible to perceive, so they took the proximate effect that entails all other effects specific to the type, and called it a differentia, and the proximate common effect that entails all other common effects for it, called it a genus, to facilitate the student. Thus the application of the term 'attribute' to the essential is justified by this consideration. He said: This is the reason the theologians apply the term 'attribute' to essential properties. And you are aware that what he mentioned is only valid for the predicable parts called essential properties; as for the external parts, the term 'psychological attributes' is not applied to them, nor is it difficult to perceive them. Indeed, the differentiating form in some types, like man, is understood in a known way, such that it was said: the rational soul is not a differentiating form for him because it is immaterial; so how can it be a differentiating form? It is inherent in him, not proceeding from him, like knowledge for man, for example. And the second: effects proceeding from him, intended from him specifically, so they are also specific to him, like writing proceeding from him and like the sharpness of the sword.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of perfection (al-kamāl). It begins by stating that the perfection of any type consists of two elements: the first perfection (al-kamāl al-awwal) is the type's specific differentia (manūʿ), and the second perfection (al-kamāl al-thānī) is the possession of particular attributes and the issuance of intended effects. The second perfection is divided into two kinds: (1) attributes that inhere in the essence without proceeding from it, such as knowledge for a human, and (2) actions that proceed from the essence and are intended specifically, such as writing for a human or sharpness for a sword. The author then engages in a critical discussion about the terminology: an objection is raised that interpreting 'perfection' as the second perfection is unnecessary and lacks benefit, because the example of human perfection (rational power) is a first perfection, not a second. The author responds by explaining that al-Abhari interpreted the phrase 'possession of attributes' as first perfections and 'issuance of effects' as second perfections, and he justifies applying the term 'attribute' to essential properties by noting that the parts of quiddities are difficult to grasp, so the proximate effect is taken as a differentia or genus. The author then notes a limitation: this applies only to predicable essential parts, not external parts. The passage concludes by restating the two divisions with examples.

Key Terms
كمال أول
كمال ثانٍ
صفات
آثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many divisions does the second perfection have according to the passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Second perfection is of two types: inherent attributes and outward actions, both specific to the type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your human perfection consists of a first perfection (your rational soul) and a second perfection (your attributes and actions). Ask yourself: Which aspect of your second perfection—your internal attributes (like knowledge, sincerity) or your outward actions (like teaching, writing)—do you tend to neglect? How would your life change if you intentionally cultivated the neglected aspect starting today?
?
The lesson uses the example of a sword: its first perfection is sharpness, and its second perfection includes cutting (an action). In your own life, what is the 'sharpness' that defines your role? Are you producing the 'cutting' actions that should naturally flow from that defining characteristic? Be specific about one area where your actions are not matching your identity.
?
Consider the attribute of knowledge: it is part of your second perfection as a human. Are you content with simply having knowledge stored in your mind, or are you actively using it to produce actions that benefit others? What is one action you can take today to manifest your knowledge, and why have you not done it yet?

Defending the Terminology: The Objection and Response
Presents an objection and the author's response on terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that interpreting 'perfection' as second perfection is unnecessary, and the author's response that clarifies how first and second perfections are related and why essential properties are called attributes.

Core Idea
Even essential properties are called attributes because they are understood through their effects.

An interlocutor raises a subtle objection: why specify 'second perfection' after 'first perfection'? The author responds by explaining the distinction between first perfections (differentiae) and second perfections (attributes and actions), and justifies applying the term 'attribute' to essential properties by noting the difficulty of grasping quiddities directly. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that interpreting 'perfection' as second perfection is unnecessary because the example of human perfection (rational power) is actually a first perfection.
The objector claims the phrase 'after its realization' is redundant.
2
Follow the author's response
The author invokes al-Abhari's interpretation: 'possession of attributes' refers to first perfections, 'issuance of effects' to second perfections. Then he explains why essential properties are called 'attributes'.
The justification is that the parts of quiddities are difficult to know, so they are known through their proximate effects.
3
Note the limitation mentioned
The author notes that this applies only to predicable essential parts, not external parts, giving the example of the rational soul not being a differentia for man.
This shows the nuance in applying the term 'attribute' to different types of parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كمال كل نوع يعني أن كماله بعد تحصله وتكمله نوعاً بمنوعه المسمى كمالاً أول على الإطلاق إنما هو ( بحصول صفاته الخاصة به، وصدور آثاره المقصودة منه ) ويسمى هذا الكمال كمالاً ثانياً وأشار إلى أنه قسمان: أحدهما: صفات تخصه
قوله: (ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً) قيل : حمل الكمال المذكور على الكمال الثاني، ليحتاج إلى تقييد النوع المذكور بقوله : بعد تحصيله وتكمله إلخ لا يحتاج إليه، بل لا طائل تحته لأن تمثيل الكمال المقصود بالنسبة إلى الإنسان بالقوة النطقية وما يتبعها من العقل مناد بأعلى صوته، على أن المراد بالكمال مطلق الأمور المختصة سواء كان أولاً أو ثانياً، وكأنه فهم ذلك من لفظ الصفات فإنها تكون خارجةً، وقد غفل عن اصطلاحهم أن الذاتيات تسمى الصفات النفسية، وأقول : أصل هذا مأخوذ من كلام الأبهري حيث حمل قوله بحصول صفاتها على الكمالات الأولى المنوعة وقوله بصدور آثارها على الكمالات الثانية، ووجه إطلاق الصفة على الذاتيات بأن أجزاء الماهيات الحقيقية يتعذر الاطلاع عليها، أو يتعسر فأخذوا الأثر القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار المختصة بالنوع، وسموه فصلاً والأثر العام القريب الذي يستتبع سائر الآثار العامة له، جنساً تسهيلاً للطالب، وصح تسمية الذاتي بهذا الاعتبار صفة له. قال : وهذا هو السبب في إطلاق المتكلمين الصفة على الذاتيات وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء المحمولة المسماة بالذاتيات وأما الأجزاء الخارجية فلا يطلق عليها الصفات النفسية، ولا يتعسر الاطلاع عليها، نعم قد يحمل الصورة المنوعة في بعض الأنواع كالإنسان على ما عرف، حتى قيل : إن النفس الناطقة ليست صورة منوعة له لأنها مجردةٌ فكيف تكون صورة منوعة
قائمة به به غير صادرة عنه، كالعلم للإنسان مثلاً . والثاني آثار صادرة عنه مقصودة منه ،بخصوصه، فتختص به أيضاً كالكتابة الصادرة عنه وكالمضاء للسيف
For the perfection of every type means that its perfection after its realization and its becoming a type through its specific differentia, called the first perfection absolutely, is only through the possession of its particular attributes and the issuance of its intended effects. And this perfection is called the second perfection. And he indicated that it is of two divisions: one of them: attributes specific to it. He said: 'and it is called the second perfection.' It was said: interpreting the mentioned perfection as the second perfection makes it necessary to qualify the mentioned type by saying 'after its realization and its becoming a type' etc., but it is not necessary; rather there is no benefit in it because the example of the intended perfection with respect to man as the rational power and what follows from it of intellect proclaims loudly that the intended perfection is absolute specific matters, whether first or second. And it is as if he understood that from the term 'attributes' because they are external, but he neglected their convention that the essential properties are called psychological attributes. I say: the origin of this is taken from the speech of al-Abhari where he interpreted his saying 'by the possession of its attributes' as the first perfections that differentiate, and his saying 'the issuance of its effects' as the second perfections. And the justification for applying the term 'attribute' to essential properties is that the parts of true essences are difficult or impossible to perceive, so they took the proximate effect that entails all other effects specific to the type, and called it a differentia, and the proximate common effect that entails all other common effects for it, called it a genus, to facilitate the student. Thus the application of the term 'attribute' to the essential is justified by this consideration. He said: This is the reason the theologians apply the term 'attribute' to essential properties. And you are aware that what he mentioned is only valid for the predicable parts called essential properties; as for the external parts, the term 'psychological attributes' is not applied to them, nor is it difficult to perceive them. Indeed, the differentiating form in some types, like man, is understood in a known way, such that it was said: the rational soul is not a differentiating form for him because it is immaterial; so how can it be a differentiating form? It is inherent in him, not proceeding from him, like knowledge for man, for example. And the second: effects proceeding from him, intended from him specifically, so they are also specific to him, like writing proceeding from him and like the sharpness of the sword.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of perfection (al-kamāl). It begins by stating that the perfection of any type consists of two elements: the first perfection (al-kamāl al-awwal) is the type's specific differentia (manūʿ), and the second perfection (al-kamāl al-thānī) is the possession of particular attributes and the issuance of intended effects. The second perfection is divided into two kinds: (1) attributes that inhere in the essence without proceeding from it, such as knowledge for a human, and (2) actions that proceed from the essence and are intended specifically, such as writing for a human or sharpness for a sword. The author then engages in a critical discussion about the terminology: an objection is raised that interpreting 'perfection' as the second perfection is unnecessary and lacks benefit, because the example of human perfection (rational power) is a first perfection, not a second. The author responds by explaining that al-Abhari interpreted the phrase 'possession of attributes' as first perfections and 'issuance of effects' as second perfections, and he justifies applying the term 'attribute' to essential properties by noting that the parts of quiddities are difficult to grasp, so the proximate effect is taken as a differentia or genus. The author then notes a limitation: this applies only to predicable essential parts, not external parts. The passage concludes by restating the two divisions with examples.

ذاتيات
الصفات النفسية
الأجزاء المحمولة
الأثر القريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'attribute' (sifa) only applies to accidental properties, not essential ones.
Correction: The author shows that theologians extend the term 'attribute' to essential properties by considering their proximate effects, though this is only valid for predicable logical parts.

Checkpoint
Why do theologians call essential properties 'attributes' according to the author?
A) Because they are external to the essence
B) Because the parts of quiddities are difficult to know, so they are known through their effects
C) Because they are identical to the essence
D) Because they are accidental

Key Takeaway
The term 'attribute' is applied to essential properties by way of their effects, but this is limited to logical essential parts, not external parts like the rational soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a technical theological distinction like 'first perfection' and 'second perfection', do you tend to dismiss it as nitpicking, or do you pause to understand how it protects a deeper truth? What does your reaction reveal about your own humility before scholars?
?
The author says essential properties are called 'attributes' because their true reality is difficult to perceive. In your life of faith, are there truths about Allah or His attributes that you accept without struggling to understand their deeper meaning? Are you content with surface-level knowledge, or do you seek the proximate effects that lead to deeper certainty?
?
The rational soul is not a differentiating form because it is immaterial. How does this affect your understanding of your own soul? Are you living as if your soul is merely a label, or as a transcendent reality that demands you to seek its perfection through knowledge and action?

Superiority and Human Rational Power
Al-Abhari establishes superiority within and between types and identifies human perfection as the rational power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Abhari explains superiority among individuals and types, and why the rational power is the unique perfection of humanity.

Core Idea
Humanity's true perfection is not shared with animals or plants but lies in the rational power.

The author begins by distinguishing between superiority among individuals of the same species and superiority among different types. He then shows that human beings share many attributes with other creatures—space-occupation with bodies, nutrition with plants, sensation with animals—but only the rational power sets humans apart as a unique type.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening verses
The author quotes poetry to illustrate that some individuals can be as excellent as a thousand others. Reflect on the idea of individual superiority within a type.
The line 'I have not seen the likes of men...' is a well-known Arabic verse.
2
Understand shared attributes
The author lists attributes humans share with bodies (space), plants (nutrition), and animals (life, movement, sensation). Notice that these are not what make humans human.
3
Identify the distinctive perfection
The passage concludes that the rational power (al-quwwa al-nuṭqiyya) is the perfection that distinguishes humans. This is the key takeaway.
This rational power is the basis for humanity's superiority over other creatures.

وبحسب زيادة ذلك المذكور، أعني الكمال الثاني ( ونقصانه بفضل بعض أفراده ) أي أفراد ذلك النوع ( بعضاً إلى أن يعد أحدهم بألف ) :
