yقال للهيولى خال عن الصور في نفسها . قوله: (أو للجسم النامي قد تقرر عند علماء البيان أن اللف إذا كان إجمالياً فالقاعدة
And according to the increase or decrease of that mentioned, I mean the second perfection, by virtue of some individuals of the type surpassing others, to the extent that one of them is counted as a thousand. And I have not seen the likes of men vary in glory until one is counted as a thousand. Rather one of them is a sky and the other is earth. People are earth upon earth, and you are above them a sky. As for the superiority of types among themselves, it is according to the superiority of their differentiae which entail their properties and their intended effects, as he indicated by his saying: 'And man shares with all bodies in occupying space and with plants in nutrition, growth, and increase, and with dumb animals in his life through breathing, voluntary movement, and sensation.' These matters shared between him and others are not a perfection for him insofar as he is a man, but rather they are perfections for the body absolutely, or for the growing body, or for the animal. Rather, man is distinguished from these matters that share with him in what was mentioned by what he was given of the rational power, which is his perfection, (origin of material? Rather he has an unknown bodily differentiating form, and due to its hiddenness they posited the abstract soul, which is the perfection of the human type apparently in its place, except that this is not valid absolutely in all types, so it does not suit this context because the discussion here is with regard to every type absolutely, considering that in what al-Abhari mentioned there is a necessary lack of attention to the first division of the second perfection, and nearer to it is the generalization of attributes, and also the first perfection that differentiates in reality, so it is not appropriate to apply attributes to it by the mentioned justification. Then his saying 'And according to its increase and decrease' prevents interpreting attributes as first perfections, since the first perfection does not vary among individuals of the type, and making reference to what is other than that is evident distortion. Also his saying 'his perfection is by reasoning intelligibles' indicates that the intended perfection mentioned first is the second perfection, as is not hidden. His saying: 'in space, in place' – he indicated by 'in place' that it means space, so it does not refute considering space a perfection for the body, realizing that the individual substance is not located in space although it occupies space due to the existence of extension in what is located, as they clarified. His saying: 'empty of what occupies space' means in itself, in the sense that what occupies space is not taken along with it, as it is said of hyle that it is empty of forms in themselves. His saying: 'or for the growing body' – it is established among rhetoricians that if the word is general, the rule is...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of superiority in perfection. It begins with the notion that individuals of the same type vary in the second perfection (e.g., one person may be as excellent as a thousand, or like heaven compared to earth). Then it discusses superiority between types, noting that human perfection is not found in shared attributes like occupying space, nutrition, or sensation, which are perfections for body or animal generally. The true human perfection is the rational power (al-quwwa al-nuṭqiyya). The author then critiques al-Abhari's position, arguing that the first perfection does not admit of variation, so 'increase and decrease' must refer to the second perfection. He then addresses a subtle point about 'space' (al-ḥayyiz) and 'place' (al-makān), explaining that place is what is meant by space, and that the individual substance (jawhar fard) is not located (mutamakkin) in space even though it occupies space (mutaḥayyiz). A gloss on 'empty of what occupies space' clarifies that the term 'empty' means not taking the occupant into account. The passage ends with a note on rhetoric about general terms.

Misconception: Human perfection includes attributes like occupying space, nutrition, and sensation.
Correction: These are perfections of the body or animal, not of humanity as such; humanity's unique perfection is the rational power.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The rational power is what distinguishes humanity and constitutes its true perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You share many attributes with animals—you eat, move, sense—but what if your rational power, the very thing that makes you human, is neglected? When you act without thought, are you not reducing yourself to mere animal? Pause right now: what is one decision you will make today using your rational power deliberately, instead of reacting instinctively?
?
The author says shared attributes are not your perfection as a human. Yet we often pride ourselves on our physical health, wealth, or social status—things animals also seek. Is your life oriented toward the rational power? In the last hour, did you do anything that truly exercised your mind in pursuit of truth? If not, resolve now to spend five minutes in reflection before sleep.
?
If the rational power is your true perfection, then ignorance and irrationality are your real failures. You may have memorized this lesson, but have you allowed it to change how you think? Name one belief you hold that you have never actually examined with your reason. Commit to examining it tonight.

Critique of al-Abhari and Clarifications on Perfections and Space
Al-Abhari critiques and clarifies the nature of first and second perfections and the concept of space and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of al-Abhari's classification of perfections and the clarification about space and place in the context of the individual substance.

Core Idea
The first perfection does not admit of increase or decrease; the second perfection does, and that is the type's distinguishing perfection.

After establishing the rational power as human perfection, the author critiques al-Abhari for conflating the first and second perfections. He argues that the first perfection cannot vary, so the increase and decrease must refer to the second perfection. He then clarifies a subtle point about space and place, noting that the individual substance occupies space but is not located in place in the usual sense.

Guided Path
1
Understand the critique
Read the author's argument that the first perfection does not vary among individuals, so the 'increase and decrease' must refer to the second perfection. Notice how he uses al-Abhari's own words against him.
The author quotes al-Abhari's saying 'and according to its increase and decrease' to support his point.
2
Follow the justification of attributes
The author discusses why attributes cannot be applied to the first perfection and suggests a generalization of attributes.
This part is complex; focus on the main claim.
3
Examine the space/place distinction
The author explains that 'place' (makān) is what is meant by 'space' (ḥayyiz), and that the individual substance (jawhar fard) occupies space but is not located in place due to the lack of extension in it.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical note
The passage ends with a note about general terms in rhetoric, though it is incomplete.
This part may be affected by OCR issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first perfection (the differential) can increase or decrease among individuals of a type.
Correction: The first perfection is the same for all individuals of a type; it is the second perfection that varies.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why cannot the attributes (like increase/decrease) be applied to the first perfection?
A) Because the first perfection is not a real perfection
B) Because the first perfection does not vary among individuals of the type
C) Because the first perfection is unknown
D) Because the first perfection belongs to the body, not the soul

Key Takeaway
The first perfection is invariable; the second perfection is variable and is the true perfection of the type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first perfection of a human is the rational soul, which is fixed and does not increase. Your second perfection—your knowledge, virtue, and actualization of your potential—varies. Right now, is your second perfection growing or declining? What will you do today to increase it?
?
Al-Abhari is criticized for conflating what you have (first perfection) with what you do (second perfection). In your life, do you confuse your intrinsic worth as a human (soul) with your achievements? How does that affect your self-perception and your efforts in worship and learning?
?
The passage argues that space is a perfection for the body even though the soul occupies space without being located. Your soul resides in your body without being limited by it. How does this truth change the way you view your body—as a mere container or as an instrument for realizing your second perfection? What are you using your body for right now?

The Intellect and the Divisions of Sciences
Explains the intellect's perfections and the division of sciences into transmitted and rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author classifies human perfections into intellectual and moral, and how the sciences branch into transmitted and rational.

Core Idea
Intellectual perfections are the highest, and the vastness of sciences leads to scholarly specialization.

This passage from al-Mawaqif examines the highest human perfection: the intellect's ability to acquire knowledge. It then explains why scholars have divided into different groups, each focusing on a branch of science. The lesson shows the link between intellectual capacity and the diversity of scholarly pursuits.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how the author moves from the intellect's preparedness to the classification of sciences.
2
Identify the two perfections
The passage distinguishes between intellectual perfections (like knowing primary intelligibles) and moral perfections (good deeds). Which does the author consider superior?
3
Understand the classification
Look for the terms 'manqul' (transmitted) and 'ma'qul' (rational). They represent two major branches of science.
4
Reflect on Ibn Abbas' quote
The quote about 500 degrees symbolizes the vast, hierarchical nature of knowledge. How does this relate to the division of scholars?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول المنوع إياه وما يتبعها من الكمالات الثانية التي بها تتفاضل أفراده بعضها على بعض من العقل أي استعداده لإدراك المعقولات والعلوم الضرورية الحاصلة له باستعمال الحواس، وإدراك المحسوسات والتنبه لما بينها من المشاركات والمباينات، وأهليته للنظر والاستدلال وترقيه بذلك في درجات الكمال، وعلمه بما أمكن واستحال فإذا كماله الأشرف الأعلى إنما هو بتعقل المعقولات الأولى واكتساب المجهولات منها، وإن كانت الأخلاق الحسنة التابعة للأعمال الصالحة كمالاً له معتداً به أيضاً لكن الكمالات العلمية أرفع وأسنى إذ لا كمال له كمعرفته تعالى. والعلوم متشعبة متكثرة والإحاطة بجملتها متعسرة أو متعذرة فلذلك افترق أهل العلم زمراً وتقطعوا أمرهم بينهم زبراً. بين منقول متخالف الأصناف ومعقول متباين الأطراف وفروع متدانية الجنوب وأصول متشابكة العروق. وتفاوت حالهم في اقتناء العلوم وتفاضل رجالهم في الترقي إلى مراتبها إلى أن قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: في درجاتهم إنها خمسمائة درجة ما بين الدرجتين مسيرة خمسمائة عام، والمراد تصوير الكثرة لا الحصر في هذه العدة. وقال بعض أكابر الأئمة وأحبار الأمة في بيان معنى الخبر المشهور والحديث المأثور: اختلاف أمتي رحمة. يعني يريد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم باختلاف أمته اختلاف هممهم في العلوم. فهمة واحد في الفقه لضبط الأحكام المتعلقة بالأفعال وهمة آخر في الكلام لحفظ...
The first specific differentia for it and what follows it from the second perfections by which its individuals surpass one another: from intellect, that is, its preparedness to apprehend intelligibles, and the necessary sciences obtained by using the senses, and apprehension of sensibles and awareness of the commonalities and differences between them, and its aptitude for reflection and inference and its progression thereby in the degrees of perfection, and its knowledge of what is possible and impossible. So his noblest and highest perfection is only in comprehending the primary intelligibles and acquiring the unknowns from them, even if good morals following good deeds are a considerable perfection for him as well, but the perfections of knowledge are higher and more exalted since he has no perfection like knowledge of the Exalted. And the sciences are numerous and branched out, and encompassing them all is difficult or impossible; therefore, the people of knowledge separated into groups and divided their matter among themselves into sections (zubur). Between transmitted (manqul) of different kinds and rational (ma'qul) of diverse aspects, and branches near each other, and roots intertwined. And their conditions varied in acquiring sciences and their men excelled in ascending to its ranks until Ibn Abbas – may God be pleased with them – said about their degrees: 'They are five hundred degrees, between every two degrees is a journey of five hundred years,' and the intent is to depict multiplicity, not limitation to this number. And some of the great imams and scholars of the community said in explaining the meaning of the famous report and the transmitted hadith: 'The difference of my community is a mercy.' He meant that the Messenger of God – may God bless him and grant him peace – intended by the difference of his community the difference of their aspirations in the sciences. One aspiration is in fiqh to master the rulings pertaining to actions, and another aspiration is in kalam to preserve...
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of human perfections, focusing on intellectual and scientific perfections. The author states that the first specific differentia for humans is the rational power, which includes preparedness for acquiring necessary sciences through the senses, reflection, and reasoning. The highest perfection is comprehending primary intelligibles and acquiring unknowns. Although good morals following good deeds are also a perfection, intellectual perfections are superior. Because the sciences are numerous and impossible to master fully, scholars have divided into groups. The author mentions transmitted sciences (منقول) and rational sciences (معقول), as well as branches and roots. He then cites Ibn Abbas who described five hundred degrees of knowledge, each separated by five hundred years’ travel, emphasizing the vastness of knowledge. Finally, a hadith 'The difference of my community is a mercy' is interpreted: it does not mean difference in doctrines but difference of aspirations in pursuing different sciences—one in fiqh, another in kalam, etc.

Key Terms
العقل
المعقولات
العلوم الضرورية
النظر والاستدلال
المنقول
المعقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest perfection for a human?
A) Good morals
B) Knowledge of God
C) Physical strength
D) Social status

Key Takeaway
The highest human perfection is intellectual knowledge, especially of God, and the vastness of knowledge necessitates scholarly specialization into transmitted and rational sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you prioritize the acquisition of intellectual knowledge of Allah over worldly pursuits? What concrete step can you take this week to increase your understanding of the primary intelligibles?
?
The passage says 'the perfections of knowledge are higher and more exalted since he has no perfection like knowledge of the Exalted.' If you were to die tonight, how confident are you that your knowledge of Allah is sufficient? What are you actively doing to grow that knowledge?
?
Scholars specialized because encompassing all sciences is impossible. Are you content with being a generalist in religious knowledge, or have you committed to deepening one area to serve the Ummah? What area calls you?

The Hadith on Difference as Mercy: A Scholarly Interpretation
Interprets the hadith on difference as mercy by referring to diversity of scholarly ambitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the hadith 'The difference of my community is a mercy' according to classical scholars.

Core Idea
The hadith refers to diversity of scholarly pursuits, not doctrinal disagreement.

A famous hadith states that difference among the Muslim community is a mercy. This lesson explores how a prominent scholar understood this hadith: as a reference to the multiplicity of aspirations in various sciences, such as fiqh, kalam, etc., not as a justification for contradictory beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith interpretation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The scholar explains the meaning by focusing on the word 'difference' as difference in aspirations (himam).
2
Identify the examples given
The passage mentions two examples: one aspiration in fiqh to master rulings, another in kalam to preserve something. What might be the purpose?
3
Contrast with a common misconception
Many people think this hadith allows different theological schools to contradict each other. Why does this interpretation differ?
This connects to the learning objective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith permits any theological disagreement among Muslims.
Correction: The hadith is about diversity of scholarly specializations, not about contradictory beliefs.

Checkpoint
How does the scholar interpret the word 'difference' (ikhtilaf) in the hadith?
A) As conflicting opinions in fiqh
B) As diversity of aspirations in sciences
C) As division into political groups
D) As disagreement in beliefs

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'The difference of my community is a mercy' is understood by classical scholars as a reference to the beneficial diversity of scholarly pursuits, not as a license for doctrinal contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a scholar whose specialization differs from yours (e.g., fiqh vs. tafsir), do you feel gratitude for the diversity of mercy or irritation at the difference? What does your reaction reveal about your heart?
?
The hadith 'Difference is a mercy' is often misused to justify division. Have you ever used it to excuse your own laziness in seeking truth or to avoid addressing a real contradiction? What will you do today to repent from that misuse?
?
Imagine a community where every Muslim pursued only one science. How would that diminish the mercy Allah intended? Which area of knowledge have you neglected, and what is one step you will take this week to honor the diversity of scholarly aspirations?

Intellectual Perfection and the Division of Sciences
Classification of human perfection and explanation for scholarly specialization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual perfection is the highest human perfection and how the impossibility of encompassing all sciences led to the specialization of scholars.

Core Idea
Intellectual perfection is the highest, and its vastness leads to scholarly specialization.

In this passage, the author explains the first type of perfection, which is intellectual, and argues that it is superior to moral perfection. Because the sciences are vast and impossible to encompass, scholars naturally divided into groups, each focusing on different branches. This lesson explores these ideas and their implications for the pursuit of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first type of perfection
Read the passage carefully to identify what the author means by 'the first type' of perfection. Note that it is intellectual perfection, stemming from the intellect's ability to perceive intelligibles and acquire necessary sciences.
The author starts by listing components: intellect, necessary sciences, suitability for reasoning, knowledge of possible and impossible.
2
Appreciate the superiority of intellectual perfection
Notice that the author explicitly states that intellectual perfections are higher and loftier than moral perfections, even though good morals are also a perfection. Understand the reasoning: knowing Allah is the highest perfection.
The phrase 'there is no perfection for him like his knowledge of Him' highlights the primacy of intellectual knowledge.
3
Grasp why the sciences cannot be fully encompassed
The author says the sciences are numerous, branching, and encompassing them is difficult or impossible. This is the key reason for specialization.
The word 'متعسرة أو متعذرة' indicates difficulty or impossibility.
4
See how scholars divided into groups
Identify the four categories mentioned: transmitted sciences (منقول), rational sciences (معقول), branches (فروع), and roots (أصول). Understand that these represent different types of knowledge.
The verse reference 'زبرا' is explained as either pieces or books.
5
Reflect on the vastness of degrees
Read the report from Ibn 'Abbas about five hundred degrees, each separated by five hundred years. Note that this is a metaphor for vast multiplicity, not a literal count.
The author clarifies: 'the intent is to depict multiplicity, not limitation to this number.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول المنوع إياه ( وما يتبعها ) من الكمالات الثانية التي بها تتفاضل أفراده بعضها على بعض ( من العقل ) أي استعداده لإدراك المعقولات ) والعلوم الضرورية ( الحاصلة له باستعمال الحواس، وإدراك المحسوسات والتنبه لما بينها من المشاركات والمباينات ، ( وأهليته للنظر والاستدلال) وترقيه بذلك في درجات الكمال، ( وعلمه بما أمكن واستحال فإذا كماله ( الأشرف الأعلى إنما هو ) بتعقل المعقولات ) الأولى واكتساب المجهولات) منها ، وإن كانت الأخلاق الحسنة التابعة للأعمال الصالحة كمالاً له معتداً به أيضاً لكن الكمالات العلمية أرفع وأسنى إذ لا كمال له كمعرفته تعالى ( والعلوم متشعبة متكثرة والإحاطة بجملتها متعسرة أو متعذرة فلذلك ) أي فلتعسر الإحاطة بل لتعذرها افترق أهل العلم زمراً ) فرقاً (وتقطعوا) أي تقسموا ( أمرهم بينهم زبرا) هو بفتح الباء الحديد ونحوها جمع زبرة وهي وبضمها جمع زبور بمعنى الكتاب أي اتخذوا أمر العلم وطلبهم إياه فيما بينهم قطعاً مختلفة، أو كتباً متفاوتة دائراً أمرهم فيه ( بين (منقول ) متخالف الأصناف ( ومعقول ) متباين الأطراف ( وفروع) متدانية الجنوب ) وأصول ) متشابكة العروق، ( وتفاوت) عطف على افترق ) حالهم ) في اقتناء العلوم ( وتفاضل رجالهم ) في الترقي إلى مراتبها إلى أن قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما ( في درجاتهم : إنها خمسمائة درجة ما بين الدرجتين) من تلك الدرج ( مسيرة خمسمائة عام، والمراد تصوير الكثرة لا الحصر في هذه العدة )
The first [type] prevented from it (and what follows it) from the secondary perfections by which its individuals excel each other: (from intellect) meaning his readiness to perceive intelligibles, and necessary sciences (obtained by using the senses, perceiving sensibles, and being alerted to the commonalities and differences between them), (and his suitability for consideration and inference) and his ascent thereby in degrees of perfection, (and his knowledge of what is possible and impossible) for his perfection (the noblest and highest is only) by comprehending the primary intelligibles and acquiring unknowns from them, even if good morals consequent upon righteous deeds are also a perfection for him worthy of consideration, but the intellectual perfections are higher and loftier since there is no perfection for him like his knowledge of Him, exalted. (And the sciences are numerous and branching, and encompassing all of them is difficult or impossible, therefore) — i.e., due to the difficulty, rather impossibility, of encompassing — (the people of knowledge divided into groups) factions (and became separated) i.e., they divided (their affair among themselves into 'zubur') — with fatha on the ba' meaning iron pieces, plural of zubra, and with dhamma plural of zabur meaning book — i.e., they made the affair of knowledge and their seeking it among themselves into different parts, or various books, their affair revolving among them (between transmitted [sciences] of diverse kinds, and rational [sciences] of different aspects, and branches near in kind, and roots intertwined veins, (and disparity) — conjunction on 'divided' — (of their states) in acquiring sciences, (and superiority of their men) in ascending to their ranks, until Ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, said (about their degrees: 'They are five hundred degrees, between each two degrees) of those degrees (is a distance of five hundred years, and the intent is to depict multiplicity, not limitation to this number).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of human perfection, which is intellectual, and explains that although moral perfection is also valuable, intellectual perfection is higher. Because it is impossible to encompass all sciences, scholars naturally specialize, dividing into groups focused on transmitted and rational sciences, branches and roots, with varying states and degrees. The report from Ibn Abbas about five hundred degrees illustrates the vast multiplicity, not a literal count.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral perfection is equal to or higher than intellectual perfection.
Correction: The passage states that intellectual perfections are 'higher and loftier' and that there is no perfection like knowledge of Allah, establishing the clear superiority of intellectual perfection.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why did the people of knowledge divide into groups?
A) Because of disagreement about the truth
B) Because encompassing all sciences is impossible
C) Because they were commanded to do so
D) Because of differences in intelligence

Key Takeaway
Intellectual perfection is the highest, and the vastness of knowledge naturally leads scholars to specialize.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage declares that the greatest perfection is knowledge of Allah. Does your daily pursuit of knowledge reflect this priority? What specific change will you make this week to seek more knowledge of Allah through His names, attributes, or creation?
?
Scholars specialized because no one could master all sciences. Are you trying to learn too many things superficially? Identify one subject within Islamic sciences that you will delve into deeply, and commit to a consistent study plan starting today.
?
Ibn 'Abbas described five hundred degrees of knowledge, each separated by five hundred years. This illustrates the vast journey of learning. Are you satisfied with your current station, or are you actively striving to ascend? Write down one concrete action you will take today to move closer to the next degree.

Interpreting 'Ikhtilaf Ummati Rahma' as Diversity of Aspirations
Explains the famous hadith as referring to diversity of intellectual pursuits, not doctrinal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'Ikhtilaf Ummati Rahma' is interpreted by scholars to refer to diversity of intellectual pursuits rather than contradictory beliefs.

Core Idea
The community's difference is a mercy in the diversity of scholarly aspirations.

This passage addresses a famous hadith that is often misunderstood. Some might think it permits contradictory teachings, but a deeper scholarly interpretation shows it celebrates the variety of specializations within Islamic sciences. Let's explore this interpretation as presented by a great imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by attributing the interpretation to 'some of the great imams and scholars'.
2
Identify the key interpretation
The passage explicitly states that the difference intended is in the aspirations (himam) for sciences, not in beliefs or doctrines. Find the phrase that makes this clear.
It says 'by the difference of his community the difference of their aspirations in the sciences'.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples: one aspiration in fiqh to master rulings, another in kalam to preserve creeds. Understand how each serves a distinct purpose yet together regulate the afterlife and justice.
4
Consider the analogy
The passage compares this to the diversity of crafts and industries that ensure the system of worldly livelihood. Notice how this analogy reinforces the idea that diversity is beneficial and orderly.
The phrase 'just as the aspirations of the people of crafts and industries differed' shows the parallel.
5
Reflect on the 'mercy'
Both the diversity of sciences and the diversity of crafts are described as a mercy. Consider why this interpretation refutes the misconception that the hadith permits contradictory beliefs.
The passage concludes that this difference is also a mercy, mentioned here as subsidiary and analogy.

Source Text

وقال بعض أكابر الأئمة وأحبار (الأمة الحبر بالكسر والفتح العالم الذي يحبر الكلام ويزينه (في) بيان معنى الخبر المشهور والحديث المأثور) المروي من أثرت الحديث إذا ذكرته عن غيرك اختلاف أمتي رحمة ) عطف بيان للخبر، وقوله ( يعني ) أي يريد الرسول ل باختلاف أمته اختلاف هممهم في العلوم) مقول ذلك البعض، وما بعده تفصيل لذلك الاختلاف أعني قوله (فهمة واحد في الفقه ) لضبط الأحكام المتعلقة بالأفعال ( وهمة آخر في الكلام لحفظ العقائد فينتظم بهما أمر المعاد، وقانون العدلُ المقيم للنوع (كما اختلف همم أصحاب الحرف والصناعات ( ليقوم كلُّ واحد منهم (بحرفة) أو صناعة (فيتم النظام ) في المعاش المعين لذلك الانتظام، وهذا الاختلاف أيضاً رحمةٌ كما لا يخفى لكنه مذكور هنا تبعاً، ونظيراً
And some of the great imams and scholars of the nation (al-hibr: with kasr and fatha, the scholar who beautifies speech and adorns it) said in explanation of the meaning of the famous report and transmitted hadith (related from 'athara al-hadith' if you narrate it from someone else) 'The difference of my community is a mercy' — this is an explanatory apposition to the report, and his saying (meaning) i.e., the Messenger intended by the difference of his community the difference of their aspirations in the sciences — that is the statement of that scholar, and what follows is a detailing of that difference, namely his saying: (so one aspiration is in fiqh) to master the rulings related to actions, (and another aspiration is in kalam) to preserve the creeds, so that the affair of the Hereafter is regulated by them both, and the law of justice that sustains the species. (Just as the aspirations of the people of crafts and industries differed) so that each one of them (takes up a craft) or industry (so that the system is perfected) in the livelihood appointed for that order. And this difference is also a mercy, as is not hidden, but it is mentioned here as a subsidiary and analogy.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage interprets the well-known hadith 'The difference of my community is a mercy' by explaining that the difference intended is in the aspirations (himam) of scholars for different sciences, not in contradictory beliefs. It gives examples: one person aspires to fiqh (jurisprudence) to master legal rulings, another to kalam (theology) to preserve creeds, and together they regulate the afterlife and establish justice. This is compared to the diversity of crafts and industries that ensure the system of worldly livelihood. This diversity is also a mercy, but is mentioned here as subsidiary and analogous.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the difference refers to diversity of scholarly specializations (aspirations in sciences), not contradictory doctrines.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Ikhtilaf ummati rahma' refers to the diversity of scholarly aspirations, not to contradictory beliefs, and this diversity is a mercy for the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What can you do to foster mutual respect for different legitimate specializations, just as the analogy of crafts shows their interdependence?
?

Commit to one concrete action you will take this week to increase your mercy towards those with different scholarly focuses from your own.

Objection and Answer on the Obligation of Kalam
Addresses the objection that kalam is not obligatory because taqlid in beliefs is permitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars answered the objection that kalam is not obligatory because taqlid is permitted, and the role of interpreting 'objectives' of kalam.

Core Idea
The obligation of kalam is defended by distinguishing the discipline from its objectives.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the study of kalam: if taqlid is permissible in beliefs, why must one learn kalam? The author presents the objection and then provides a subtle answer that preserves kalam's foundational role through a grammatical reinterpretation. By understanding this exchange, you will see how classical scholars defended the necessity of kalam even while acknowledging alternative views.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim that kalam is not obligatory because taqlid in beliefs is allowed. Notice that the objection targets the assumption that kalam must be studied for ijtihad.
The objection relies on the majority view that taqlid in beliefs is permissible.
2
Analyze the answer's structure
The answer first concedes the chosen view (that taqlid is permissible), then reinterprets the original statement by applying the principle of 'deletion of the genitive construct' (hadhf al-mudaf).
This shifts the meaning from 'upon the science of kalam' to 'upon the objectives of kalam'.
3
Reflect on the interpretive principle
Consider why such a grammatical reinterpretation is used. How does it preserve the foundational status of kalam without insisting on its personal obligation for every individual?
This reflects the sophistication of kalam scholarship in handling objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أي) وعلى علم الكلام بناء العلوم الشرعية والأحكام الفقهية) قيل هذا مبني على وجوب علم الكلام فى الاجتهاد والمختار خلافه بناء على جواز التقليد في الاعتقاديات عند الجمهور، وجوابه بعد تسليم أن المختار ما ذكر الحمل على حذف المضاف، أي وعلى مقاصد
His saying: (i.e., upon the science of kalam is built the religious sciences and jurisprudential rulings) — it was said: 'This is based on the obligation of the science of kalam in ijtihad, and the chosen [view] is contrary to it, based on the permissibility of taqlid in beliefs according to the majority.' And the answer is, after conceding that the chosen [view] is what was mentioned, [it is] to be interpreted as deletion of the genitive construct, i.e., upon the objectives.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This marginal note presents an objection: the claim that kalam is the foundation of other sciences assumes that studying kalam is obligatory for ijtihad, but the preferred view (according to the majority) is that taqlid is permissible in beliefs, so kalam may not be obligatory. The answer concedes the preferred view but interprets the statement as involving deletion of the genitive construct (hadhf al-mudaf), meaning that it is not kalam itself but the objectives (maqasid) of kalam that are the foundation.

Key Terms

اجتهاد
تقليد
اعتقاديات
علم الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the permissibility of taqlid in beliefs makes the study of kalam entirely unnecessary.
Correction: While taqlid may be personally permissible, the objectives of kalam remain essential as the foundation of other religious sciences.

Checkpoint

B) By interpreting 'science of kalam' as the objectives of kalam
C) By rejecting the permissibility of taqlid
D) By saying the statement is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The answer to the objection shows that while taqlid in beliefs may be permissible, the objectives of kalam remain essential as the foundation of religious knowledge.

Five Flawed Approaches in Kalam Authorship
Author classifies kalam authors into five types with their flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five deficient types of kalam authors described by the author and why each approach is inadequate for sound theological inquiry.

Core Idea
Not all kalam authors are equal; five common flaws reveal how methodology must be refined.

The author of the matn provides a sharp critique of common but flawed approaches in kalam writing. By classifying authors into five types, he highlights the pitfalls that students must avoid. This lesson unpacks each type, showing how they deviate from achieving true knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the classification. Notice the recurring phrase 'ومنهم من' which introduces each type.
2
Identify each author type and its flaw
List each of the five types described. For each, write down the description and the specific flaw mentioned by the author (e.g., relying on mere conviction, looking from a distance, etc.).
3
Reflect on the criteria for proper method
Ask yourself: What would the opposite (correct) approach look like for each flaw? Use the commentary to guide your understanding.
4
Connect to broader kalam methodology
Consider how these flaws relate to the overall goal of kalam: achieving certainty and uncovering genuine knowledge of divine matters.
This lesson sets the stage for appreciating correct methodology in later texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنهم من كشف عن مقاصده القناع بإزالة أستارها عنها لكنه قنع من دلائله بالإقناع بما يفيد الظن ويقنع، ومنهم من سلك المسلك السديد في الدلائل لكن يلحظ المقاصد من مكان بعيد فلم يكشفها ولم يحررها، ومنهم من غرضه نقل المذاهب التي ذهبت إليها طوائف من الناس واستقروا عليها والأقوال التي صدرت عمن قبله والتصرف في وجوه الاستدلال وتكثير السؤال والجواب ولا يبالي إلام المآل، ومنهم من يلفق مغالط لترويج رأيه ولا يدري أن النقاد من ورائه، ومنهم من ينظر في مقدمة مقدمة ويختار منها من المقدمات التي نظر فيها ما يؤدي إليه بادئ رأيه ويبني عليها مطالبه وربما يكر بعضها بعض تلك المقدمات على بعض بالإبطال ويتطرق إلى
For among them is one who removed the veil from its purposes by removing their covers, but he was satisfied with his proofs by conviction that yields opinion and convinces. And among them is one who followed the right path in proofs, but he looks at the purposes from a far place, so he did not uncover them nor refine them. And among them is one whose aim is to transmit the schools that groups of people held and settled upon, and the statements that came from those before him, and to manipulate aspects of inference and multiply question and answer, and he does not care what the outcome is. And among them is one who fabricates fallacies to promote his opinion, and he does not know that the critics are behind him. And among them is one who looks at a premise, then at another premise, and chooses from the premises he considered what his first impression leads him to, and builds his demands upon them; and perhaps he attacks some of those premises with others by nullification and proceeds to
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a classification of authors who write on kalam, enumerating five types with their specific shortcomings. 1) The first type removes the veil from the subject but relies on mere convincing (iẓnâ‘) rather than certainty, yielding only opinion. 2) The second type follows the right method in proofs but only looks at the purposes from a distance, failing to uncover or refine them. 3) The third type merely transmits the opinions of past groups and individuals, multiplying questions and answers without concern for the outcome. 4) The fourth type fabricates fallacious arguments to promote his own view, unaware that critics are watching. 5) The fifth type selects premises based on his initial impression without deep reflection, building his demands upon them, and may even set premises against each other in a destructive manner. The list is open-ended as the text trails off into commentary. Each type represents a deviation from proper scholarly methodology.

الإقناع
مسلك سديد
نقل المذاهب
مغالط
بادئ الرأي

Checkpoint
Which type of kalam author fabricates fallacies to promote his opinion?
A) The one who removes the veil but relies on conviction.
B) The one who follows the right path but looks from afar.
C) The one who fabricates fallacies.
D) The one who selects premises based on first impression.

Key Takeaway
A sound kalam author must combine correct proof methodology with deep insight into purposes, avoiding mere transmission, fallacy, or hasty premise selection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author criticizes those who rely on mere convincing rather than certainty. In what areas of your faith do you hold strong opinions without deep certainty? What would it cost you to seek certainty instead?
?
The fifth type chooses premises based on first impression. When have you accepted a religious argument because it 'felt right' rather than because it was soundly grounded in revelation and reason? How can you guard against that?

Defects of Previous Kalam Books and Authors
The author enumerates problems with previous kalam books and classifies seven defective author types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various shortcomings of earlier kalam works and authors that prompted the author to write his own book.

Core Idea
Previous kalam books fail in different ways, and their authors have seven distinct defects.

The author begins by describing the low state of learners and the inadequacy of both abridged and lengthy theological works. Then he identifies seven types of deficient authors, each with a characteristic flaw. This lesson unpacks these criticisms, which serve as the backdrop for the author's own contribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the author's critique. Notice the flow from learners' state to book types to author types.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two categories of books
Note how the author criticizes both 'mukhtasarat' (abridgements) and 'mutawwalat' (long works) for opposite reasons: brevity vs. prolixity.
These are two sides of the same problem.
3
List the seven types of authors
Go through each type and understand the specific defect. Use the guided breakdown below for details.
Try to memorize the types as a set.
4
Reflect on the purpose of this critique
Ask yourself why the author includes such a list. It is not just complaining; it establishes his own credibility and justifies his writing.
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المؤلف
حال
قوله ( والهمم قاصرة مؤولة بالظرف نظراً إلى قرب الحال من ظرف الزمان فصح وقوعها صلة ،لما، وهذا من قبيل الميل إلى المعنى والإعراض عما يقتضيه اللفظ بظاهره، أي انتفي حصول الشفاء والإرواء عن تلك الكتب في كل زمان لا مثل انتفائه في زمان قصور الهمم، فإن هذا الانتفاء أقوى ) والرغبات ) في تعلمه ( فاترة والدواعي ) إليه (قليلة والصوارف) عنه ( متكاثرة ) ، ثم إنه بين ما أجمله من تلك الكتب بقوله فمختصراتها قاصرة عن إفادة المرام) باختصارها المخل ) ومطولاتها مع الأسام) بما فيها من الإسهاب الممل ( مدهشة للأفهام في الوصول إلى حقائق المسائل، ثم زاد في ذلك البيان بذكر أحوال المصنفين في تصانيفهم الكلامية، فقال : ( فمنهم من كشف عن مقاصده ) أي مقاصد علم الكلام ( القناع ) بإزالة أستارها عنها (و) لكنه (قنع من دلائله (بالإقناع بما يفيد الظن ويقنع، ( ومنهم من سلك المسلك السديد ) في الدلائل ( لكن يلحظ المقاصد ) ينظر إليها بمؤخر عينه ( من مكان بعيد ) فلم يكشفها، ولم يحررها . ( ومنهم من غرضه نقل المذاهب التي ذهبت إليها طوائف من الناس واستقروا عليها ( والأقوال ) التي صدرت عمن قبله ( والتصرف ) بالرفع عطفاً على نقل ( في وجوه الاستدلال وتكثير السؤال والجواب ولا يبالي إلامَ المآل ) إلى أي شيء مرجع نقله وتصرفه، وتكثيره هل يترتب عليها ثمرة أو يزداد بها حيرة ( ومنهم من يلفق) يجمع ويضم ( مغالط) شبهاً يغلط فيها لترويج رأيه ولا يدري أن النقاد من ورائه) فيزيفها ويفضحها ( ومنهم من ينظر في مقدمة مقدمة ويختار منها من المقدمات التي نظر فيها ( ما يؤدي إليه بادئ رأيه أي أوله بلا إمعان تأمل ويبني عليها مطالبه (وربما يكر) ويحمل (بعضها بعض تلك المقدمات ) على بعض بالإبطال ويتطرق إلى المقاصد بسببه الاختلال ومنهم من يكبر حجم الكتاب بالبسط) في العبارة ) والتكرار ) في المعنى ليظن به أنه بحر زخار كثير الماء مواج من زخر البحر امتد وارتفع ( ومنهم من هو كحاطب (ليل كمن يجمع الحطب في الليل، فلا يميز بين الرطب واليابس والضار والنافع ) وجالب رجل وخيل الرجل جمع الراجل وهو خلاف الفارس والخيل الفرسان يعني كجالب العسكر بأسره ضعيفه وقويه، ثم أشار إلى وجه الشبه في جانب المشبه في كلا التشبيهين، بقوله : ( يجمع ما يجده من كلام القوم ينقله نقلاً، ولا يستعمل عقلا ليعرف أغث ما أخذه أم ثمين وسخيف ) أي رقيق ركيك ( ما ألفاه ما وجده أم متين أي قوي فصار جميع ما ذكره باعثاً له على تأليف الكتاب
Introduction of the author. Status. He said: "And the aspirations are deficient, interpreted as an adverb, considering closeness of status [as] a time adverb, so it is correct that it occurs as a relative clause for 'lamma', and this is of the type of inclining towards meaning and disregarding what the literal wording requires, meaning: the attainment of healing and quenching from those books is negated at every time, not like its negation at the time of deficient aspirations, for this negation is stronger." And the desires in learning it are languid, and the motivations towards it are few, and the diversions from it are many. Then he explained what he summarized from those books by his saying: "So their abridgements are deficient in giving the desired goal due to their defective brevity, and their long versions, despite their praise, due to their tedious prolixity, bewilder intellects in reaching the realities of the problems." Then he increased that clarification by mentioning the conditions of the authors in their theological works, and said: "Among them is one who removed the veil from its purposes, but he contented himself with his proofs by persuasion that yields probability and satisfies. Among them is one who followed the correct path in proofs, but he looks at the purposes with the back of his eye from a distant place, so he neither revealed them nor refined them. Among them is one whose aim is to transmit the schools that groups of people adopted and settled upon, and the statements that issued from before him, and to dispose in the aspects of argumentation and multiply question and answer, and he cares not to what end, to what result his transmission and disposition and multiplication lead, whether fruit results from them or he increases in bewilderment. Among them is one who pieces together fallacies, sophistries, to promote his view, and he does not know that the critics are behind him, so they debunk and expose them. Among them is one who looks into a premise, a premise, and selects from them from the premises he examined what leads to his initial opinion, without careful reflection, and builds his claims on them, and perhaps he repeats and imposes some of those premises on others by refutation and thus the purposes are corrupted. Among them is one who enlarges the size of the book by extension in expression and repetition in meaning, so that it may be thought that he is a roaring sea with much water, surging. Among them is one who is like a gatherer of firewood at night, like one who collects firewood at night and cannot distinguish between dry and wet, harmful and beneficial, and a bringer of foot soldiers and horsemen meaning like one who brings the entire army, its weak and its strong." Then he pointed to the aspect of similarity in the compared side in both comparisons by his saying: "He collects what he finds from the speech of the people, transmitting it verbatim, and does not use reason to know whether what he took is inferior or valuable, and insipid meaning thin and flimsy, and what he found is solid meaning strong." So all that he mentioned became an incentive for him to compose the book.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a classification of various shortcomings of previous kalam works. The author begins by noting the state of learners: aspirations are deficient, desires are weak, and diversions are many. Then he describes the books themselves: abridgements are too brief, long works are too verbose and confuse the understanding. Next, he lists seven types of authors based on their defects: (1) one who unveils purposes but relies only on probable proofs (إقناع); (2) one who uses sound proofs but fails to clarify the purposes; (3) one who merely transmits doctrines and opinions without discernment; (4) one who compiles fallacies to promote his own view; (5) one who selects premises hastily without deep reflection; (6) one who inflates his book with verbosity and repetition; and (7) one who indiscriminately collects material like a firewood gatherer at night or a commander bringing all soldiers weak and strong, without using reason to separate valuable from worthless. The passage concludes by stating that these various inadequacies motivated the author to write his own book.

Misconception: One might think the author is merely listing complaints.
Correction: He is actually establishing his own authority and justifying his composition.

?

The Author's Motivation and Balanced Methodology
Author explains his motivation and balanced approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's motivation for writing this book and the balanced, methodical approach he employed.

Core Idea
The author's middle path—neither too long nor too short—guides his entire work.

The author begins by telling us why he wrote this book and how he wrote it. He describes his motivation as compassion for students and a drive to present the truth. He then outlines the key features of his approach, emphasizing balance and careful revision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses vivid imagery like 'arguments strutting'.
2
Identify the key methodological principles
From the passage, extract the principles the author lists: moderate length, inclusion of core ideas, distinction between true and false, effort in refining topics, and the use of critical points.
This helps you see the author's self-conscious methodology.
3
Reflect on the author's self-critique
The author describes looking from sources to origins, pondering exits before entering, and retreating to review. Consider why this iterative process is important for theological writing.
This shows his commitment to accuracy and thoroughness.

فحداني ( ساقني وبعثني ( الحدب ) العطف والشفقة على أهل الطلب ) لهذا العلم ) ومن له في تحقيق الحق) فيه (أرب) حاجة إلى أن كتبت هذا إشارةً إلى كتابه ( كتاباً مقتصداً) متوسطاً (لا) مطولاً مثلاً) بتطويله ) ولا مختصراً مخلاً بإيجازه ( أودعته ) أوردت فيه (لب الألباب) خلاصة العقول ( وميزت فيه القشر من اللباب ولم آل أي لم أترك ( جهداً) سعياً وطاقة ( في تحرير المطالب الكلامية (وتقرير المذاهب) الاعتقادية (وتركت الحجج (تتبختر تتمايل في مشيها كالمتدلل بجماله ) اتضاحاً) مفعول له ( والشبه تتضاءل) تتصاغر وتتحاقر افتضاحاً) كالذي ظهرت قبائحه، وانكشفت سوآته ونبهت في النقد والتزييف ) للدلائل ( والهدم والترصيف ) أي الأحكام للمقاصد على نكت هي ينابيع التحقيق وفقر تهدي إلى مظان التدقيق ) النكتة طائفة من الكلام منقحة مشتملة على لطيفة مؤثرة في القلوب والينبوع عين الماء، والفقرة بالسكون فقارة الظهر وتطلق على أجود بيت في القصيدة تشبيهاً له بها وعلى قرينة الأسجاع أيضاً ) وأنا أنظر من (الموارد مواضع الورود جمع مورد من ورد الماء. So impelled me (drove me and motivated me) compassion (affection and sympathy for the people of seeking) of this science, and whoever has in attaining the truth in it a need, to write this, referring to his book, a moderate book (intermediate) not making it prolix by its prolongation, nor abbreviated making it defective by its conciseness. I deposited in it the core of cores (the cream of intellects), and I distinguished in it the husk from the core, and I did not spare (I did not leave) effort (striving and ability) in refining the theological topics and establishing the doctrinal positions. And I left the arguments strutting (swaying in their walk like one displaying his beauty) due to clarity, and the doubters becoming small (diminishing and shrinking) due to shame, like one whose uglinesses appeared and whose private parts were exposed. And I alerted in criticism and debunking of proofs, and demolition and consolidation of the purposes, to subtle points that are sources of verification and paragraphs that guide to the places of scrutiny. (The subtle point is a refined snippet of speech containing a pleasant subtlety affecting hearts; the spring is a source of water; the paragraph is like a vertebra of the back, and is applied to the best verse in a poem by analogy, and also to the rhyme link.) And I look from the sources (places of arrival) to the origins (places of return), and I ponder the exits before I place my foot in the entrances, then I retreat backwards (i.e., turn back) contemplating what I have presented: is there any deficiency in it? so I remove it and complete it. And I return the gaze time after time: do I see any cracks? (i.e., splits). He said: "And I did not leave" it implies the meaning of leaving, so he made 'juhdan' its object, and there are other aspects we mentioned in the margins of al-Mutawwal.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the author's statement of purpose and methodology. He begins by saying that compassion for students of this science and the need for truth motivated him to write this book. Key features: the book is moderate (مقتصد) – not overly long nor overly short. He includes the essence of intellects (لب الألباب) and distinguishes the true from the false. He spares no effort in refining theological topics (المطالب الكلامية) and presenting doctrinal positions (المذاهب الاعتقادية). He describes how arguments become clear and doubters are shamed. He also mentions that he includes critical points (نكت) that are sources of verification and guidance. His method involves careful examination: he looks from the sources (الموارد) to the origins (المصادر), ponders the exits before entering, and revises his work to remove deficiencies. The final commentary explains the grammar of 'لم أترك'.

The Author's Description of His Book's Perfection
The author enumerates the perfections of his book using negations and structural metaphors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that his book is perfectly crafted: free from defects and harmonious in structure.

Core Idea
A perfect book has no crookedness, doubt, hesitation, or inconsistency.

The author concludes his introduction by describing the care he took in composing this book. He uses vivid metaphors to assert that the book is flawless: no crookedness, no doubt, no hesitation, no inconsistency. He also emphasizes the harmony of its parts. This passage reveals the author's confidence in his work and sets the stage for the content that follows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Context
Recognize that the author is praising his own book after editing it. He lists qualities that demonstrate its perfection.
This is a common feature in classical Islamic scholarly works.
2
Identify the Negated Qualities
The author uses four negations: no crookedness (عوج), no doubt (ارتياب), no hesitation (لجلجة), and no inconsistency (اضطراب). These cover both conceptual and stylistic flaws.
These are not literal physical defects but rhetorical metaphors.
3
Analyze the Structural Harmony
The author says the book's beginnings are proportionate to its ends, and its predecessors are intertwined with its successors. This emphasizes internal consistency and coherence.
4
Reflect on the Author's Purpose
Why does the author make such a claim? It establishes his authority and assures the reader that the book is reliable and well-crafted.
This sets a high standard for the content to follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأسده وأصلحه ( حافظاً) حال من فاعل كتبت وما في حيزه من أودعته، وما عطف عليه أي فعلت كل ذلك حافظاً (للأوضاع ) التي ينبغي أن يحافظ عليها (رامزاً) مشيراً بإيجاز العبارة ( مشبعاً) موضحاً بإطنابها ( في مقام الرمز والإشباع) ولقد بالغ في تحرير كتابه ونصح طالبيه ( حتى جاء) متعلق بتلك الأفعال المذكورة ( كما أردت، ووفق الله وسدد في إتمام ما قصدت ) ثم بين مجيئه على وفق إرادته بقوله (جاء كلاماً لا عوج فيه ولا ارتياب ولا لجلجةً أي ولا تردد ( ولا اضطراب متناسباً صدوره ) أوائله ( وروادفه ) أواخره ( متعانقاً سوابقه ولواحقه)
Then I consolidated and corrected it (keeping) – a circumstantial accusative from the agent of 'I wrote' and what is in its scope from 'I deposited it' and what is conjoined to it – meaning I did all that while preserving (the proprieties) which ought to be preserved, (signaling) briefly with expression, (elaborating) clarifying with amplification (in the place of signaling and elaboration). And he went to great lengths in editing his book and advising its students (until it came) – attached to those mentioned verbs – (as I desired, and God granted success and guidance in completing what I intended). Then he explained its coming according to his will by his saying: (it came as speech with no crookedness (عوج), no doubt (ارتياب), no hesitation – i.e., no vacillation – and no inconsistency (اضطراب), its beginnings (صدوره) proportionate and its ends (روادفه), its predecessors (سوابقه) and successors (لواحقه) intertwined).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that explains the author's own praise of his book. It begins by explaining the phrase 'I consolidated and corrected it, keeping' – noting the grammatical analysis that 'keeping' is a circumstantial accusative. The author then clarifies each descriptive term: 'signaling' means expressing briefly, 'elaborating' means clarifying with amplification, and these are used appropriately. He then remarks on the author's diligence in editing and advising students. The phrase 'until it came as I desired' is linked to the previous verbs. The final description states that the book came as speech with no crookedness (عوج), no doubt (ارتياب), no hesitation, and no inconsistency (اضطراب), with its parts harmoniously proportionate and intertwined from beginning to end.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The metaphor of 'no crookedness' is taken as a literal physical defect in the book.
Correction: It is a rhetorical device asserting perfection of argument and style, not a physical description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you produce work—whether writing, teaching, or speaking—do you aim for the same level of perfection as the author describes, free from crookedness, doubt, hesitation, and inconsistency? What area of your life most needs this kind of meticulous refinement, and what concrete step will you take today to begin removing these defects?
?
The author attributes the perfection of his book to God's success and guidance. Think of your own achievements—your knowledge, skills, or projects. Do you habitually recognize God's role in them, or do you subtly take credit for yourself? How can you shift your internal narrative to acknowledge divine assistance in even the smallest accomplishments?
?
The author 'went to great lengths in editing his book and advising its students.' Reflect on your attitude toward feedback and correction. Are you eager to be advised and to improve, or do you resist? How can you actively seek out honest critique of your character or work starting this week, and what would it cost you to act on it?

The Six Stations of the Book
Author enumerates the six stations and their basis in classification of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six-station structure of al-Mawaqif and how the classification of existence determines the sequence of these stations.

Core Idea
The book's six stations mirror the logical divisions of existence.

Before diving into the detailed arguments, the author provides a bird's-eye view of the entire work. This passage reveals how the six stations are not arbitrary but follow a rational classification of what exists: the preliminary needed for kalam, universal matters, accidents, substances, divinity, and transmitted matters. Understanding this framework will help you navigate the rest of the book.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the six stations
Read the Arabic passage that enumerates the six stations. Notice how each station is defined by what kind of existent it studies.
The classification starts with what must be presented first, then moves to what is not specific, then specific contingents, then the Necessary.
2
Identify the three divisions of existence
The passage divides existence into universal matters (non-specific), contingent beings (accidents and substances), and the Necessary (God). Locate these and see how they map to stations 2-6.
Station 1 is preliminaries, which is not a division of existence but a prerequisite.
3
Understand the logic of 'must'
The author says some topics 'must' be presented in kalam. The commentary clarifies that 'must' means commendatory, not logical necessity. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكتاب مرتب على ستة مواقف وذلك لأن ما يذكر فيه إما أن يجب تقديمه في علم الكلام، وهو الموقف الأول في المقدمات أو لا يجب وحينئذ إما أن يبحث فيه عما لا يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة للموجود ، وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة، أو عما يختص فإما بالممكن الذي لا يقوم بنفسه بل بغيره، وهو الموقف الثالث في الأعراض، أو بالممكن الذي يقوم بنفسه وهو الموقف الرابع في الجواهر، وإما بالواجب تعالى فإما باعتبار إرساله الرسل وبعثه الأنبياء وهو الموقف السادس في السمعيات، أو لا باعتباره وهو الموقف الخامس في الإلهيات والوجه في التقديم والتأخير أن المقدمات يجب تقديمها على الكل، والأمور العامة كالمبادئ لما عداها، والسمعيات متوقفة على الإلهيات المتوقفة على مباحث الممكنات، وأما تقديم العرض على الجوهر فلأنه قد يستدل بأحوال الأعراض على أحوال الجواهر، كما يستدل بأحوال الحركة والسكون على حدوث الأجسام، وبقطع المسافة المتناهية في زمان متناه على عدم تركبها من الجواهر الأفراد التي لا تتناهى، ومنهم من قدم مباحث الجوهر نظراً إلى أن وجود العرض متوقف على وجوده . قوله: (ما يذكر فيه أي المقصود الذي يذكر فيه فلا يرد الخطبة، والمراد بالوجوب الوجوب الاستحساني وبالتقديم التقديم على كل ما عداه فلا يرد بعض المباحث الذي هو كالمبادي لبعض دون بعض، كالأمور العامة . قوله : ( في علم الكلام أي في تحصيله سواء كان جزءاً منه كمباحث النظر، أو لا كالرؤوس الثمانية التي هي مبادئ الشروع . قوله : ( وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة) أي هو المقصود من الموقف الثاني وإن ذكر بالاستطراد في هذا الموقف ما يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة كالوجوب والتقدم، ووجود القدم الصفات لا ينافي القول باختصاصه بالواجب على معنى عدم وجوده في الجوهر، والعرض، فإن الصفات ليست منهما على أنها ليست غير الذات وأيضاً قالوا: فالقدم الذاتي لا يوجد فيه أصلاً ، وقيل : المراد بعدم الاختصاص أن لا يختص مع مقابله كما أشير إليه في أول هذا الموقف، كما سيجيء زيادة بحث إن شاء الله تعالى
And the book is arranged into six stations. That is because what is mentioned in it either must be presented in the science of kalam – and that is the first station on preliminaries – or it need not be. In the latter case, either it investigates what is not specific to any of the three divisions of existence – and that is the second station on universal matters – or it investigates what is specific, either to the contingent that does not subsist by itself but by another – and that is the third station on accidents – or to the contingent that subsists by itself – and that is the fourth station on substances – or to the Necessary, exalted be He, either with consideration of His sending messengers and raising prophets – and that is the sixth station on transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) – or without that consideration – and that is the fifth station on divinity (ilahiyyat). The reason for the order is that preliminaries must be presented before everything, universal matters are like principles for what comes after them, and transmitted matters depend on divinity which depends on inquiries into contingents. As for presenting accident before substance, it is because the states of accidents can be used to infer the states of substances, as the states of motion and rest are used to infer the origination of bodies, and crossing a finite distance in finite time is used to infer that they are not composed of indivisible atoms that are infinite. And some people presented substance before accident because the existence of accident depends on the existence of substance. His saying: 'what is mentioned in it' means the intended content that is mentioned in it, so the sermon is not included. What is meant by 'must' is commendatory obligation (wujub istihsani), and by 'presentation' is presentation over all else, so it does not negate some topics that are like principles for some but not others, such as universal matters. His saying: 'in the science of kalam' means in its acquisition, whether it is part of it – like discussions of consideration (nazar) – or not – like the eight heads that are principles of commencement. His saying: 'and that is the second station on universal matters' means that it is the intended content of the second station, even if in this station what is specific to one of the three divisions – like necessity and priority – is mentioned by way of digression. The existence of the attribute of pre-eternity does not contradict the statement that it is specific to the Necessary in the sense that it does not exist in substance and accident, for attributes are not among them, and they are not other than the essence. Also, they said: essential pre-eternity does not occur in it at all. And it is said: the intended meaning of non-specificity is that it is not specific together with its opposite, as was indicated at the beginning of this station, as further discussion will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the overall structure of the book into six stations (mawaqif). The classification is based on whether a topic must be presented first in kalam (preliminaries), and then on whether it deals with universal matters or with specific existents. Specific existents are divided into accidents (contingent non-self-subsisting), substances (contingent self-subsisting), and the Necessary (God). The Necessary is then discussed in terms of divinity (ilahiyyat) and prophethood (sam'iyyat). The order is justified: preliminaries first, then universal matters as principles, then accidents before substances because accidents can be used as evidence for substances (e.g., motion and rest indicate bodily origination), then divinity, then transmitted matters. The following commentary clarifies that 'must' means commendatory obligation, that 'science of kalam' includes both its parts and prerequisites, and that the second station (universal matters) may include specific topics by digression.

المقدمات
الأمور العامة
الأعراض
الجواهر
الإلهيات
السمعيات

Key Takeaway
The six stations of al-Mawaqif are logically derived from the divisions of existence: preliminaries, universal matters, accidents, substances, divinity, and transmitted matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author insists that certain topics 'must be presented first' in the science of kalam. What foundational truths about Allah or His attributes have you assumed you already know, but never actually studied from a systematic source? Will you commit to revisiting those 'preliminaries' this week?
?
The book's structure separates divine attributes (ilahiyyat) from prophethood (sam'iyyat). Which of these two areas do you find yourself more drawn to, and why? Could your imbalance be leaving you with a lopsided understanding of Allah's relationship to humanity?
?
This classification forces you to see that all topics in kalam fit into a rational order. Have you ever felt that some Islamic teachings are just 'scattered'? How does recognizing this internal logic change the way you approach learning about your creed?

Rationale for the Order of Stations
Author justifies the order of the six stations with logical and evidential arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific reasons for the sequence of the six stations, especially why accidents precede substances.

Core Idea
The order of stations is justified by logical dependencies and evidential relationships.

After listing the six stations, the author explains why they appear in this particular order. The reasoning involves both necessary dependencies (like preliminaries before everything) and evidential priorities (like using accidents to prove substance properties). This passage is key to appreciating the methodological sophistication of the book.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'والوجه في التقديم والتأخير'. Notice the chain of dependencies.
2
Trace the dependency chain
The author explains that preliminaries must come first, then universal matters, then accidents, substances, divinity, and transmitted matters. Follow the reasoning: transmitted matters depend on divinity, which depends on contingents, etc.
Also note the special reasoning for accidents before substances.
3
Understand the controversy about accidents/substances
The passage mentions that some scholars prefer substances first because accidents depend on substances. But the author's preferred order is accidents first for evidential reasons. Think about why evidence might take precedence over ontological dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكتاب مرتب على ستة مواقف وذلك لأن ما يذكر فيه إما أن يجب تقديمه في علم الكلام، وهو الموقف الأول في المقدمات أو لا يجب وحينئذ إما أن يبحث فيه عما لا يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة للموجود ، وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة، أو عما يختص فإما بالممكن الذي لا يقوم بنفسه بل بغيره، وهو الموقف الثالث في الأعراض، أو بالممكن الذي يقوم بنفسه وهو الموقف الرابع في الجواهر، وإما بالواجب تعالى فإما باعتبار إرساله الرسل وبعثه الأنبياء وهو الموقف السادس في السمعيات، أو لا باعتباره وهو الموقف الخامس في الإلهيات والوجه في التقديم والتأخير أن المقدمات يجب تقديمها على الكل، والأمور العامة كالمبادئ لما عداها، والسمعيات متوقفة على الإلهيات المتوقفة على مباحث الممكنات، وأما تقديم العرض على الجوهر فلأنه قد يستدل بأحوال الأعراض على أحوال الجواهر، كما يستدل بأحوال الحركة والسكون على حدوث الأجسام، وبقطع المسافة المتناهية في زمان متناه على عدم تركبها من الجواهر الأفراد التي لا تتناهى، ومنهم من قدم مباحث الجوهر نظراً إلى أن وجود العرض متوقف على وجوده . قوله: (ما يذكر فيه أي المقصود الذي يذكر فيه فلا يرد الخطبة، والمراد بالوجوب الوجوب الاستحساني وبالتقديم التقديم على كل ما عداه فلا يرد بعض المباحث الذي هو كالمبادي لبعض دون بعض، كالأمور العامة . قوله : ( في علم الكلام أي في تحصيله سواء كان جزءاً منه كمباحث النظر، أو لا كالرؤوس الثمانية التي هي مبادئ الشروع . قوله : ( وهو الموقف الثاني في الأمور العامة) أي هو المقصود من الموقف الثاني وإن ذكر بالاستطراد في هذا الموقف ما يختص بواحد من الأقسام الثلاثة كالوجوب والتقدم، ووجود القدم الصفات لا ينافي القول باختصاصه بالواجب على معنى عدم وجوده في الجوهر، والعرض، فإن الصفات ليست منهما على أنها ليست غير الذات وأيضاً قالوا: فالقدم الذاتي لا يوجد فيه أصلاً ، وقيل : المراد بعدم الاختصاص أن لا يختص مع مقابله كما أشير إليه في أول هذا الموقف، كما سيجيء زيادة بحث إن شاء الله تعالى
And the book is arranged into six stations. That is because what is mentioned in it either must be presented in the science of kalam – and that is the first station on preliminaries – or it need not be. In the latter case, either it investigates what is not specific to any of the three divisions of existence – and that is the second station on universal matters – or it investigates what is specific, either to the contingent that does not subsist by itself but by another – and that is the third station on accidents – or to the contingent that subsists by itself – and that is the fourth station on substances – or to the Necessary, exalted be He, either with consideration of His sending messengers and raising prophets – and that is the sixth station on transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) – or without that consideration – and that is the fifth station on divinity (ilahiyyat). The reason for the order is that preliminaries must be presented before everything, universal matters are like principles for what comes after them, and transmitted matters depend on divinity which depends on inquiries into contingents. As for presenting accident before substance, it is because the states of accidents can be used to infer the states of substances, as the states of motion and rest are used to infer the origination of bodies, and crossing a finite distance in finite time is used to infer that they are not composed of indivisible atoms that are infinite. And some people presented substance before accident because the existence of accident depends on the existence of substance. His saying: 'what is mentioned in it' means the intended content that is mentioned in it, so the sermon is not included. What is meant by 'must' is commendatory obligation (wujub istihsani), and by 'presentation' is presentation over all else, so it does not negate some topics that are like principles for some but not others, such as universal matters. His saying: 'in the science of kalam' means in its acquisition, whether it is part of it – like discussions of consideration (nazar) – or not – like the eight heads that are principles of commencement. His saying: 'and that is the second station on universal matters' means that it is the intended content of the second station, even if in this station what is specific to one of the three divisions – like necessity and priority – is mentioned by way of digression. The existence of the attribute of pre-eternity does not contradict the statement that it is specific to the Necessary in the sense that it does not exist in substance and accident, for attributes are not among them, and they are not other than the essence. Also, they said: essential pre-eternity does not occur in it at all. And it is said: the intended meaning of non-specificity is that it is not specific together with its opposite, as was indicated at the beginning of this station, as further discussion will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the overall structure of the book into six stations (mawaqif). The classification is based on whether a topic must be presented first in kalam (preliminaries), and then on whether it deals with universal matters or with specific existents. Specific existents are divided into accidents (contingent non-self-subsisting), substances (contingent self-subsisting), and the Necessary (God). The Necessary is then discussed in terms of divinity (ilahiyyat) and prophethood (sam'iyyat). The order is justified: preliminaries first, then universal matters as principles, then accidents before substances because accidents can be used as evidence for substances (e.g., motion and rest indicate bodily origination), then divinity, then transmitted matters. The following commentary clarifies that 'must' means commendatory obligation, that 'science of kalam' includes both its parts and prerequisites, and that the second station (universal matters) may include specific topics by digression.

Key Terms
التقديم
التأخير
المبادئ
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of stations is purely conventional or based on author's preference.
Correction: The author provides explicit logical and evidential reasons for the sequence, showing it is methodologically grounded.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim accidents should be discussed before substances?
A) Because accidents are more important
B) Because accidents are easier to understand
C) Because accidents provide evidence for substances
D) Because accidents depend on substances

Key Takeaway
The order of stations is justified by a combination of necessary dependencies and evidential priorities, with accidents placed before substances for their probative value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach learning Islamic theology by paying attention to the author's reasoning for the order of topics, or do you merely accept the structure without thought? Examine your own method of study: are you following a logical sequence because you understand why, or just because it's the way it's taught? Today, commit to understanding the 'why' behind every subject order you tackle.
?
The author prioritizes evidential power over existential dependence by placing accidents before substances. In your own life, do you evaluate what is most important based on what gives you the strongest evidence of truth, or based on what seems to depend on what? Where have you been assuming a priority that is weak, only to realize you needed to reorder? Confess one reordered priority now and act on it.
?
The passage shows scholarly difference about the order. Are you open to revisiting your deeply held assumptions when you see that even scholars had valid disagreements? Or do you cling to a single order because it's comfortable? This lesson challenges you to appreciate the reasoning behind different positions. Write down one assumption you hold about Islamic learning that you might need to re-evaluate based on logical evidence, not just authority.
