The Wherewithal and the Subject of a Science
Refutation of al-Taftazani on the role of the wherewithal in defining a science's subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator of al-Mawaqif insists that the 'wherewithal' in the definition of a science's subject must have a role in the occurrence of its essential attributes, and how this relates to the principle that sciences are distinguished by subjects rather than predicates.

Core Idea
The wherewithal must affect occurrence, or the subject's attributes become foreign.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, the commentator responds to al-Taftazani's interpretation of the phrase 'known from wherewith such-and-such' in defining a science's subject. Taftazani held that the wherewithal only qualifies the investigation, not the actual occurrence of attributes. The commentator refutes this, arguing that such a view makes the attributes foreign to the subject. He then reinforces his argument by stating the general rule that sciences are distinguished by their subjects, not predicates, and responds to Taftazani's objection. This lesson unpacks that intricate debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: initial refutation, general principle, objection, and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two key arguments
First, the author insists the wherewithal must affect occurrence. Second, he argues sciences are distinguished by subjects. See how these connect.
The first argument is the main point; the second supports it.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Al-Taftazani's objection and the author's rebuttal are crucial. Understand why the author says the definition fails without role in occurrence.
The author's final sentence emphasizes that many overlook this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضوع، وفي قوله : وإن كان بحث المتكلم عن قدرته تعالى لإثبات عقيدة دينية إلخ، رد على العلامة التفتازاني حيث ذكر في التلويح أن قولنا من حيث كذا يجوز أن يتعلق بالبحث المذكور تضمنا في ضمن لفظ الموضوع، فحينئذ يجب أن يلاحظ الحيثية في البحث عن أحواله ولا يجب أن يكون له مدخل في العروض ووجه الرد أنه لا بد من المدخلية لئلا تصير أعراضاً غريبة، فتدبر . العلوم بحسب تمايز الموضوعات لا بالمحمولات، لأن المحمول لو جعل وجه التمايز بأن يكون البحث عن بعض الأحوال الذاتية علماً، وعن بعض آخر علماً آخر لم ينضبط أمر الاختلاف، والاتحاد ويكون كل علم علوماً جمةً ضرورة اشتماله على أنواع جمة من الأعراض كما ذكره في شرح المقاصد ، لأن أنواع الأعراض الذاتية إذا كانت داخلة تحت أمر جامع يحصل له الانضباط، بل لأن المحمولات صفات مطلوبة لذوات الموضوعات، فالأنسب أن تجعل جهة الوحدة قيد الموضوع على أن المقصود من اعتبار كل طائفة علماً على حدة، هو تسهيل أمر التعليم ولا نزاع في أن السهولة في جانب الموضوع أظهر منها في جانب المحمول فإن قلت : قد أجاب المحقق التفتازاني في التلويح الاعتراض عن السؤال المذكور بأن الموضوع لما كان عبارة عن المبحوث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية قيد بالحيثية على معنى أن البحث عن العوارض، يكون باعتبار الحيثية وبالنظر إليها أي يلاحظ في جميع المباحث هذا المعنى الكلي لا على معنى أن جميع العوارض المبحوث عنها لحوقها بهذه الحيثية إليه، وتلخيصه أن لفظ الموضوع يتضمن معنى فعلى البحث والعروض فالجار في قولهم موضوع الكلام المعلوم من حيث كذا متعلق بلفظ الموضوع باعتبار جزء معناه أعني البحث لا باعتبار الجزء الآخر أعني العروض حتى يلزم أن يكون للحيثية مدخل في عروض العوارض فلم لم يلتفت إليه الشارح قلت : لأن الحيثية إذا من تتمة الموضوع ولم يكن لها مدخل في عروض العوارض ، لم يصدق تعريف مطلق الموضوع على العلم المذكور، إذ لا يصدق حينئذ على الموضوع المقيد بالحيثية أنه يبحث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية إذ الأعراض على تقدير أن لا يكون للحيثية مدخل في العروض، ليست لذلك المقيد بل للمطلق، وهذا ظاهر وإن غفل عنه كثير من الناس.
Regarding the subject, and in his statement: 'And if the mutakallim's investigation of His power is for establishing a religious creed, etc.', this is a refutation of al-Allama al-Taftazani, for he mentioned in al-Talwih that our statement 'from wherewith such-and-such' is permissible to be attached to the mentioned investigation implicitly within the word 'subject'. So it would then be necessary to observe the wherewithal in the investigation of its states, and it is not necessary that it have a role in the occurrence. The refutation is that it must have a role, lest the attributes become foreign. So contemplate. Sciences are distinguished by their subjects, not by their predicates, because if the predicate were made the basis of distinction such that the investigation of some essential states becomes one science and of other states another science, then the matter of difference and unity would not be regulated, and each science would become many sciences necessarily due to its containing many types of accidents as mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid. Because the types of essential accidents, if they fall under a comprehensive principle, discipline is achieved. Rather, because predicates are attributes sought for the subjects themselves, it is more appropriate to make the cause of unity a restriction on the subject, given that the purpose of considering each group as an independent science is to facilitate teaching, and there is no dispute that ease is more apparent on the side of the subject than on the side of the predicate. If you say: al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani in al-Talwih answered the objection to the mentioned question by saying that since the subject is an expression for that which is investigated in the science regarding its essential accidents, it is restricted by the wherewithal in the sense that the investigation of the accidents is by virtue of the wherewithal and in consideration of it, i.e., this universal meaning is observed in all investigations, not that all the investigated accidents belong to this wherewithal. His summary is that the word 'subject' includes the meaning of both investigation and occurrence. So the particle in their statement 'subject of kalam known from wherewith such-and-such' is attached to the word 'subject' by virtue of the part of its meaning, namely investigation, not by virtue of the other part, namely occurrence, such that the wherewithal would have a role in the occurrence of the accidents. So why did the commentator not pay attention to him? I say: Because if the wherewithal is a completion of the subject and has no role in the occurrence of the accidents, then the definition of the absolute subject would not apply to the mentioned science. For then it would not be true regarding the subject restricted by the wherewithal that in this science its essential accidents are investigated, since the accidents, on the assumption that the wherewithal has no role in the occurrence, belong not to that restricted subject but to the absolute. This is apparent, though many are heedless of it.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative response to al-Taftazani's view on the role of the 'wherewithal' (al-haythiyya) in the definition of a science's subject. The author begins by stating that the phrase 'from wherewith such-and-such' in the definition of the subject of kalam must have a role in the occurrence ('urud) of the accidental attributes; otherwise, the attributes would be foreign (ghariba) to the subject. He then discusses the general principle that sciences are distinguished by their subjects, not predicates, because using predicates leads to uncontrolled multiplication of sciences. The predicates are attributes sought for the subjects, so it is better to make the unity condition on the subject for ease of teaching. He then presents al-Taftazani's counter-argument that the wherewithal is attached only to the investigation part of the word 'subject', not to the occurrence part, so it does not need to affect occurrence. The author refutes this by arguing that if the wherewithal is part of the subject but does not affect occurrence, then the restricted subject does not truly have its essential accidents investigated; the accidents would belong to the absolute subject, not the restricted one. Hence the definition would fail. This is a technical discussion in the methodology of defining sciences.

Misconception: The wherewithal is merely a qualifier for the investigation and does not need to affect the occurrence of attributes.
Correction: The author argues that without the wherewithal's role in occurrence, the attributes would be foreign to the subject, breaking the definition of a science.

Checkpoint
Why must the wherewithal have a role in the occurrence of the accidents according to the author?
A) Otherwise the accidents would be foreign to the subject
B) Because al-Taftazani said so
C) To make the science easier to teach

Key Takeaway
The wherewithal in the definition of a subject must have a role in the occurrence of its essential accidents, otherwise the definition fails.

Is there an attribute of your own soul—like patience or sincerity—that you have described in a way that separates it from its proper subject? How will you reintegrate them in your self-accountability today?

Scope of Kalam: Early vs. Late Mutakallimun
Commentator distinguishes early and late mutakallimun and critiques justifications for including substances and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator distinguishes between early and late mutakallimun regarding the subject of kalam and why certain topics are included or excluded.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam depends on the school: early or late mutakallimun.

In this passage, the commentator responds to an objection about kalam investigating substances and accidents. He explains that this objection only applies if the subject is God's essence as per later mutakallimun, not for earlier ones. He then examines and dismisses attempts to justify such investigations as digression or citation, and offers a deeper purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct parts: the distinction between early/late mutakallimun and the discussion of justifications.
2
Identify the two stances
The commentator states that the objection applies only if the subject is God's essence (as for later mutakallimun). For early mutakallimun, it does not. Find where this distinction is made.
3
Understand the rejection of justifications
The commentator dismisses digression (istitrad) and citation (hikaya) as valid reasons for including substances and accidents. He argues that many kalam investigations are essential for establishing beliefs, so they are not mere digressions.
Also note the comment about personal union (al-ittihad bi al-shakhs) being arbitrary.
4
Reflect on the alternative purpose
The commentator suggests that the ultimate purpose of all kalam investigations is to attain knowledge of God's perfection. This can include studying substances and accidents as they reveal divine wisdom.
This ties back to the objection: if the purpose is knowledge of God, then discussing creation is relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإنه قد يبحث فيه إلخ) هذا وارد على تقدير أن ذاته تعالى موضوع لكلام المتأخرين، وأما على قوله أنه موضوع لكلام المتقدمين فلا إذ لا يبحث فيه عن الجواهر والأعراض بل عما سوى ذات الله وصفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه . قوله : (لا يقال ذلك إلى آخره لم يتعرض لجواز أن يكون البحث عنهما على سبيل الاستطراد تكميلاً للصناعة بأن يذكر مع المطلوب ماله نوع تعلق به من الفروع، واللواحق والمتقابلات أو أن يكون البحث على سبيل الحكاية لكلام المخالف لأن كثيراً المباحث مما يستعان بها في إثبات العقائد فلا وجه لجعلها استطرادية، وليس البحث عنها على سبيل الحكاية أيضاً. من تلك عين التعليم لكن حمله على الاتحاد بالشخص لا يخلو عن تعسف لقيام كل منهما بموضوع على حدة فتأمل. قوله : (لا) من حيث حيث هي مستندة إليه) قد يمنع ذلك بناءً على أن المقصود من جميع مباحثها الاطلاع على كمال الصانع حسب ما يبلغه طوق البشر على الوجه الأتم الأوفر.
His statement: 'For it investigates in it, etc.' This applies on the assumption that His essence is the subject of the later mutakallimun. As for his statement that it is the subject for the earlier mutakallimun, then no, because they investigate not substances and accidents but rather what is other than God's essence, attributes, actions, and judgments. His statement: 'It cannot be said, etc.' He did not address the possibility that the investigation of them might be by way of digression (istitrad) as a completion of the craft, by mentioning with the desired object what has a type of connection with it of branches, corollaries, and opposites, or that the investigation might be by way of citation (hikaya) of the opponent's words, because many investigations are used in establishing beliefs, so there is no basis for making them digressive, and the investigation of them is not by way of citation either. From those, the same teaching, but carrying it upon personal union is not free from arbitrariness because each stands on an independent subject, so reflect. His statement: 'Not from where they are dependent upon Him' may be prevented based on the fact that the purpose of all its investigations is to attain knowledge of the perfection of the Maker according to the extent of human capacity in the most complete and abundant manner.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage contains two commentator's notes. The first note clarifies that the previous objection (about investigating substances and accidents) applies only if the subject of kalam is God's essence as per the later mutakallimun. For the earlier mutakallimun, the subject is different, so the objection does not apply. The second note addresses the possibility that such topics might be included in kalam by way of digression (istitrad) or citation (hikaya) of opponents' views. The commentator argues that these are not genuine justifications because many kalam investigations are used to establish beliefs, so they are not mere digressions. He also mentions a point about personal union (al-ittihad bi al-shakhs) but cautions against it. Finally, he notes an alternative viewpoint that the ultimate purpose of all kalam investigations is to attain knowledge of God's perfection, which could include discussion of substances and accidents as they reveal divine wisdom. This commentary helps contextualize the arguments.

Why a Science Cannot Prove Its Own Subject's Existence
Al-Jurjani proves that the subject's existence is not proven within its own science, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of a science's subject cannot be proven within that science itself, and how the objection and its resolution rely on careful grammatical interpretation.

Core Idea
A science proves the accidents of its subject, not the subject's existence itself.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a foundational methodological rule: the subject of a science is not proven within that science. Al-Jurjani provides a rigorous proof, considers an objection, and resolves it by clarifying the meaning of dependence. Understanding this rule helps you grasp the structure of Islamic sciences and their interrelations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without fully analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage contains a proof, an objection, and an answer. Notice the logical flow.
2
Identify the rule and its proof
Find the statement of the rule and the logical reason: establishing essential accidents depends on the subject's existence, so existence cannot itself be an accident proven in that science.
The proof hinges on avoiding circularity.
3
Consider the objection
The objection states that establishing existence itself is not problematic because it does not depend on subject existence in the same way other accidents do.
The objector thinks the dependence applies only to non-existential accidents.
4
Follow the answer's two parts
First, the answer references external clarification in other sciences. Second, it provides a detailed grammatical analysis of the pronoun in 'it depends' to show the objection's flaw.
The key is whether the pronoun refers to 'establishing existence' or 'accidentality of existence'.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the two different interpretations of the pronoun lead to different conclusions. The correct interpretation (accidentality) blocks the objection.
This highlights the precision needed in kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني : أن موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه وجوده ) وذلك لأن المطلوب المبين في العلم إثبات الأعراض الذاتية لموضوعه، ولا شك أنه متوقف على وجوده فلا يكون وجوده عرضاً ذاتياً مبيناً فيه، وإلا لزم توقفه على نفسه واعترض عليه بأن إثبات العرض الذاتي الذي هو غير الوجود متوقف عليه، وأما إثباته فلا محذور فيه أصلاً، وأجيب : بأن قوله : (قد تبين إلخ ) للإطباق على أن علم الأصول يستمد من العربية، ويبين فيها بعض مباديه وتفصيل ذلك على ما سيجيء في الشفاء أن مبادئ العلم قد تكون بينة بنفسها، وقد تكون غير بينة فتبين في علم أعلى لعلو شأنه عن أن تتبين في ذلك العلم، كقولنا : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة، أو في علم أدنى لدنو شأنه عن أن تتبين في ذلك العلم، كمسألة امتناع الجزء ، وقد تتبين في ذلك العلم بشرط أن لا يكون مبدأ لجميع مسائله ليكون مسألة من وجه ومبدأ من وجه . قوله : ( ولا شك أنه متوقف إلخ ) الظاهر أن الضمير في أنه راجع إلى الإثبات، فاللازم على ذلك التقدير أن يكون إثبات الوجود للموضوع موقوفاً على وجوده في نفسه، وليس فيه توقف الشيء على نفسه بل الواقع كذلك فإن إثبات شيء لشيء، أي بيان ثبوته موقوف على ثبوته في نفسه، فلا يتم التقريب إلا بتقدير المضاف أي على إثبات وجوده لأن الهلية المركبة بعد الهلية البسيطة فإنه ما لم يعلم وجود شيء لا يطلب ثبوت شيء له، وعلى هذا ورود الاعتراض ظاهر لأن إثبات ما سوى الوجود موقوف على إثبات الوجود، ولو جعل الضمير راجعاً إلى العروض المستفاد من قوله الأعراض الذاتية، ولا شك أن عروض شيء لشيء موقوف على وجود المعروض في ظرف العروض، إذ اللاشيء لا يكون معروضاً لشيء في ذلك الظرف، فلو كان الوجود عرضاً ذاتياً له لكان عارضاً له ضرورة أن العرض الذاتي ما يلحق الشيء لذاته، أو لما يساويه فيكون موقوفاً على وجوده في نفسه فيلزم توقف الشيء على نفسه، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يرد الاعتراض المذكور كما لا يخفى .
(The second: that the subject of a science is not proven in it its existence) That is because what is sought to be clarified in a science is the establishment of the essential accidents of its subject, and there is no doubt that it depends on its existence, so its existence is not an essential accident clarified in it; otherwise, it would entail its dependence on itself. And it was objected that the establishment of an essential accident which is not existence depends on it, but as for establishing it, there is no difficulty in it at all. And it was answered: by his statement: (قد تبين etc.) to indicate that the science of principles derives from Arabic, and some of its principles are clarified in it. The detail of that is as will come in al-Shifa: that the principles of a science may be self-evident, and may be non-evident, so they are clarified in a higher science due to its loftiness from being clarified in that science, like our statement: the body is composed of hyle and form, or in a lower science due to its lowliness from being clarified in that science, like the issue of the indivisible part, and they may be clarified in that science on condition that it is not a principle for all its problems so that it is a problem from one aspect and a principle from one aspect. His statement: (ولا شك أنه متوقف etc.) Apparently, the pronoun in 'that it' refers to the establishment, so the necessary on that estimation is that establishing existence for the subject depends on its existence in itself, and there is no dependence of the thing on itself, rather the reality is such. For establishing something for something, i.e., clarifying its affirmation, depends on its being affirmed in itself. So the approximation is not completed except by estimating an annexed word, i.e., on establishing its existence, because the composite predication is after the simple predication; for as long as the existence of something is not known, the affirmation of something for it is not sought. On this, the occurrence of the objection is apparent, because establishing other than existence depends on establishing existence. And if the pronoun is made to refer to the accidentally derived from his statement 'the essential accidents', and there is no doubt that the accidentally of something for something depends on the existence of the subject in the locus of occurrence, since nothing cannot be a subject for something in that locus. So if existence were an essential accident for it, it would be an accident for it necessarily because an essential accident is what attaches to a thing by itself or to what equals it, so it would depend on its existence in itself, leading to the dependence of the thing on itself. On this estimation, the aforementioned objection does not arise, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection-answer unit from the commentary on Al-Mawaqif. It begins by stating the second rule: the subject of a science is not proven within that science itself. The proof: a science aims to establish the essential accidents of its subject. Establishing accidents presupposes the existence of the subject, so existence itself cannot be an essential accident proven in that science—otherwise circularity would result. An objection is raised: establishing a non-existential essential accident depends on the subject's existence, but establishing existence itself does not face this problem. The answer is given in two parts. First, by quoting the original text (قد تبين إلخ), the commentator notes that principles of a science may be clarified in other sciences (higher or lower) or within the same science if they are not a principle for all its problems. Second, the commentator clarifies the ambiguity of the pronoun in 'it depends.' If the pronoun refers to 'the establishment of existence,' then the proof requires that establishing existence depends on the existence of the subject, which is actually true (composite predication after simple predication); thus the objection stands. But if the pronoun refers to 'the accidentally of existence' (derived from 'essential accidents'), then accidentally depends on the existence of the subject—so if existence were an essential accident, it would depend on its own existence, leading to circularity. On this interpretation, the objection does not apply. The passage thus defends the rule by distinguishing two senses of dependence.

Key Terms
موضوع العلم
الأعراض الذاتية
إثبات الوجود
الهلية المركبة
الهلية البسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully refutes the rule that the subject's existence cannot be proven in its own science.
Correction: The objection is resolved by clarifying that the dependence in question is about accidentally, not about establishing existence, thus the rule stands.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given that the existence of a subject cannot be proven in its own science?
A) Because existence is self-evident and needs no proof.
B) Because establishing essential accidents depends on the subject's existence, leading to circularity if existence itself were an accident.
C) Because sciences only deal with accidents, not existence.
D) Because the subject is always known from a higher science.

Key Takeaway
The subject's existence is presupposed, not proven, in its own science; attempts to prove it would lead to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, did you ever assume that the foundations of your knowledge (like the existence of God or of the soul) could be proven within the same field that studies them? Are you willing to accept that some truths must be presupposed before entering a science?
?
The objection in this passage arises from a subtle ambiguity in language. When you read the Qur'an or the words of scholars, how often do you pause to clarify the referent of a pronoun before drawing a conclusion? Will you commit to reading more carefully from now on?
?
This rule reminds us that every science rests on premises it does not itself prove. What is the ultimate foundation of your certainty? Have you actually established it, or are you relying on unexamined presuppositions? Will you investigate that foundation today?

Absolute and Particular Existence
Al-Jurjani defines absolute and particular existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute and particular existence and why absolute existence cannot be an essential accident.

Core Idea
Absolute existence is shared; particular existence is unique and cannot be predicated.

Nearly every theological argument involves existence. Al-Jurjani begins by clarifying two fundamental senses of existence: the absolute, common to all things, and the particular, unique to each. This distinction is critical for understanding how existence can be discussed in proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of existence
Read the sentence and note how the author differentiates 'absolute existence' from 'particular existence'.
2
Understand why absolute existence is not an essential accident
The author states it is common to all existents, so it cannot be an accident of a specific one.
An accident must be particular to its subject.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'true particular'
A true particular cannot be predicated of anything else—consider why this is important for theological method.

الوجود المطلق مشترك بين الموجودات بأسرها، فلا يكون عرضاً ذاتياً لشيء منها، وأما الوجود الخاص بواحد منها فهو جزئي حقيقي، لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً، وربما يقال : لما امتاز الوجود عما عداه من الأعراض الذاتية بتوقفها عليه لم يستحسنوا أن يجعل معها في قرن فيطلب إثباته مع إثباتها في علم واحد ( فيلزم ) إذا كان موضوع الكلام ذاته تعالى ( أما كون إثبات الصانع بينا بذاته فلا يحتاج إلى بيان أصلا ( أو كونه مبيناً في علم أعلى ) سواءً كان شرعياً، أو لا فإن بيان وجود الموضوع إنما يجوز المقيد بالوجوب، وأما ثانياً فلانه يستلزم أن لا يكون وجود شيء من الموجودات مسألة في شيء من العلوم، فلا يصح قولهم أن موضوع العلم إنما يبين وجوده في علم أعلى منه، وأما ثالثا فلان قولهم موضوع العلم لا يبين وجوده فيه بعد تقرير أنه لا يثبت في العلم سوى الأعراض الذاتية يكون لغوا من الكلام والجواب عن الأول إن اعتبار التقييد بالوجوب في قولنا: إن الواجب موجود بوجود يجب له لغو كذا تقييد الجوهر موجود بالوجود الجوهري، والعرض موجود بالوجود العرضي إلى غير ذلك، وأيضاً المبين إنما هو الوجود مطلقاً لا المقيد بالوجوب، وإن كان متحققاً في ضمنه وعن الثاني إن وجود الأخص إنما بين في الأعم بانقسامه إليه، وإلى غيره والانقسام من الأعراض الذاتيه للأعم كما سيجيء وعن الثالث أن التنصيص على الحكم الجزئي بعد بيان الحكم الكلي إذا كان خفاء في كونه جزئياً له لا يكون لغواً . قوله : ( لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً) أما بالمواطأة فلما ذكر الشارح رحمه الله في تصانيفه أن الجزئي الحقيقي متأصل في الوجود لا ينتزع من شيء حتى يحمل عليه، وتفصيله في حواشينا على شرح الرسالة الشمسية، وأما بالاشتقاق فإن صاحب العرض الجزئي جزئي حقيقي لامتناع تشخص العارض بدون تشخص معروضه فاندفع ما قيل : إن المعتبر في حمل الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ويجوز أن يقال زيد صاحب هذا البياض. قوله : ( وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ ) هذا بالحقيقة عدول عن الوجه الأول، واستدلال بوجه آخر على عدم جواز البحث عن وجود موضوع علم في ذلك العلم ومثله مقبول في صناعة المناظرة لكن فيه نظر وهو أنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الوجود المطلق عرضاً ذاتياً لشيء من الموجودات وعدم صحة حمل الوجود الخاص عدم كون الوجود مطلقا من الأعراض الذاتية لشيء منها لجواز أن يكون الوجود مقيداً بالوجوب من الأعراض الذاتية للواجب، ويصح علية لكونه كلياً على أن الاتحاد في الوجود، إذا كفى في الحمل كما تدل عليه كلماتهم، لزم صحة حمل الجزئي الحقيقي على شيء كما يصح أن يحمل عليه، لأن الاتحاد من من الطرفين فكما جاز زيد إنسان فليجز عكسه أيضاً سلمنا أن الجزئي الحقيقي لا يحمل على شيء مواطأة، لكن الحمل في الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ولا مانع من أن يقال : زيد صاحب هذا الوجود فتأمل.
Absolute existence is common among all existents, so it is not an essential accident for any of them. As for particular existence belonging to one of them, it is a true particular, not predicated of anything at all. And perhaps it is said: Since existence is distinguished from other essential accidents by their dependence on it, they did not deem it proper to place it with them in the same rank and to seek its proof alongside their proof in one science. Therefore, if the subject of kalam is His essence, may He be exalted, either the proof of the Maker is self-evident and needs no clarification at all, or it is clarified in a higher science, whether revealed or not. For clarifying the existence of the subject is only permissible with existence qualified by necessity. Second, it entails that the existence of no existent would be a problem in any science, so their statement that the subject of a science is only clarified in a higher science would be invalid. Third, their statement that the subject of a science is not clarified in it, after establishing that only essential accidents are established in a science, becomes superfluous. The answer to the first: that considering the qualification of necessity in our statement 'the Necessary is existent with an existence due to it' is superfluous, similarly the qualification 'the substance is existent with substantial existence,' and 'the accident is existent with accidental existence,' and so on. Moreover, what is clarified is absolute existence, not existence qualified by necessity, even if it is realized within it. And to the second: the existence of the more specific is clarified in the more general by its division into it and into something else, and division is among the essential accidents of the more general, as will come. And to the third: specifying the particular judgment after clarifying the universal judgment, when there is obscurity in its being a particular of it, is not superfluous. His statement: (لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً) As for by synonymy (muwaṭa'ah), because the commentator, may God have mercy on him, mentioned in his works that the true particular is rooted in existence and is not abstracted from anything to be predicated of it; its detail is in our glosses on the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. As for by derivation (ishtiqaq), the possessor of a particular accident is a true particular because the individuation of the accident without the individuation of its subject is impossible. Thus what was said—that what is considered in the predication of essential accidents is predication by derivation, and it is permissible to say 'Zayd is the possessor of this whiteness'—is refuted. His statement: (وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ) This is in reality a deviation from the first method and an argument by another method for the impermissibility of investigating the existence of the subject of a science in that science. Its like is accepted in the art of dialectic, but there is a consideration: it does not follow from the fact that absolute existence is not an essential accident for any of the existents and the impropriety of predicating particular existence that absolute existence is not among the essential accidents for some of them, because it is possible that existence qualified by necessity is an essential accident for the Necessary, and it is valid as a predicate because it is universal. Moreover, if union in existence suffices for predication, as their expressions indicate, then the validity of predicating a true particular of something would follow, just as it is valid to predicate of it, because the union is from both sides; just as 'Zayd is a human' is permissible, so also its converse should be permissible. We grant that a true particular is not predicated of anything by synonymy, but the predication of essential accidents is by derivation, and there is no obstacle to saying 'Zayd is the possessor of this existence.' So reflect.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a further discussion of the rule that a subject's existence is not proven in its science, specifically addressing the case of kalam (theology) whose subject is God's essence. The commentator begins by distinguishing absolute existence (common to all existents) from particular existence (unique to one thing). Absolute existence cannot be an essential accident because it is shared; particular existence is a true particular and cannot be predicated at all. A possible objection is then presented: since other essential accidents depend on existence, scholars did not include existence among them, so if the subject of kalam is God, either His existence is self-evident or proven in a higher science (revelation). This leads to three further objections: (1) only existence qualified by necessity is considered; (2) no existence would be a problem in any science; (3) stating that the subject is not proven in its science becomes redundant. The answers follow: (1) the qualification of existence by necessity is itself redundant because existence is absolute; (2) the existence of a specific thing is clarified in the more general science by division; (3) specifying a particular judgment after a universal one is not redundant if the particular is not obvious. The commentator then explains why a true particular cannot be predicated (by synonymy or derivation) and addresses an alternative answer that absolute existence is not an essential accident. He critiques this answer: existence qualified by necessity could be an essential accident for the Necessary, and if union in existence suffices for predication, then true particulars could be predicated—but they are not, so the argument needs refinement. He concludes that the predication of essential accidents is by derivation, so 'Zayd is the possessor of this existence' is acceptable.

Key Terms
الوجود المطلق
الوجود الخاص
عرض ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is absolute existence not an essential accident?
A) Because it is self-evident.
B) Because it is common to all existents.
C) Because it is a particular.
D) Because it is not a predicate.

Key Takeaway
Absolute existence is universal and thus cannot be an essential accident; particular existence is unique and cannot be predicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since your particular existence is unique and cannot be transferred to anyone else, what does that demand from you in how you spend each moment of this life? Are you living as if your existence is replaceable?
?
If absolute existence is shared but your particular existence is yours alone, then your accountability before Allah is also uniquely yours. What specific sin have you been hiding behind the excuse that 'everyone does it'? How does this lesson force you to own your actions?
?
The passage states that particular existence cannot be predicated of anything else. Your existence is not a label that can be applied to any other. How should this reality reshape your intention (niyyah) in every deed you perform today?

Objections to Proving Existence in Kalam and Their Answers
Al-Jurjani presents three objections and their answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against proving God's existence in kalam and how al-Jurjani answers them.

Core Idea
Proving existence in a science is permissible if done through essential accidents, but existence itself is not an accident.

Having established that existence is not an essential accident, a problem arises: if kalam's subject is God, how can it prove His existence? The objector raises three points, each questioning the possibility. Al-Jurjani responds systematically, showing that existence can still be established through division and specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Follow the three sub-objections and identify the core concern about proving existence in the same science.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer shows that qualification by necessity is redundant; what is clarified is absolute existence.
3
Understand the second answer
The existence of a specific thing is clarified in a higher science by division into it and something else.
Division is an essential accident of the more general.
4
Consider the third answer
Even if the universal judgment is known, specifying a particular instance is not superfluous if it is not obvious.

Predication of True Particulars and the Alternative Argument
Al-Jurjani explains predication of true particulars and critiques an alternative argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a true particular cannot be predicated by synonymy but can by derivation, and how this applies to the alternative argument about absolute existence.

Core Idea
True particulars cannot be predicated directly, but their possessors can be described via derivation.

After the main answers, al-Jurjani delves deeper into the nature of true particulars and predication. He explains why a true particular like 'this existence' cannot be predicated by synonymy (it is not abstracted) but can be predicated by derivation ('possessor of this existence'). He then critiques an alternative answer that claimed absolute existence is not an essential accident, showing its flaws.

Guided Path
1
Understand the commentary on true particular
Read why a true particular cannot be predicated by synonymy—it is not abstracted from anything.
2
Learn about derivation
Predication by derivation is possible: 'Zayd is the possessor of this existence'.
3
Analyze the alternative argument
The alternative claims absolute existence is not an essential accident, but al-Jurjani shows that existence qualified by necessity could be an accident, and if union suffices for predication, then true particulars could be predicated—so the argument fails.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Jurjani says 'so reflect,' indicating the student should think carefully about these distinctions.

وعن الثاني إن وجود الأخص إنما بين في الأعم بانقسامه إليه، وإلى غيره والانقسام من الأعراض الذاتيه للأعم كما سيجيء وعن الثالث أن التنصيص على الحكم الجزئي بعد بيان الحكم الكلي إذا كان خفاء في كونه جزئياً له لا يكون لغواً . قوله : ( لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً) أما بالمواطأة فلما ذكر الشارح رحمه الله في تصانيفه أن الجزئي الحقيقي متأصل في الوجود لا ينتزع من شيء حتى يحمل عليه، وتفصيله في حواشينا على شرح الرسالة الشمسية، وأما بالاشتقاق فإن صاحب العرض الجزئي جزئي حقيقي لامتناع تشخص العارض بدون تشخص معروضه فاندفع ما قيل : إن المعتبر في حمل الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ويجوز أن يقال زيد صاحب هذا البياض. قوله : ( وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ ) هذا بالحقيقة عدول عن الوجه الأول، واستدلال بوجه آخر على عدم جواز البحث عن وجود موضوع علم في ذلك العلم ومثله مقبول في صناعة المناظرة لكن فيه نظر وهو أنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الوجود المطلق عرضاً ذاتياً لشيء من الموجودات وعدم صحة حمل الوجود الخاص عدم كون الوجود مطلقا من الأعراض الذاتية لشيء منها لجواز أن يكون الوجود مقيداً بالوجوب من الأعراض الذاتية للواجب، ويصح علية لكونه كلياً على أن الاتحاد في الوجود، إذا كفى في الحمل كما تدل عليه كلماتهم، لزم صحة حمل الجزئي الحقيقي على شيء كما يصح أن يحمل عليه، لأن الاتحاد من من الطرفين فكما جاز زيد إنسان فليجز عكسه أيضاً سلمنا أن الجزئي الحقيقي لا يحمل على شيء مواطأة، لكن الحمل في الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ولا مانع من أن يقال : زيد صاحب هذا الوجود فتأمل.
Absolute existence is common among all existents, so it is not an essential accident for any of them. As for particular existence belonging to one of them, it is a true particular, not predicated of anything at all. And perhaps it is said: Since existence is distinguished from other essential accidents by their dependence on it, they did not deem it proper to place it with them in the same rank and to seek its proof alongside their proof in one science. Therefore, if the subject of kalam is His essence, may He be exalted, either the proof of the Maker is self-evident and needs no clarification at all, or it is clarified in a higher science, whether revealed or not. For clarifying the existence of the subject is only permissible with existence qualified by necessity. Second, it entails that the existence of no existent would be a problem in any science, so their statement that the subject of a science is only clarified in a higher science would be invalid. Third, their statement that the subject of a science is not clarified in it, after establishing that only essential accidents are established in a science, becomes superfluous. The answer to the first: that considering the qualification of necessity in our statement 'the Necessary is existent with an existence due to it' is superfluous, similarly the qualification 'the substance is existent with substantial existence,' and 'the accident is existent with accidental existence,' and so on. Moreover, what is clarified is absolute existence, not existence qualified by necessity, even if it is realized within it. And to the second: the existence of the more specific is clarified in the more general by its division into it and into something else, and division is among the essential accidents of the more general, as will come. And to the third: specifying the particular judgment after clarifying the universal judgment, when there is obscurity in its being a particular of it, is not superfluous. His statement: (لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً) As for by synonymy (muwaṭa'ah), because the commentator, may God have mercy on him, mentioned in his works that the true particular is rooted in existence and is not abstracted from anything to be predicated of it; its detail is in our glosses on the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. As for by derivation (ishtiqaq), the possessor of a particular accident is a true particular because the individuation of the accident without the individuation of its subject is impossible. Thus what was said—that what is considered in the predication of essential accidents is predication by derivation, and it is permissible to say 'Zayd is the possessor of this whiteness'—is refuted. His statement: (وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ) This is in reality a deviation from the first method and an argument by another method for the impermissibility of investigating the existence of the subject of a science in that science. Its like is accepted in the art of dialectic, but there is a consideration: it does not follow from the fact that absolute existence is not an essential accident for any of the existents and the impropriety of predicating particular existence that absolute existence is not among the essential accidents for some of them, because it is possible that existence qualified by necessity is an essential accident for the Necessary, and it is valid as a predicate because it is universal. Moreover, if union in existence suffices for predication, as their expressions indicate, then the validity of predicating a true particular of something would follow, just as it is valid to predicate of it, because the union is from both sides; just as 'Zayd is a human' is permissible, so also its converse should be permissible. We grant that a true particular is not predicated of anything by synonymy, but the predication of essential accidents is by derivation, and there is no obstacle to saying 'Zayd is the possessor of this existence.' So reflect.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a further discussion of the rule that a subject's existence is not proven in its science, specifically addressing the case of kalam (theology) whose subject is God's essence. The commentator begins by distinguishing absolute existence (common to all existents) from particular existence (unique to one thing). Absolute existence cannot be an essential accident because it is shared; particular existence is a true particular and cannot be predicated at all. A possible objection is then presented: since other essential accidents depend on existence, scholars did not include existence among them, so if the subject of kalam is God, either His existence is self-evident or proven in a higher science (revelation). This leads to three further objections: (1) only existence qualified by necessity is considered; (2) no existence would be a problem in any science; (3) stating that the subject is not proven in its science becomes redundant. The answers follow: (1) the qualification of existence by necessity is itself redundant because existence is absolute; (2) the existence of a specific thing is clarified in the more general science by division; (3) specifying a particular judgment after a universal one is not redundant if the particular is not obvious. The commentator then explains why a true particular cannot be predicated (by synonymy or derivation) and addresses an alternative answer that absolute existence is not an essential accident. He critiques this answer: existence qualified by necessity could be an essential accident for the Necessary, and if union in existence suffices for predication, then true particulars could be predicated—but they are not, so the argument needs refinement. He concludes that the predication of essential accidents is by derivation, so 'Zayd is the possessor of this existence' is acceptable.

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي
المواطأة
الاشتقاق
عرض ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a true particular can be predicated directly like 'this existence is God'.
Correction: A true particular cannot be predicated by synonymy; only derivation allows saying 'this existence belongs to God'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can a true particular like 'this existence' be predicated?
A) By synonymy (muwaṭa'ah)
B) By derivation (ishtiqaq)
C) It cannot be predicated at all
D) Only by intuition

Key Takeaway
True particulars are predicated by derivation, not synonymy, and this accommodates talk of particular existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a true particular: unique and unrepeatable. Every quality you have—your intelligence, your patience—is not you, but is 'possessed' by you. Does this realization humble you before the One who is the source of all existence? How does it change the way you see your own attributes and achievements?
?
If union in existence were enough to make two things identical, you would be nothing but a fragment of the universe. But you are distinct, dependent on the Creator every moment. When you look in the mirror, do you see a self-sufficient being, or a constantly sustained 'possessor of an existence'? What would it mean to live each day in that awareness?
?
The passage shows that even essential attributes are predicated by derivation. Your very existence is 'borrowed' from God. When you say 'I am,' are you claiming an independent reality, or are you acknowledging that you are merely the locus of a gift? Reflect: can you truly say 'I am' without also saying 'He causes me to be'?

Method of Learning
intermediate

Objection and Response on Proving the Subject's Existence
An objection that the subject's existence must be proven within its science is refuted with three answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that the subject's existence must be proven within its own science and how scholars responded with three distinct answers, including the role of proper accidents and division.

Core Idea
The subject of a science need not be proven within it; it may be self-evident or from a higher science.

This lesson takes you into a classic kalam debate: must the existence of God—the subject of kalam—be proven within kalam itself? An objector raises three challenges, each of which is answered with precision. By following the argument, you'll see how scholars protect the integrity of their science while grounding it in higher principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This is a dense argumentative passage; don't rush.
2
Identify the three parts of the objection
The objector gives three reasons why proving the subject's existence in its own science is problematic. Find each one in the text: the first about necessity, the second about consequence for all sciences, the third about meaninglessness.
Notice the words 'أما كون', 'وأما ثانياً', 'وأما ثالثا'.
3
Understand the first two answers
The answer to the first objection clarifies that existence is absolute, not restricted. The answer to the second uses the principle of division: the specific is known through the general.
The second answer introduces 'الانقسام' (division) as a proper accident of the general.
4
Trace the third answer and the additional argument
The third answer deals with particular judgments after general ones. Then an additional objection is raised about absolute existence, and the response addresses predication by derivation.
The additional argument begins with 'قوله: (وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ)'.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider how the responses defend kalam as a science without needing to prove God's existence within it. What does this tell you about the relationship between theology and higher sciences?
This connects to the broader theme of methodology in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقال : لما امتاز الوجود عما عداه من الأعراض الذاتية بتوقفها عليه لم يستحسنوا أن يجعل معها في قرن فيطلب إثباته مع إثباتها في علم واحد ( فيلزم ) إذا كان موضوع الكلام ذاته تعالى ( أما كون إثبات الصانع بينا بذاته فلا يحتاج إلى بيان أصلا ( أو كونه مبيناً في علم أعلى ) سواءً كان شرعياً، أو لا فإن بيان وجود الموضوع إنما يجوز المقيد بالوجوب، وأما ثانياً فلانه يستلزم أن لا يكون وجود شيء من الموجودات مسألة في شيء من العلوم، فلا يصح قولهم أن موضوع العلم إنما يبين وجوده في علم أعلى منه، وأما ثالثا فلان قولهم موضوع العلم لا يبين وجوده فيه بعد تقرير أنه لا يثبت في العلم سوى الأعراض الذاتية يكون لغوا من الكلام والجواب عن الأول إن اعتبار التقييد بالوجوب في قولنا: إن الواجب موجود بوجود يجب له لغو كذا تقييد الجوهر موجود بالوجود الجوهري، والعرض موجود بالوجود العرضي إلى غير ذلك، وأيضاً المبين إنما هو الوجود مطلقاً لا المقيد بالوجوب، وإن كان متحققاً في ضمنه وعن الثاني إن وجود الأخص إنما بين في الأعم بانقسامه إليه، وإلى غيره والانقسام من الأعراض الذاتيه للأعم كما سيجيء وعن الثالث أن التنصيص على الحكم الجزئي بعد بيان الحكم الكلي إذا كان خفاء في كونه جزئياً له لا يكون لغواً . قوله : ( وأجيب بأن الوجود المطلق إلخ ) هذا بالحقيقة عدول عن الوجه الأول، واستدلال بوجه آخر على عدم جواز البحث عن وجود موضوع علم في ذلك العلم ومثله مقبول في صناعة المناظرة لكن فيه نظر وهو أنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الوجود المطلق عرضاً ذاتياً لشيء من الموجودات وعدم صحة حمل الوجود الخاص عدم كون الوجود مطلقا من الأعراض الذاتية لشيء منها لجواز أن يكون الوجود مقيداً بالوجوب من الأعراض الذاتية للواجب، ويصح علية لكونه كلياً على أن الاتحاد في الوجود، إذا كفى في الحمل كما تدل عليه كلماتهم، لزم صحة حمل الجزئي الحقيقي على شيء كما يصح أن يحمل عليه، لأن الاتحاد من من الطرفين فكما جاز زيد إنسان فليجز عكسه أيضاً سلمنا أن الجزئي الحقيقي لا يحمل على شيء مواطأة، لكن الحمل في الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ولا مانع من أن يقال : زيد صاحب هذا الوجود فتأمل.
It might be said: Because existence is distinguished from other proper accidents by their dependence on it, they (the scholars) did not approve to place it together with them in the same category, so its proof is required along with their proof in one science. Then it follows, if the subject of kalam is God's essence, that either the proof of the Creator is self-evident and needs no proof at all, or it is proven in a higher science, whether religious or not. For the clarification of the existence of the subject is only permissible restricted by necessity. And secondly, because it would entail that the existence of nothing from existents would be a question in any science, so their statement that the subject of a science is clarified in a higher science would be invalid. And thirdly, because their statement that the subject of a science is not proven within it, after establishing that only proper accidents are taught in a science, would be meaningless speech. The answer to the first: the consideration of restriction by necessity in our saying 'the necessary existent exists by an existence necessary for it' is meaningless, as is restricting 'the substance exists by substantial existence' and 'the accident exists by accidental existence', etc. Also, what is clarified is only absolute existence, not existence restricted by necessity, even if it is realized within it. As for the second: the existence of the specific is clarified in the general by its division into it and other things, and division is among the proper accidents of the general, as will come. As for the third: specifying a particular judgment after clarifying the general judgment, if there is hiddenness in its being particular to it, is not meaningless. His statement: 'And it is answered: by absolute existence...' – this is in reality a departure from the first way, and an argument by another way against the permissibility of researching the existence of the subject of a science in that science. And its like is accepted in the art of disputation, but there is a problem: it does not follow from the fact that absolute existence is not a proper accident for any existent and the invalidity of predicating particular existence that absolute existence is not a proper accident for something, because it is possible that existence restricted by necessity is a proper accident for the necessary. And it is predicable because it is universal. And if unification in existence is sufficient for predication, as their words indicate, then it would follow the validity of predicating a true particular of something, just as it is valid to predicate of it. Because unification is from both sides, just as it is permissible to say 'Zayd is a human', so its converse should also be permissible. We concede that the true particular is not predicated of anything by verbal agreement, but predication in proper accidents is predication by derivation, and there is no obstacle to saying 'Zayd is the owner of this existence.' So reflect.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents an objection, its implications, and a detailed response. The objector argues that because existence is a prerequisite for other proper accidents, scholars did not include it in the same category, so its proof must be in the same science as those accidents. Consequently, if kalam's subject is God's essence, either God's existence is self-evident (needing no proof) or it is proven in a higher science. The objector then adds two further objections: second, if the subject's existence is not proven within its own science, then no existence would be a question in any science, invalidating the principle that a subject is clarified in a higher science; third, the statement that a subject's existence is not proven in its own science becomes meaningless if only proper accidents are taught. The response systematically answers each: to the first, the restriction to necessity is irrelevant—what is clarified is absolute existence, not restricted; to the second, the specific existence is clarified in the general by division (e.g., existence divided into necessary and possible), and division is a proper accident of the general; to the third, specifying a particular judgment after a general one is not meaningless if there is hiddenness. A further objection is then raised: absolute existence not being a proper accident does not prevent restricted existence (e.g., necessary existence) from being a proper accident for the necessary. The response argues that if unification in existence sufficed for predication, then a true particular could be predicated, which is not allowed by verbal agreement; however, in proper accidents, predication is by derivation, so one can say 'Zayd is the owner of this existence.'

Key Terms
إثبات الصانع
علم أعلى
موضوع العلم
الأعراض الذاتية
الانقسام
الموضوع
المسألة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of God must be proven within kalam itself, otherwise it cannot be a valid science.
Correction: The subject's existence need not be proven within the same science; it may be self-evident or proven in a higher science. This is a standard position in kalam methodology.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection (ثانياً) against proving the subject's existence in a higher science?
A) It leads to an infinite regress
B) It entails that no existence is a question in any science
C) It contradicts the definition of proper accidents
D) It makes the science dependent on a non-religious science

Key Takeaway
The objection that the subject's existence must be proven within its own science is answered by showing that existence is either self-evident or clarified in a higher science, and that proper accidents like division provide the necessary connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading that God's existence may be self-evident or proven in a higher science, ask yourself: Do I truly accept that there are truths I must simply acknowledge without proof? In what area of my life have I been demanding proof where none is needed, delaying my submission to the truth?
?
The passage mentions that specifying a particular judgment after a general one is not meaningless if there is hiddenness. Where in your own understanding of faith have you overlooked a particular truth because you were satisfied with a general principle? What specific aspect of God's attributes have you neglected?
?
Consider the phrase 'so reflect' at the end. When you study kalam, do you reflect deeply on the arguments, or do you just read them as information? What would it mean for you to make the study of aqeedah a matter of personal inquiry rather than passive learning?

The Necessity of Believing in a Benefit Before Studying
The commentator argues that a student must believe in a specific benefit to avoid absurdity in starting study.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a student must believe in a specific benefit before beginning a science, and how the commentator defends this against the Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
Without a believed benefit, starting study is absurd and inconceivable.

Before diving into the details of a science, a student must have a clear purpose. The commentator argues that without a specific benefit in mind, the very act of beginning is inconceivable. This lesson explores that argument and its implications across different theological schools.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule
The commentator states that presenting the benefit of a science is obligatory to avoid absurdity. Read the opening sentence and grasp the necessity.
The word 'دفعاً للعبث' is key: 'to ward off absurdity'.
2
Examine the reasoning
The commentator explains that voluntary action requires belief in a specific benefit. Consider the two cases: no benefit at all vs. some benefit. Why does the first make beginning impossible?
3
Consider the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'ari school holds that will can exist without belief in benefit. The commentator acknowledges this but shows that without some benefit, absurdity would follow. Notice how the argument is left incomplete.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the commentator likely concludes that even Ash'aris must admit a benefit to avoid absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : فائدته ]
وإنما وجب تقديم فائدة العلم الذي يراد أن يشرع فيه (دفعاً للعبث) فإن الطالب إن لم يعتقد فيه فائدة أصلاً لم يتصور منه الشروع فيه قطعاً، وذلك لظهوره لم
قوله : (فائدة ) أي مخصوصة فإما أن لا يعتقد فائدة أصلاً، أو يعتقد أن له فائدة ما .
قوله : (لم يتصور منه الشروع فيه قطعاً قال قدس سره في حواشي شرح الرسالة على ما بين في من أنه لا بد للفعل الاختياري من التصديق بفائدة مخصوصة، لئلا يلزم الحكمة محله أي في الترجيح بلا مرجح .
قوله : ( لظهوره إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى أن توقف الفعل الاختياري على التصديق بفائدة معينة أمر ظاهر في الشاهد، وأن القول بكفاية مجرد الإرادة في ترجيح أحد المتساويين كما في
قوله: (لم يتصور منه الشروع فيه قطعاً هذا إنما يستقيم على مذهب الحكماء، والمعتزلة القائلين: بوجوب وجود الاعتقاد بالنفع في الإرادة، وأما الأشعرية القائلون بوجود الإرادة بدون الاعتقاد النفع فلا استقامة لما ذكر على أصولهم لكفاية الإرادة في الشروع بلا شبهة، وأما ما يقال : في بيان إمكان الشروع بدون تصور الفائدة من أنه لو لم يمكن لم يتصور وجود العبث لأنه على قاعدة الاختيار ما لا يقصد به فائدة مع أنه يتصور قطعاً، ولذا يحترز عنه فقد يجاب عنه بأن
The Third Purpose: Its Benefit. It is necessary to present the benefit of the science that one intends to begin, to avoid absurdity. For if the student does not believe in a benefit at all, his beginning it is absolutely inconceivable, because of its obviousness. His statement: (Benefit) i.e., specific benefit. He either does not believe in a benefit at all, or he believes that it has some benefit. His statement: (He conceived beginning it absolutely) may his spirit be sanctified said in the glosses of Sharh al-Risala, as was explained that voluntary action must be accompanied by belief in a specific benefit, lest there be wisdom in its place, i.e., in preference without a preponderator. His statement: (Because of its obviousness, etc.) He indicated by that that the dependence of voluntary action on belief in a specific benefit is an obvious matter in the empirical world, and that the statement that mere will suffices to prefer one of two equals, as in... His statement: (He conceived beginning it absolutely) This is only sound according to the school of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila who affirm the necessity of the existence of belief in benefit in the will. As for the Ash'ariyya who affirm the existence of the will without belief in benefit, what was mentioned is not sound according to their principles because the will suffices for beginning without doubt. And as for what is said in explanation of the possibility of beginning without conception of benefit: that if it were impossible, then no absurdity could be conceived, because on the basis of choice it is what is not intended for benefit, yet it is conceived absolutely, and therefore one guards against it... Then it may be answered that
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessity of presenting the benefit of a science before studying it, to avoid absurdity. The commentator explains that the student must believe in a specific benefit for starting; otherwise, beginning is inconceivable. He then discusses that this applies to specific benefit (not any benefit). The commentator cites a previous work that voluntary action requires belief in a specific benefit to avoid preference without preponderator. He then notes that this principle is obvious in empirical experience. The passage ends with an objection from the Ash'ari school, which holds that will can exist without belief in benefit, so the premise is not sound for them. The commentator responds by noting that even Ash'aris must believe in a benefit to avoid absurdity, and he gives an argument: if starting without benefit were possible, then absurd actions could be conceived, but they are not.

Misconception: The Ash'ari view on will makes the benefit condition unnecessary.
Correction: The commentator shows that even Ash'aris must believe in a benefit to avoid absurdity, as otherwise no action would be purposeful.

Checkpoint
Why must a student believe in a specific benefit before starting a science, according to the commentator?
A) To ensure the teacher is respected.

C) Because the Ash'ari school requires it.
D) To guarantee that the student will succeed.

Key Takeaway
Every voluntary action, including study, requires a conscious belief in its benefit to avoid absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently studying this 'ilm (knowledge) with a clear, specific belief in its benefit for your hereafter, or are you merely going through the motions out of habit? If you lack that conviction, will you pause today to sincerely ask Allah to plant that certainty in your heart before you open the next book?
?
When you begin any act of obedience—prayer, fasting, seeking knowledge—do you consciously remind yourself of the specific benefit you hope to gain? If not, what does that say about the depth of your intention? Commit today to start each action by silently affirming: 'I do this believing in its benefit from Allah.'
?
The lesson argues that even the Ash'ari position must avoid absurdity. Is there any area of your life where you are acting without believing in a benefit—either out of blind imitation or false hope? Will you expose that area to the light of this principle and either reform your intention or abandon the action?

Benefit of a Science: Necessity and Sectarian Views
Argues for stating a science's benefit and discusses the Ash'ari objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why presenting the benefit of a science is necessary to avoid futility, and how the Ash'ari position challenges this requirement.

Core Idea
Belief in benefit is not universally required for action; the Ash'ariyya allow will without it.

This lesson explores a foundational methodological principle: before engaging in any science, one must state its benefit to avoid futile effort. The passage then examines a key theological divergence: while philosophers and Mu'tazila insist that belief in benefit is necessary for action, the Ash'ariyya argue that will alone suffices. Understanding this debate sharpens our grasp of Islamic theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening statement that presenting the benefit is necessary to avoid futility. Then see the conditional: if a student does not believe in any benefit, beginning is inconceivable.
The author presents this as a methodological necessity.
2
Observe the sectarian distinction
The passage explicitly contrasts the Mu'tazila and philosophers with the Ash'ariyya. The former require belief in benefit for the will to act; the latter do not. Identify which side the author's initial argument supports.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the debate.
3
Examine the objection and response
The passage raises an objection: if belief in benefit were necessary, then futile actions would be impossible. The author responds with an analogy (mirage and paths) to show that the actual benefit may not match the agent's belief. This closes the argument.
The Ash'ari position is subtle and has been criticized.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : فائدته ] وإنما وجب تقديم فائدة العلم الذي يراد أن يشرع فيه ( دفعاً للعبث ) فإن الطالب إن لم يعتقد فيه فائدة أصلاً لم يتصور منه الشروع فيه قطعاً، وذلك لظهوره لمخالفاً جميع المسائل من الكتاب والسنة غير صحيح، فإن زيادة الوجود في اعتقاده وإلا فأخذ وعينيته وتركب الجسم من الجواهر الفردة إلى غير ذلك عقليات صرفة غير مأخوذة منهما. قوله: (لم يتصور منه الشروع فيه قطعاً) هذا إنما يستقيم على مذهب الحكماء، والمعتزلة القائلين: بوجوب وجود الاعتقاد بالنفع في الإرادة، وأما الأشعرية القائلون بوجود الإرادة بدون الاعتقاد النفع فلا استقامة لما ذكر على أصولهم لكفاية الإرادة في الشروع بلا شبهة، وأما ما يقال: في بيان إمكان الشروع بدون تصور الفائدة من أنه لو لم يمكن لم يتصور وجود العبث لأنه على قاعدة الاختيار ما لا يقصد به فائدة مع أنه يتصور قطعاً، ولذا يحترز عنه فقد يجاب عنه بأنه يترتب عليه ما اعتقده، بل ما هو فائدته، وربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه فيعد سعيه في تحصيله قدحي العطشان وطريقي الهارب، كما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة أمر خفي حتى قال بعض الأذكياء : لا يسلم وجود مثل هذه الصفة لاستلزامه المحال .
The third section: its benefit. It is necessary to present the benefit of the science that one intends to engage in, in order to avoid futility. For if the student does not believe in any benefit at all, it is absolutely inconceivable that he would begin it, and that is due to its obviousness. His saying: ('It is absolutely inconceivable that he would begin it') This only holds on the doctrine of the philosophers and the Mu'tazila who affirm the necessity of the existence of belief in benefit in the will. As for the Ash'ariyya who affirm the existence of will without belief in benefit, what was mentioned does not hold on their principles, because the will is sufficient for beginning without doubt. And as for what is said: in explaining the possibility of beginning without conceiving of benefit, that if it were impossible, then the existence of futility would be inconceivable, because according to the principle of choice, that which is not intended for a benefit [is futile], yet it is absolutely conceivable, so they guard against it. It may be answered that what he believed [to be the benefit] does not actually follow, rather the actual benefit follows, and perhaps it does not agree with his purpose, so his effort in pursuing it is like the mirage of the thirsty and the two paths of the fugitive, as the Ash'ariyya held, a subtle matter, such that some intelligent ones said: the existence of such an attribute is not conceded because it entails impossibility.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage addresses the methodological necessity of stating the benefit of a science before studying it. The author argues that presenting the benefit is required to avoid futile action. He notes that if a student does not believe in any benefit whatsoever, he cannot logically begin studying. However, he then considers an objection from Ash'ari theology, which holds that the will can act without any belief in benefit. The author responds that the Ash'ari position is problematic because it would imply that even futile actions are conceivable, and he cites an analogy of a mirage and two paths to illustrate that the actual benefit may not match the student's expectation. The passage ends by noting that the Ash'ari view is subtle and has been criticized as entailing impossibility.

?

What would it take to realign your efforts?
?

Believing in a Benefit Different from the Actual Benefit
Commentary on a student's mistaken belief about benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the implications of a student pursuing knowledge with a mistaken belief about its benefit, and how scholars reconcile this with the concept of futility.

Core Idea
Even mistaken beliefs about benefit can lead to actual benefit, but the student's purpose may not be served.

This passage examines a subtle scenario: a student embarks on studying a science believing it has a certain benefit, but its real benefit is something else. The commentator explores whether such study is futile or can still be beneficial, and clarifies the nuanced language used by the author to describe this situation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Read the passage to grasp the core situation: a student believes in a benefit for a science that is not its actual benefit. Consider the initial claim that the student can begin, but the believed benefit does not follow.
2
Recognize the two categories
Identify the two categories of belief in benefit: believing in a specific benefit versus not believing in any benefit. The commentator discusses whether a non-specific benefit falls outside these categories.
The commentator argues it is better to include a hidden genitive to avoid futility.
3
Analyze the phrase 'ربما'
Focus on the three explanations the commentator provides for 'ربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه'. Understand how each explanation addresses the objection that 'ربما' implies probability.
4
Grasp the universal judgment about purpose
Consider the final point: the judgment is not limited to the assumed case. Understand the reinterpretation of 'غرض' as the agent's general objective.

His saying: ('And if he believes in it a benefit other than its actual benefit, he can begin it, except that what he believed does not follow, rather its actual benefit follows, and perhaps it is not in accordance with his purpose, so his effort in pursuing it becomes futile') like the mirage of the thirsty and the two paths of the fugitive, as the Ash'ariyya held, a subtle matter such that some intelligent ones said: the existence of such an attribute is not conceded because it entails impossibility. His saying: ('And if he believes in it a benefit other than its actual benefit, etc.') It was said: that which intends a non-specific benefit falls outside the two divisions, and the desired benefit may possibly follow, so it is not futile even though he did not conceive a specific benefit. So it is better to say: there is an omitted genitive, its estimated form being 'to avoid the possibility of futility', and warding off the possibility of corruption is a desired thing among rational people. And it may be answered that it is included in the first division, because the meaning of his saying 'if he does not believe in any benefit at all' is that he does not believe in a specific benefit, and that is either by not believing in any benefit at all, or by believing in some non-specific benefit, and on both suppositions, beginning is conceivable. What has occurred in the books, that what beginning depends on is conception in some way and belief in some benefit, is not meant as belief in a non-specific benefit, but rather a specific benefit, and the generalization is with respect to its actual benefit, such that saying the possibility of the desired benefit following in this case does not have much meaning, as is not hidden. His saying: ('And perhaps it is not in accordance with his purpose') If you say: the assumption is that the one who begins the science conceived a benefit that is its actual benefit, and there is no doubt that what follows is its actual benefit, so its lack of agreement with his purpose is complete, so there is no meaning for 'perhaps' which indicates reduction or multiplication. I say: First, 'perhaps' may be used for investigation, as Ibn al-Hajib mentioned in His saying 'And perhaps those who disbelieve wish they were Muslims' [al-Hijr: 2]. Second, what is meant by agreement is suitability, not correspondence, and then it is possible for the actual benefit to have suitability with the benefit that the student believed, by having some connection to it and dependence on it. Third, the pronoun in 'alayhi in his saying 'except that it does not follow from it' refers to the belief, not to the science that is begun, and the meaning is that it does not follow from the belief of benefit in something that what is intended [follows], rather the actual benefit of that thing follows, so it may be in accordance with the purpose if what he believed is the actual benefit, and may not be if it is not. In summary, his saying 'perhaps it is not' etc. is a universal judgment, not limited to the assumption; rather the state of the assumed case is known from it. And it may be said that what is meant by 'purpose' in his saying 'and perhaps it is not in accordance with his purpose' is not the purpose of the act in which he believed a benefit other than its actual benefit such that what was mentioned would apply, but rather the purpose of the agent in general, for it is possible that... غير ما هي.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the scenario where a student believes in a benefit for a science that is not its actual benefit. The commentator first notes that this scenario is complex and engages with objections. He discusses whether believing in a non-specific benefit falls under the previous division of 'no belief in benefit' and argues that it is better to interpret the original phrase as including a hidden genitive ('to avoid the possibility of futility'). He then tackles the phrase 'perhaps it is not in accordance with his purpose' (ربما لم تكن موافقة لغرضه). Three explanations are given: (1) 'ربما' can be used for emphasis or investigation (as in the Quranic verse), not necessarily for probability. (2) 'Agreement' (موافقة) here means suitability (ملاءمة) rather than exact correspondence, so the actual benefit may still be suitable even if not identical. (3) The pronoun in 'عليه' refers to the belief, not the science, so meaning: the believer's belief in benefit does not guarantee the actual benefit, but the actual benefit may still agree with his purpose if his belief was correct. Finally, he suggests that 'purpose' (غرض) may refer to the agent's general objective rather than the specific purpose of the act.

ربما

المطابقة
الغرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'ربما' always indicates probability or uncertainty.
Correction: In this context, 'ربما' is used for investigation or emphasis, as seen in the Quranic verse (al-Hijr: 2).

Checkpoint

B) Two

Key Takeaway
Even if a student's initial belief about benefit is wrong, the actual benefit can still be realized, but it may not align with his purpose, and the language 'ربما' emphasizes investigation, not uncertainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take for you to trust that the actual benefit—even if not what you expected—might still be suitable for your spiritual journey?
?
This lesson teaches that even a mistaken belief about benefit can lead to actual benefit. Reflect on a time you were wrong about the benefit of an act of worship. Did you continue anyway? What does that say about your trust in the system of reward from Allah? Are you willing to act on a non-specific hope? Or do you demand certainty before acting?
?
The commentator says 'دفع احتمال الفساد أمر مطلوب عند العقلاء' (warding off the possibility of corruption is desired by rational people). In your daily life, how often do you consider the possibility of corruption in your actions? When you study or worship, do you ever consider that your intention might be corrupted? Does this thought push you to purify your intention more rigorously?
