Subject Matter and Its Issues
Defines subject-issue relationship via essential accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the subject matter of a science encompasses its issues through essential accidents.

Core Idea
A science's issues are just its subject matter dressed in essential accidents.

In classical Islamic sciences, every discipline is defined by its subject matter and the things studied about it. This passage clarifies the precise relationship: all issues (masa'il) within a science are rooted in the main subject, and their predicates are essential accidents of that subject. This ensures the science remains unified and coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the technical terms 'عوارض ذاتية' and 'مسائل'. Try to grasp the flow: a refutation of a misunderstanding and then a positive definition.
The passage uses a commentarial style, referencing an earlier statement.
2
Understand the core definition
Focus on the last sentence: 'أن يكون موضوعات المسائل راجعاً إليه، ومحمولاتها من الأعراض الذاتية له'. This is the key definition of how the subject matter encompasses its issues.
The subjects of the issues refer back to the main subject, and the predicates (what is said about them) are essential accidents.
3
Address the potential objection
The passage first dismisses a possible objection about higher science principles being explained in lower sciences. Understand why this objection does not undermine the definition.
The commentator explains that the definition is based on essential accidents belonging to the subject, not on not explaining higher principles.
4
Apply to Kalam
Think how this definition applies to the science of Kalam. Its subject is Allah's essence and attributes, and its issues are topics like the world's temporality (predicate) which is an essential accident of the subject? (This is just to internalize the concept.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فليست إلخ) بناء على أن جميع ما يبين فيه من العوارض الذاتية لموضوعه، كما بينه الشارح رحمه الله لا على أنه لا يبين مبادي الأعلى في العلم الأدنى ليرد عليه أنه قد تبين مبادي الأعلى في الأدنى على قلة فلا يصح التفريع المذكور . قوله : ( على وجه يتناول تناول الموضوع للمسائل هو أن يكون موضوعات المسائل راجعاً إليه، ومحمولاتها من الأعراض الذاتية له .
His statement: 'so it is not etc.' based on the fact that all that is explained in it of essential accidents belong to its subject matter, as the commentator (rahimahullah) explained, not that it does not explain the principles of the higher science in the lower science, so that it might be objected that the principles of the higher have been explained in the lower, albeit rarely, so the mentioned inference is not valid. His statement: 'in a manner that encompasses the subject matter's encompassing of the issues is that the subjects of the issues refer back to it, and their predicates are essential accidents of it.'
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between the subject matter of a science and its issues. It explains that all the essential accidents (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya) clarified in a science belong to its subject matter. This does not mean that the principles of a higher science are not explained in a lower science, as might be objected. The author clarifies that the subject matter 'encompasses' the issues in the sense that the subjects of the issues refer back to it, and their predicates are essential accidents of that subject. This defines how issues are constituted within a science.

Key Terms
موضوع
مسائل
عوارض ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a science's issues are independent of its subject matter or that the subject matter only encompasses issues by being the topic of discussion without a formal relationship.
Correction: The passage specifies that issues are not independent: their subjects refer back to the main subject, and their predicates are essential accidents of that subject. This formal relationship unifies the science.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the predicates of the issues?
A) Principles of higher sciences
B) Essential accidents of the subject matter
C) Independent topics
D) The subjects of the issues

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of a science encompasses its issues by making the issues' subjects refer back to it and their predicates essential accidents of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a new Islamic science, do you consciously trace each new piece of information back to its core subject and ask whether it is an essential property of that subject? Or do you passively collect facts? Commit this week to mapping each lesson's issues to the subject's essential accidents.
?
Consider your own understanding of a basic science like Fiqh. Do you treat it as a list of rulings or as a coherent system where each ruling's predicate (e.g., obligatory, prohibited) is an essential accident of the act (the subject matter of Fiqh)? How would this shift your engagement with the rulings?
?
If you realized that much of Islamic knowledge you've learned is not grounded in its proper subject-issue relationship, would you be willing to re-study it with this logical structure in mind? What is holding you back from doing so?

Mabahith al-Nazar as Part of Kalam
Argues that the investigations of reasoning are part of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against including the investigations of reasoning (mabahith al-nazar) as part of kalam, and why the author maintains their integral role.

Core Idea
The investigations of reasoning are part of kalam because they deal with states of the known in establishing religious beliefs.

This passage presents a debate about the scope of kalam. While some consider the science of reasoning (mabahith al-nazar) as belonging to logic, a separate science, the author argues that because these investigations are concerned with the states of the known (ahwal al-ma'lum) specifically for establishing religious beliefs, they are properly part of kalam. He refutes opposing views to solidify his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the different opinions referenced.
The passage uses technical terms like 'mabahith al-nazar' and 'ahwal al-ma'lum'.
2
Identify the key opinions
The passage mentions the majority view, the commentator's view, and the author's own view. Identify each and note how they differ.
3
Understand the author's argument
The author argues that mabahith al-nazar are part of kalam because they deal with states of the known in relation to establishing religious beliefs. See how he refutes the counterargument that logic is separate.
The author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction matters for defining the scope of kalam. What does it say about the relationship between kalam and logic?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجعلوا إلخ) ما ذكره ها هنا من جعل مباحث النظر من مقاصد الكلام مذهب أن جميع الجمهور، والحق عند الشارح رحمه الله ما ذكره في حواشي شرح مختصر الأصول من العلوم في صحة مواد أدلتها وصورها تحتاج إلى المنطق، وأنه علم على حياله ليس جزءاً لعلم، ولا يلزم من ذلك كونه أعلى من الكلام والإلهي لأن احتياجهما إليه باعتبار ما يعرض لمباديهما التصورية والتصديقية لا باعتبار المبادي أنفسها، فلا مخالفة بين كلاميه، والحق عندي أن مباحث النظر جزء من الكلام لكونه من أحوال المعلوم من حيث يتعلق به إثبات العقائد الدينية، مخالفة لكثير من المسائل المنطقية والاشتراك في البعض لا يستلزم الاتحاد فكون المنطق علماً على حدة لا يستلزم أن تكون مباحث النظر كذلك وما قيل إن المسائل المنطقية من حيث إنها يتوقف عليها إثبات العقائد الدينية من الكلام ومن حيث إنها يتوقف عليها إثبات المطالب مطلقاً، ليست جزءاً منه كلام يلوح عليه آثار الضعف فإنه يلزم منه أن تكون تلك المسائل من حيث إنها يتوقف عليها استنباط الأحكام الشرعية جزءاً من الأصول وقس على ذلك
His statement: '(And they made etc.)' What he mentioned here about making the investigations of reasoning (mabahith al-nazar) among the aims of kalam is the doctrine of the majority. And the truth according to the commentator (rahimahullah) is what he mentioned in the glosses of Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul: that the sciences, in the validity of the materials of their proofs and their forms, need logic, and that it is a science on its own, not a part of any science. And it does not follow from that that it is higher than kalam and theology (ilahi) because their need for it is with respect to what pertains to their conceptual and justificatory principles, not with respect to the principles themselves, so there is no contradiction between his two statements. And the truth in my view is that the investigations of reasoning are part of kalam because they are among the states of the known (ahwal al-ma'lum) insofar as it relates to establishing religious beliefs, differing from many logical issues; and commonality in some does not necessitate union. So logic being a separate science does not necessitate that the investigations of reasoning are also separate. And what was said that the logical issues, insofar as establishing religious beliefs depends on them, are from kalam, and insofar as establishing claims absolutely depends on them, are not part of it – this is speech that shows signs of weakness, because it would necessitate that those issues, insofar as deriving legal rulings depends on them, are part of usul, and analogize to that.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage argues that the investigations of reasoning (mabahith al-nazar) are part of kalam. The author presents the view of the majority that they are among the aims of kalam. He then cites the commentator's view that the sciences need logic for the validity of their proofs, but logic is a separate science. However, the author disagrees and states that mabahith al-nazar are part of kalam because they deal with the states of the known (al-ma'lum) in relation to establishing religious beliefs. He refutes the objection that logical issues, when considered absolutely, are not part of kalam, arguing that such a view is weak because it would lead to contradictions. The author maintains that sharing some issues with logic does not make them the same science, and that the specific purpose of establishing religious beliefs makes these investigations integral to kalam.

Key Terms
مباحث النظر
مقاصد الكلام
منطق
أحوال المعلوم
العقائد الدينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because logic is a separate science, all investigations of reasoning (mabahith al-nazar) must also be separate from kalam.
Correction: The author argues that mabahith al-nazar are part of kalam because they are specifically concerned with the states of the known in the context of establishing religious beliefs, differing from many logical issues.

Checkpoint
According to the author's own view, why are mabahith al-nazar part of kalam?
A) Because they are the same as logic
B) Because they deal with states of the known related to establishing religious beliefs
C) Because the majority said so
D) Because they are needed for all sciences

Key Takeaway
The investigations of reasoning are integral to kalam because their subject matter—the states of the known as they relate to religious beliefs—places them within the scope of kalam, not as a separate science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a field of study, like logic or reasoning, as 'not part of your deen' without realizing how it serves your core purpose of strengthening your faith? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the tools you need? What is one concrete step you will take this week to integrate sound reasoning into your practice of Islam?
?
The author insists that the investigations of reasoning are part of kalam because they directly relate to establishing religious beliefs. When you face doubts about your faith, do you actively engage in structured reasoning, or do you rely solely on emotional comfort? Are you willing to learn the 'how' of reasoning to protect your beliefs?
?
If reasoning is essential to establishing your religious beliefs, how much time and effort do you actually invest in learning it compared to other Islamic sciences? What is holding you back from studying the methodology of kalam or logic with sincerity and humility?

Method of Learning

Principles of Kalam: Self-Evident or Proven Within
Classification of principles in kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the principles of kalam are either self-evident (requiring no proof) or proven within kalam itself, and that self-evident principles are not classified as 'issues' (masa'il).

Core Idea
Kalam's principles stand on their own or are proven within it.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif establishes a foundational methodological point: the science of kalam does not borrow its first principles from other disciplines. Instead, its principles are either completely self-evident, needing no explanation, or they are demonstrated within the science itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of kalam arguments and what counts as a starting point versus what requires proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the contrast between the two types of principles mentioned.
The passage includes interlinear commentary; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the two types of principles
The passage divides principles into 'self-evident' (bayyina bi-nafsiha) and 'proven within' (mubayyana fihi). Mark these in the text.
3
Understand why self-evident principles are not issues
The commentary explains that self-evident principles are independent of proof, reminder, or explanation of cause, so they are not considered 'issues' (masa'il), which always require some form of inquiry.
4
Reflect on the methodological significance
Consider why this classification matters: it shows that kalam is self-sufficient and does not depend on other sciences for its foundations, and it clarifies how different types of propositions function within the science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه نفسه عما عداه ليس له مباد في علم آخر ( بل مباديه إما بينة بنفسها ) مستغنية عن البيان بالكلية (أو) مبينة فيه فهي أي فتلك المبادي المبينة فيه مسائل له من الحيثية ) ومباد لمسائل أخر منه لا تتوقف ( تلك المبادي ( عليها ) أي على المسائل . قوله : ( مستغنية إلخ) أي لا تحتاج إلى دليل ولا تنبيه ولا إلى بيان اللمية، فلا تكون من المسائل لأن المسألة إما نظرية أو بديهية تحتاج إلى تنبيه أو إلى بيان اللمية كما مر .
This [science] itself, besides what is other than it, has no principles in another science. Rather, its principles are either self-evident, completely independent of explanation, or proven within it, so those principles proven within it are issues for it in one respect and principles for other issues within it that do not depend on those principles, i.e., on the issues. His statement: 'independent etc.' meaning they have no need for a proof, nor a reminder, nor an explanation of the reason why, so they are not from among the issues because the issue is either theoretical or intuitive needing a reminder or an explanation of the reason why, as has passed.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of the principles (mabadi') of kalam. It states that kalam itself has no principles derived from another science. Instead, its principles are of two types: either self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsiha), totally independent of any explanation, or they are proven within kalam itself. Those principles that are proven within kalam are considered issues of kalam in one respect and principles for other issues within kalam that do not depend on those principles. The author then explains that the self-evident principles are not considered 'issues' (masa'il) because a mas'ala is either theoretical or intuitive that requires a reminder or demonstration of cause. Thus, self-evident principles fall outside the category of issues.

Key Terms
مباد
بينة بنفسها
مسائل
نظرية
بديهية
بيان اللمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principles of kalam are derived from other sciences like logic or physics.
Correction: Kalam has its own principles that are either self-evident or proven within itself; it does not rely on external sciences for its foundational principles.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of principles that are self-evident in kalam?
A) They are considered issues of kalam
B) They are not considered issues because they need no proof or explanation
C) They are principles from another science
D) They require a reminder

Key Takeaway
The principles of kalam are either self-evident and require no proof, or they are proven within kalam itself, functioning as both issues and principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a self-evident truth of faith as if it needed proof, seeking arguments for what you already know in your heart? Which truth, and why did you doubt?
?
The passage teaches that some principles function as both issues and foundations. In your own life, what core belief—so central that it shapes other beliefs—are you willing to re-examine as a 'issue' rather than a fixed 'principle'?
?
If self-evident principles require no explanation, what stops you from living with full certainty in those truths today? Is it pride, fear, or distraction—and what will you do about it this week?

Logic: A Means, Not a Master
Introduces logic as necessary for kalam and defends its instrumental status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why logic is considered a necessary instrument for kalam but not a superior science.

Core Idea
Logic is a tool, not a rival, to kalam.

The passage opens with a bold claim: the knowledge of proofs and definitions depends on logic. But this immediately raises a problem: if logic provides principles, doesn't it make logic higher than kalam? The author anticipates this objection and provides a crucial distinction between providing principles themselves and providing the means to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement about logic's necessity
Read how logic is needed for proofs and definitions, and how it is attributed to kalam as a principle.
Notice the phrase 'not a part of them, but a science on its own'.
2
Identify the objection that logic would be higher
Find the objection: if logic explains many principles for kalam, it would be higher than kalam.
This is a natural objection to the preceding claim.
3
Understand the response
The response says logic does not explain the principles themselves but explains what pertains to them (the ways and means). It is a means (wasila) and instrument (ala).
This is key: logic is instrumental, not foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبين دلائل، وتعريفات معينة والعلم بكونها موصلة إلى المقصود لا يحصل إلا من المباحث المنطقية، أو يتقوى بها فهي محتاج إليها لتلك العلوم، وليس جزءاً منـهـا بـل هـي عـلـم عـلـى حيالها، وعلم الكلام لما كان رئيس العلوم الشرعية ومقدماً عليها انتسب إليه هذه القاعدة المحتاج إليها فعدت مبادي كلامية للعلوم الشرعية هذا ،كلامه ونقل عنه في الحواشي لا يقال فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون المنطق أعلى من الكلام والإلهي لأنه يبين مبادي كثيرة لهما لا مثلها في الأدنى كما لا يخفى لأنا نقول لا يبين مباديهما أصلا بل يبين ما يعرض مباديهما التصورية، والتصديقية المصطلح عليها من الطرق الموصلة إلى مقاصدها، ومثلها يسمى وسيلةً وآلة، ويمكن أن يقال : في التلفيق لا شك أن أحوال المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية أحوال الموضوعات الكلامية، لكن إذا حملت تلك الأحوال عليها وجعلت مسألة ففيه اعتباران الأول اعتبار أنه يتوقف عليه، إثبات المطالب مطلقاً، وليست بهذا الاعتبار جزءاً من الكلام أصلاً، قيل : ولهذا احترز عن المنطق في تعريف الكلام ، ولو كان جزءاً لم يكن للاحتراز وجه إذ لا معنى قيد في تعريف علم يوجب خروج بعض أجزائه والثاني اعتبار أنه يتوقف عليه إثبات العقائد بهذا الاعتبار جزء منه فذكر تلك المسائل في كتب الكلام لا من حيث الاعتبار الأول، بل من حيث الاعتبار الثاني، وذكره في مبادي الأصول لا من حيث الاعتبار الثاني إذ لا له، بل من حيث الاعتبار الأول، وإليه ينظر قوله : في الحواشي والجواب أن إثبات العلوم النظرية إلخ لا يقال : تلك المسائل إذا لم تجعل جزءاً من علم الكلام، ولو بالاعتبار الثاني لم يلزم أن يكون المنطق أعلى منه كما مر فأي احتياج إلى جعلها جزءاً لأنا نقول : لأنهم لم يرضوا أن يحتاجوا في علمهم هذا إلى شيء سواء بقي الكلام في قواعد العربية المحتاج إليها لاستنباط الأدلة السمعية، وقد يقال هو أيضا جزء من الكلام أفرز عنه إفراز الكحالة الدينية، معنى بعض من وهي لذكر الاعتقاديات من الطب وإفراز الفرائض من الفقه فليتأمل . قوله : (أو) مبينة فيه فهي مسائل (له قيل كلام الشارح يشعر بأن مباديها البينة بنفسها ليست من مسائل الكلام من أن فيها الحكم بمعلوم يتوقف على ذلك الحكم، إثبات شيء من العقائد وليس مع مسائل الكلام، إلا ذلك وأما التقييد بالنظرية فقد عرفت أنه بالنظر إلى الغالب، وأما احتمال كون عروض محمولات المبادي البينة من حيث خصوص موضوع المسألة لا من حيث أنه معلوم مخصوص حتى يكون من المسائل فقائم في المبادي النظرية أيضاً اللهم إلا أن يقال : لم يوجد ذلك في المبادي النظرية .
Demonstrates proofs and specific definitions, and the knowledge of their leading to the intended goal is not obtained except from logical investigations, or is strengthened by them. So they are in need of them for those sciences, and they are not a part of them, but they are a science on their own. And since the science of kalam is the chief of the legal sciences and is placed before them, this needed rule became attributed to it, so it was counted as kalamic principles for the legal sciences. This is his statement, and it is transmitted from him in the glosses. It is not said: 'On this basis, it would follow that logic is higher than kalam and theology because it explains many principles for them, unlike the lower sciences' as is not hidden, because we say: it does not explain their principles at all, rather it explains what pertains to their conceptual and justificatory principles, the established ways leading to their goals, and such is called a means and an instrument. And it can be said in composition: There is no doubt that the states of conceptual and justificatory information are states of kalamic subjects, but when those states are carried upon them and made into an issue, there are two considerations: The first consideration is that establishing claims absolutely depends on it, and in this respect it is not part of kalam at all. It is said: For this reason he avoided [including] logic in the definition of kalam, and if it were part, there would be no justification for avoidance, since there is no meaning in a condition in the definition of a science that necessitates the exclusion of some of its parts. The second consideration is that establishing religious beliefs depends on it; in this respect it is part of it. So those issues are mentioned in the books of kalam not from the first consideration, but from the second consideration, and their mention in the principles of usul is not from the second consideration since it does not have [that], but from the first consideration. And to this refers his statement in the glosses: 'The answer that establishing theoretical sciences etc.' It is not said: 'Those issues, if not made part of the science of kalam, even with the second consideration, it does not follow that logic is higher than it as has passed, so what need is there to make them part?' Because we say: They were not satisfied that their science should need anything, whether the issue remains in the rules of Arabic needed for deriving scriptural proofs, and it may be said that it is also part of kalam, separated from it like the separation of ophthalmology from medicine and the separation of the laws of inheritance from jurisprudence, so let it be considered. His statement: 'or proven within it, so they are issues for it.' It is said that the speech of the commentator suggests that its self-evident principles are not from the issues of kalam because they involve a judgment about something known that depends on that judgment, establishing something of beliefs, and there is nothing with the issues of kalam except that. As for the restriction to theoretical [issues], you have known that it is with respect to the majority. As for the possibility that the ascription of predicates of self-evident principles from the perspective of the particular subject of the issue, not from the perspective that it is a particular known, so as to be from the issues, is present in theoretical principles as well, unless it is said that that does not occur in theoretical principles.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between logic and kalam in depth. It begins by stating that the knowledge of proofs and definitions leading to the intended goal is obtained only from logical investigations, or is strengthened by them. Therefore, the sciences need logic, but logic is a separate science, not part of them. Since kalam is the chief of the legal sciences, this needed rule (logic) became attributed to it, so it was counted as kalamic principles for the legal sciences. The author then addresses an objection: if logic explains many principles for kalam, doesn't that make logic higher than kalam? The answer is that logic does not explain the principles of kalam themselves; it explains what pertains to them, i.e., the ways and means. Then the author presents two considerations (i'tibaran) regarding logical issues: (1) considered in terms of establishing claims absolutely, they are not part of kalam, which is why the definition of kalam excludes logic; (2) considered in terms of establishing religious beliefs, they are part of kalam. Therefore, when these issues appear in kalam books, they are from the second consideration. The author also discusses the self-evident principles, noting that they are not from the issues of kalam because they involve a judgment that depends on them. Finally, he considers the possibility that even theoretical principles might be considered in terms of particular subjects, suggesting that no issue of a science is purely about the subject matter without a specific perspective.

Key Terms
منطق
مبادي
وسيلة
آلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is logic not considered higher than kalam?
A) Because logic is a part of kalam
B) Because logic explains principles themselves
C) Because logic only explains the means to the principles
D) Because kalam is the chief of the legal sciences

Key Takeaway
Logic is a necessary instrument for kalam, but it remains a separate, subordinate science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you treat logical reasoning as a humble servant to the revealed truths, or have you allowed it to become the judge of what is true? Will you commit to using logic only as a means, never as a master?
?
In your own pursuit of knowledge, are you more attached to the tools (like logic, argumentation) or to the ends (closeness to Allah)? How can you realign your heart today?
?
If someone were to accuse you of relying too much on logic in your faith, would your defense be that you use it as a tool, or would you realize you've made it a source of authority? Will you examine your methods this week?

Two Considerations: When Logic Belongs to Kalam
Presents two considerations for logical issues in kalam and addresses a follow-up objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two considerations (i'tibaran) that determine when logical issues are considered part of kalam and why the definition of kalam excludes logic.

Core Idea
Logical issues are part of kalam only when they serve religious beliefs.

After establishing that logic is a means, the author introduces a nuanced distinction. Logical issues can be considered from two angles: as necessary for establishing any claim, or as necessary for establishing religious beliefs. This distinction is key to understanding why kalam books include logic in some places but not as a part of the science itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction of the two considerations
The author states that when the states of information are carried onto kalamic subjects and made into an issue, there are two considerations.
This is a key turning point in the argument.
2
Identify the first consideration
The first: establishing claims absolutely depends on logic, but in this respect it is not part of kalam. This is why the definition of kalam avoids including logic.
The author says it was to avoid including logic that the definition was formulated.
3
Identify the second consideration
The second: establishing religious beliefs depends on logic, and in this respect it is part of kalam. So logical issues appear in kalam books from this second perspective.
This explains the presence of logic in kalam texts.
4
Understand the subsequent objection and response
An objection arises: if logic is not part of kalam even in the second respect, why make it part? The response: because kalam should not need anything outside itself, similar to how Arabic grammar is considered part of kalam.
This reinforces the practical inclusion.

In the second consideration, logic is considered part of kalam because it is needed for:
A) Establishing any claim absolutely

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change if you reconsidered it under the second consideration?
?

?

Self-Evident Principles in Kalam
Discusses whether self-evident principles are part of kalam issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether self-evident principles are part of kalam issues and how the perspective of the subject matter affects this.

Core Idea
Self-evident principles are not kalam issues because they do not depend on theoretical judgment.

The passage concludes with a subtle point about self-evident (bayyin) principles. The commentator suggests they are not from the issues of kalam because they involve a judgment that itself depends on those principles. This raises questions about the nature of scientific issues and the role of perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about self-evident principles
The commentator says that self-evident principles are not from the issues of kalam because they contain a judgment that depends on that judgment itself.
This is a philosophical point about the nature of self-evidence.
2
Understand the restriction to theoretical issues
The author notes that the restriction to theoretical issues is based on majority, suggesting self-evident issues are exceptions.
This implies that most kalam issues are theoretical.
3
Consider the possibility for theoretical principles too
The author suggests that even theoretical principles could be considered from a particular subject perspective, but this does not occur.
This is a fine-grained point about classification.

الكحالة الدينية، معنى بعض من وهي لذكر الاعتقاديات من الطب وإفراز الفرائض من الفقه فليتأمل . قوله : (أو) مبينة فيه فهي مسائل (له قيل كلام الشارح يشعر بأن مباديها البينة بنفسها ليست من مسائل الكلام من أن فيها الحكم بمعلوم يتوقف على ذلك الحكم، إثبات شيء من العقائد وليس مع مسائل الكلام، إلا ذلك وأما التقييد بالنظرية فقد عرفت أنه بالنظر إلى الغالب، وأما احتمال كون عروض محمولات المبادي البينة من حيث خصوص موضوع المسألة لا من حيث أنه معلوم مخصوص حتى يكون من المسائل فقائم في المبادي النظرية أيضاً اللهم إلا أن يقال : لم يوجد ذلك في المبادي النظرية .
Demonstrates proofs and specific definitions, and the knowledge of their leading to the intended goal is not obtained except from logical investigations, or is strengthened by them. So they are in need of them for those sciences, and they are not a part of them, but they are a science on their own. And since the science of kalam is the chief of the legal sciences and is placed before them, this needed rule became attributed to it, so it was counted as kalamic principles for the legal sciences. This is his statement, and it is transmitted from him in the glosses. It is not said: 'On this basis, it would follow that logic is higher than kalam and theology because it explains many principles for them, unlike the lower sciences' as is not hidden, because we say: it does not explain their principles at all, rather it explains what pertains to their conceptual and justificatory principles, the established ways leading to their goals, and such is called a means and an instrument. And it can be said in composition: There is no doubt that the states of conceptual and justificatory information are states of kalamic subjects, but when those states are carried upon them and made into an issue, there are two considerations: The first consideration is that establishing claims absolutely depends on it, and in this respect it is not part of kalam at all. It is said: For this reason he avoided [including] logic in the definition of kalam, and if it were part, there would be no justification for avoidance, since there is no meaning in a condition in the definition of a science that necessitates the exclusion of some of its parts. The second consideration is that establishing religious beliefs depends on it; in this respect it is part of it. So those issues are mentioned in the books of kalam not from the first consideration, but from the second consideration, and their mention in the principles of usul is not from the second consideration since it does not have [that], but from the first consideration. And to this refers his statement in the glosses: 'The answer that establishing theoretical sciences etc.' It is not said: 'Those issues, if not made part of the science of kalam, even with the second consideration, it does not follow that logic is higher than it as has passed, so what need is there to make them part?' Because we say: They were not satisfied that their science should need anything, whether the issue remains in the rules of Arabic needed for deriving scriptural proofs, and it may be said that it is also part of kalam, separated from it like the separation of ophthalmology from medicine and the separation of the laws of inheritance from jurisprudence, so let it be considered. His statement: 'or proven within it, so they are issues for it.' It is said that the speech of the commentator suggests that its self-evident principles are not from the issues of kalam because they involve a judgment about something known that depends on that judgment, establishing something of beliefs, and there is nothing with the issues of kalam except that. As for the restriction to theoretical [issues], you have known that it is with respect to the majority. As for the possibility that the ascription of predicates of self-evident principles from the perspective of the particular subject of the issue, not from the perspective that it is a particular known, so as to be from the issues, is present in theoretical principles as well, unless it is said that that does not occur in theoretical principles.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between logic and kalam in depth. It begins by stating that the knowledge of proofs and definitions leading to the intended goal is obtained only from logical investigations, or is strengthened by them. Therefore, the sciences need logic, but logic is a separate science, not part of them. Since kalam is the chief of the legal sciences, this needed rule (logic) became attributed to it, so it was counted as kalamic principles for the legal sciences. The author then addresses an objection: if logic explains many principles for kalam, doesn't that make logic higher than kalam? The answer is that logic does not explain the principles of kalam themselves; it explains what pertains to them, i.e., the ways and means. Then the author presents two considerations (i'tibaran) regarding logical issues: (1) considered in terms of establishing claims absolutely, they are not part of kalam, which is why the definition of kalam excludes logic; (2) considered in terms of establishing religious beliefs, they are part of kalam. Therefore, when these issues appear in kalam books, they are from the second consideration. The author also discusses the self-evident principles, noting that they are not from the issues of kalam because they involve a judgment that depends on them. Finally, he considers the possibility that even theoretical principles might be considered in terms of particular subjects, suggesting that no issue of a science is purely about the subject matter without a specific perspective.

Key Terms
مبادي
بينة
نظرية
مسائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident principles are considered part of kalam issues because they are used in arguments.
Correction: The passage argues that self-evident principles are not part of kalam issues because they involve a judgment that is self-subsisting and not dependent on theoretical analysis.

Checkpoint
Why are self-evident principles not considered issues of kalam according to the passage?
A) Because they are too simple
B) Because they involve a judgment that depends on themselves for establishing beliefs
C) Because they are from the perspective of a particular subject
D) Because they are theoretical

Key Takeaway
Self-evident principles are excluded from kalam issues because they are not dependent on theoretical reasoning, unlike the majority of kalam topics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey of faith, do you treat the core beliefs (like the existence of Allah) as self-evident and act on them with certainty, or do you constantly subject them to theoretical doubt? How does that affect your relationship with Allah?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you ever find yourself arguing about matters that are fundamentally clear? What would change if you embraced the self-evident as a foundation rather than a topic for debate?

Logic as an Instrument: Refuting the Claim of Superiority
Initial answer to the objection that logic is superior to kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial rebuttal to the claim that logic is superior to kalam, namely that logic is merely an instrument for kalam and does not provide its principles.

Core Idea
Logic does not dictate kalam's principles; it is just a tool.

A common objection to kalam is that if it depends on logic for its principles, then logic must be a higher science. This passage presents the first part of the answer: logic does not provide the principles themselves but only the methods for using them, making it an instrument rather than a superior science.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection that claims logic must be higher than kalam because it explains many principles.
The objection is introduced by 'لا يقال'.
2
Identify the initial answer
The answer says logic does not explain the principles at all, but only explains what pertains to them from the methods. Understand the distinction.
The key phrase is 'وسيلة وآلة'.
3
Reflect on the instrumental role
Think about why describing logic as a means and instrument prevents it from being considered higher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون المنطق أعلى من الكلام والإلهي لأنه يبين مبادي كثيرة لهما لا مثلها في الأدنى كما لا يخفى لأنا نقول لا يبين مباديهما أصلا بل يبين ما يعرض مباديهما التصورية، والتصديقية المصطلح عليها من الطرق الموصلة إلى مقاصدها، ومثلها يسمى وسيلةً وآلة، ويمكن أن يقال : في التلفيق لا شك أن أحوال المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية أحوال الموضوعات الكلامية، لكن إذا حملت تلك الأحوال عليها وجعلت مسألة ففيه اعتباران الأول اعتبار أنه يتوقف عليه، إثبات المطالب مطلقاً، وليست بهذا الاعتبار جزءاً من الكلام أصلاً، قيل : ولهذا احترز عن المنطق في تعريف الكلام ، ولو كان جزءاً لم يكن للاحتراز وجه إذ لا معنى قيد في تعريف علم يوجب خروج بعض أجزائه والثاني اعتبار أنه يتوقف عليه إثبات العقائد بهذا الاعتبار جزء منه فذكر تلك المسائل في كتب الكلام لا من حيث الاعتبار الأول، بل من حيث الاعتبار الثاني، وذكره في مبادي الأصول لا من حيث الاعتبار الثاني إذ لا له، بل من حيث الاعتبار الأول، وإليه ينظر قوله : في الحواشي والجواب أن إثبات العلوم النظرية إلخ لا يقال : تلك المسائل إذا لم تجعل جزءاً من علم الكلام، ولو بالاعتبار الثاني لم يلزم أن يكون المنطق أعلى منه كما مر فأي احتياج إلى جعلها جزءاً لأنا نقول : لأنهم لم يرضوا أن يحتاجوا في علمهم هذا إلى شيء .
It is not said: on this basis, logic must be higher than kalam and theology because it explains many principles for them, unlike their lower counterparts, as is not hidden. For we say: it does not explain their principles at all, but rather explains what pertains to their conceptual and assertoric principles from the methods leading to their aims, and such is called a means and instrument. It can be said: in the compilation, there is no doubt that the states of conceptual and assertoric information are states of kalam subjects, but when those states are applied to them and made a problem, there are two considerations. The first consideration: that it is depended upon absolutely for establishing demands, and by this consideration it is not a part of kalam at all. It is said: for this reason one avoids logic in the definition of kalam, and had it been a part, there would be no point in avoidance, for a restriction in a definition of a science that necessitates exclusion of some of its parts is meaningless. The second consideration: that it is depended upon for establishing beliefs; by this consideration it is a part of it. So those problems are mentioned in kalam books not from the first consideration but from the second consideration, and mentioning it in the principles of usul is not from the second consideration because it has no part, but from the first consideration. To this his statement in the glosses refers: 'The answer is that establishing theoretical sciences, etc.' It is not said: if those problems are not made a part of kalam, even by the second consideration, logic would not be higher than it, as mentioned, so what need is there to make them part? For we say: because they were not satisfied that they need anything in their science.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (إيراد وجواب) defending the status of kalam against the claim that logic is higher than it. The objection states that if kalam depends on logic for its principles, then logic is superior. The author answers that logic does not provide the principles themselves but only the methods (طرق) for using them, so logic is merely an instrument (وسيلة وآلة). A further refined answer introduces two considerations (اعتباران) regarding logical topics included in kalam books: from the first consideration, these topics are prerequisites for any proof and are not part of kalam; from the second, they are part of kalam specifically for establishing beliefs. Thus, kalam is not subordinate to logic. The passage also addresses a secondary objection: if the topics are not part of kalam even by the second consideration, then logic is still not higher. The answer explains that kalam scholars preferred to incorporate these topics to avoid dependence on an external science.

Key Terms
منطق
وسيلة وآلة
مبادئ تصورية
مبادئ تصديقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If kalam uses logic, logic must be superior.
Correction: Logic is a tool, not a source of authority.

Checkpoint
According to the initial answer, what role does logic play in relation to kalam?
A) It provides the principles of kalam.
B) It is a means and instrument.
C) It is a higher science.
D) It is part of kalam.

Key Takeaway
Logic is a tool for kalam, not a superior science, because it only provides methods for using principles, not the principles themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study kalam or theology, do you find yourself more captivated by the logical arguments themselves—their cleverness and structure—or by the divine truths they aim to uncover? Which one occupies more space in your heart?
?
If logic is merely a tool, not the source of truth, then what is the source of the principles you hold about God and His attributes? Have you ever examined whether your convictions rest on the tool or on the revealed content? How would knowing this difference reshape your study?
?
Take a moment: in your personal journey of seeking knowledge, have you ever treated a method—whether logic, debate, or memorization—as an end in itself, measuring success by mastery of the method rather than by nearness to Allah? What would it cost you to reorient that today?

Two Considerations: Integrating Logic into Kalam
Two considerations explaining the inclusion of logical topics in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two considerations (اعتباران) that distinguish when logical topics are part of kalam and when they are not, and how this refines the answer to the objection.

Core Idea
Logical topics are part of kalam only in the second consideration, for establishing beliefs.

The initial answer established logic as a tool, but a further objection requires a deeper analysis. The author introduces two considerations regarding logical topics: from one perspective they are prerequisites for all proofs and not part of kalam; from another, they are part of kalam specifically for establishing beliefs. This explains their inclusion in kalam books without subordinating kalam to logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two considerations
Read about the two considerations: the first considers logical topics as prerequisites for all proofs (not part of kalam), the second as necessary for establishing beliefs (part of kalam).
The text says: 'ففيه اعتباران'.
2
See how this answers the objection
Notice that logical topics are included in kalam books only under the second consideration, so logic is not higher.
3
Consider the secondary objection
The passage also addresses an objection: if the topics are not part of kalam even by the second consideration, logic would still not be higher. The answer explains that scholars wanted to avoid needing an external science.
The final phrase: 'لأنهم لم يرضوا أن يحتاجوا في علمهم هذا إلى شيء'.

How can you bring your intellectual tools into alignment with your core beliefs so that they serve your faith directly?

What Are Kalam Problems?
Sanusi defines kalam problems and rejects external derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of kalam problems and why they must not be derived from non-religious sciences.

Core Idea
Kalam problems are inherently Islamic; borrowing from non-shar'i sciences is illegitimate.

Sanusi begins by defining what counts as a kalam problem, including states of the non-existent and investigations of reflection. He then strongly rejects the idea that kalam could depend on non-religious sciences for its principles. This lesson establishes the autonomy of kalam as a science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining kalam problems
Identify what Sanusi lists as kalam problems and note his emphatic denial that principles could come from outside.
The comparison to usul al-fiqh needing Arabic is dismissed as invalid.
2
Understand the rejection of external dependence
Sanusi says only a philosopher or pseudo-philosopher would claim kalam needs non-shar'i science. Why?
3
Reflect on the mixing of sciences
Sanusi admits that kalam books may include problems from other sciences, but only for benefit, not necessity.
This shows that kalam is self-sufficient but can enrich content from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي المقاصد من الأخر ( لئلا يلزم الدور) ومما قررناه تبين لك أن أحوال المعدوم، والحال ومباحث النظر والدليل مسائل كلامية وتجويز أن تكون مبادي أعلى علوم الشرع مبينة في علم غير شرعي، وتحتاج بذلك إليه مما لا يجترئ عليه إلا فلسفي، أو متفلسف يلحس فضلات الفلاسفة وتشبيه ذلك باحتياج أصول الفقه إلى العربية مما لا يفوه به محصل فإن وجدت في الكتب الكلامية مسائل لا يتوقف عليها إثبات العقائد أصلاً، ولا دفع الشبه عنها قطعاً فذلك من خلط مسائل علم آخر به تكثيراً للفائدة في الكتاب ( فمنه ( أي من الكلام ( تستمد العلوم الشرعية ( وهو لا يستمد من غيره ) أصلاً فهو رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق لنفاذ حكمه فيها بأسرها، وليس ينفذ فيه حكم شيء منها نعم قد ينفذ حكم بعض منها على بعض آخر فيكون لذلك البعض رياسة. قوله : ( وتجويز إلخ) ذلك رد على العلامة التفتازاني لما في شرح المقاصد أنه يجوز أن يكون من الكلام على تقدير أن يكون موضوعه ذات الله تعالى مبينا في العلم الإلهي، وهو تشنيع قبيح ينبغي أن يصدر مثله عن مميز فضلاً. عن عالم العالم، ومع ذلك يرد عليه أنه إن أراد أنه يلزم احتياج العلم الشرعي إلى غير الشرعي فيما يخالف فيه الشرع، فممنوع وإن أراد إنه يلزم الاحتياج في أمر لم يبينه الشرع فمسلم لكن لا قدح فيه إذا كان ذلك الأمر مما يقبله الشرع والعقل السليم، وساق إليه البرهان القويم فإن الحكمة ضالة المؤمن يأخذها أينما ظفر بها، وهل هذا الامجرد عصبية كيف وقد احتاج الفقه في قسمة التركة ومسائل الوصية، إلى علم الحساب وقال : حجة الإسلام في الإحياء إن تعلمه من فروض الكفاية . قوله : ( مما لا يفوه به محصل بناء على أن العربية من العلوم الشرعية لأن مدونها أهل الشرع، ولا كذلك الإلهي، وقد عرفت أن ذلك مجرد عصبية بقي هاهنا بحث وهو إنه جوز في حواشي مختصر الأصول كما مر كون الكلام، والإلهي محتاجين إلى علم المنطق، ولا يلزم كونه أعلى منهما بناء على أنه لمن يبين فيه مباديهما بل ما يعرض المباديهما، وبذلك يستحق أن يسمى خادماً وآلة لهما، ولا يخفى أن الفرق المذكور تحكم إذ الاحتياج في إثبات المسائل على التقديرين لازم لأن ما يعرض المبادي من الصحة مادة وصورة مما يحتاج إليه في إقامة الدلائل عليهما . قوله: (مسائل لا يتوقف عليها كمسائل الرياضي والحركة والسكون والكيفيات وغير ذلك . قوله : (من خلط إلخ) يعني أنه من فضول الكلام لا تعلق له بعلم الكلام. قوله : (قد ينفذ إلخ) كنفوذ حكم التفسير والحديث في الفقه. قوله : ( فيكون لذلك إلخ) وفيه إنه يلزم أن يكون لعلم النحو واللغة رئاسة على علم قوله : ( فهو رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق هذا كما ذكره لكن ها هنا مسألة لا يخلو إنه ذكر صاحب القنية وغيره من الثقات في حق ترتيب الكتب، التنبيه عليها عن الفائدة، وهي بحسب الوضع أن اللغة والنحو نوع واحد فيوضع بعضها فوق بعض والتعبير فوقهما والكلام فوق ذلك، والفقه فوق الكلام والأخبار والمواعظ والدعوات المروية فوق ذلك والتفسير فوق ذلك .
The first observatory - the fifth aim: Its problems which are the aims from the other (to avoid circularity). And from what we have established, it is clear to you that the states of the non-existent, the state, and investigations of reflection and proof are kalam problems. And the allowing that the principles of the highest religious sciences could be explained in a non-religious science, and thereby needing it, is something none dares except a philosopher or pseudo-philosopher who licks the leftovers of the philosophers. And likening that to the need of usul al-fiqh for Arabic is something no scholar would utter. If there are found in kalam books problems that do not depend on establishing beliefs at all, nor definitively warding off doubts, that is from mixing problems of another science with it to increase benefit in the book. For from it (i.e. from kalam) the religious sciences derive, and it does not derive from anything else at all. So it is the chief of the religious sciences absolutely, because its judgment permeates all of them, and no judgment of any of them permeates it. Indeed, the judgment of some of them may permeate others, so that one has a chiefship. His statement: 'And the allowing, etc.' is a refutation of al-Allama al-Taftazani regarding his statement in Sharh al-Maqasid that it is permissible for kalam, assuming its subject is the essence of Allah, to be explained in divine science. That is an ugly condemnation that should not issue from a discerning person, let alone a scholar of the world. Moreover, it is rebutted: if he means that a religious science needs a non-religious science in what the religious law disagrees, it is denied; if he means need in a matter not explained by the law, it is granted but not objectionable if that matter is accepted by law and sound intellect—he brought a sound proof for it, for wisdom is the lost property of the believer, he takes it wherever he finds it. Is this mere partisanship? How, when fiqh needed arithmetic in dividing inheritance and wills, and Hujjat al-Islam in al-Ihya said learning it is a communal obligation. His statement: 'what no scholar utters' is based on Arabic being a religious science because its codifiers are people of the law, not so the divine science. And you have known that is mere partisanship. There remains a discussion: he permitted in the glosses on Mukhtasar al-Usul, as passed, that kalam and divine science need logic without it being higher than them, because it does not explain their principles but rather what pertains to their principles. Thereby it deserves to be called servant and instrument for them. It is not hidden that the mentioned distinction is arbitrary because the need in establishing problems in both cases is necessary, for what pertains to principles—soundness of matter and form—is needed in establishing proofs for them. His statement: 'problems that do not depend on it, like problems of mathematics, motion, rest, qualities, etc.' His statement: 'from mixing, etc.' means it is idle talk unrelated to kalam. His statement: 'may permeate, etc.' like the permeation of the judgment of tafsir and hadith in fiqh. His statement: 'so that one has, etc.' and in it, it follows that grammar and language have chiefship over... His statement: 'so it is the chief of the religious sciences absolutely' as he mentioned, but here is a matter not devoid of benefit: the author of al-Quniya and other trustworthy authorities mentioned regarding the order of books, which is that language and grammar are one type; they place some above others, and expression (or Tafsir?) above them, and kalam above that, and fiqh above kalam, and narrations, sermons and transmitted supplications above that, and tafsir above that.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a composite of assertions and refutations regarding kalam's independence and its rank. It begins with a conclusion that states of non-existent (ma'dum), state (hal), and investigations of reflection and proof are kalam problems. It then strongly rejects the idea that the principles of the highest religious sciences could be taken from a non-religious science, calling it a philosophical excess. It compares the need of usul al-fiqh for Arabic unfavorably to this claim. The text then asserts that kalam is the source from which all religious sciences derive, and it derives from nothing else; thus it is the absolute chief. However, some sciences like tafsir and hadith may have jurisdiction over fiqh, but not over kalam. A long refutation of al-Taftazani follows, arguing that his allowance for kalam to be explained in divine science is an error. The author then addresses the earlier discussion about logic, stating that the distinction that logic only explains what pertains to principles is arbitrary because both cases require the same need. Finally, the passage notes a different ordering of sciences according to some authorities, placing tafsir at the top, then hadith, then fiqh, then kalam, and then language, showing that kalam is not always considered the highest in all contexts.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الحال
مباحث النظر
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about the possibility of kalam's principles being explained in a non-religious science?
A) It is allowed by some scholars.
B) It is something only a philosopher would dare.
C) It is common practice.
D) It is required for completeness.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is fully self-sufficient and does not need non-religious sciences for its principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself relying on a non-Islamic intellectual framework to defend or understand a matter of creed? What part of Sanusi's warning applies most directly to you?
?
Sanusi likens the claim that kalam needs philosophy to the claim that usul al-fiqh needs Arabic, calling it something no scholar would utter. Where in your own thinking do you unconsciously borrow from non-Islamic sources for Islamic truths? Will you cut that root today?
?
If you were to 'lick the leftovers of the philosophers' in some aspect of your faith—whether in theology, ethics, or practice—what would it be? What step will you take to ensure your methodology remains purely from revelation and the early scholars?

Method of Learning

Kalam's Chiefship Among Religious Sciences
Sanusi declares kalam the absolute chief of religious sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam is considered the chief of religious sciences and what that means for its relation to other sciences.

Core Idea
Kalam is the source and judge of all religious sciences; no science judges it.

After establishing kalam's independence, Sanusi asserts its supreme rank. He explains that kalam is the source from which all religious sciences derive, and it derives from nothing else. This lesson explores that absolute chiefship and the limited chiefship some sciences have over others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on kalam's chiefship
Notice the strong language: 'it is the chief of the religious sciences absolutely'.
2
Understand 'permeation' of judgment
Sanusi says kalam's judgment permeates all religious sciences, but no judgment of any other science permeates it.
This is why it is chief.
3
Consider the exception
Sanusi notes that some sciences may have judgment over others (e.g., tafsir over fiqh), but this does not affect kalam's absolute chiefship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي المقاصد من الأخر ( لئلا يلزم الدور) ومما قررناه تبين لك أن أحوال المعدوم، والحال ومباحث النظر والدليل مسائل كلامية وتجويز أن تكون مبادي أعلى علوم الشرع مبينة في علم غير شرعي، وتحتاج بذلك إليه مما لا يجترئ عليه إلا فلسفي، أو متفلسف يلحس فضلات الفلاسفة وتشبيه ذلك باحتياج أصول الفقه إلى العربية مما لا يفوه به محصل فإن وجدت في الكتب الكلامية مسائل لا يتوقف عليها إثبات العقائد أصلاً، ولا دفع الشبه عنها قطعاً فذلك من خلط مسائل علم آخر به تكثيراً للفائدة في الكتاب ( فمنه ( أي من الكلام ( تستمد العلوم الشرعية ( وهو لا يستمد من غيره ) أصلاً فهو رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق لنفاذ حكمه فيها بأسرها، وليس ينفذ فيه حكم شيء منها نعم قد ينفذ حكم بعض منها على بعض آخر فيكون لذلك البعض رياسة. قوله : ( وتجويز إلخ) ذلك رد على العلامة التفتازاني لما في شرح المقاصد أنه يجوز أن يكون من الكلام على تقدير أن يكون موضوعه ذات الله تعالى مبينا في العلم الإلهي، وهو تشنيع قبيح ينبغي أن يصدر مثله عن مميز فضلاً. عن عالم العالم، ومع ذلك يرد عليه أنه إن أراد أنه يلزم احتياج العلم الشرعي إلى غير الشرعي فيما يخالف فيه الشرع، فممنوع وإن أراد إنه يلزم الاحتياج في أمر لم يبينه الشرع فمسلم لكن لا قدح فيه إذا كان ذلك الأمر مما يقبله الشرع والعقل السليم، وساق إليه البرهان القويم فإن الحكمة ضالة المؤمن يأخذها أينما ظفر بها، وهل هذا الامجرد عصبية كيف وقد احتاج الفقه في قسمة التركة ومسائل الوصية، إلى علم الحساب وقال : حجة الإسلام في الإحياء إن تعلمه من فروض الكفاية . قوله : ( مما لا يفوه به محصل بناء على أن العربية من العلوم الشرعية لأن مدونها أهل الشرع، ولا كذلك الإلهي، وقد عرفت أن ذلك مجرد عصبية بقي هاهنا بحث وهو إنه جوز في حواشي مختصر الأصول كما مر كون الكلام، والإلهي محتاجين إلى علم المنطق، ولا يلزم كونه أعلى منهما بناء على أنه لمن يبين فيه مباديهما بل ما يعرض المباديهما، وبذلك يستحق أن يسمى خادماً وآلة لهما، ولا يخفى أن الفرق المذكور تحكم إذ الاحتياج في According to the passage, why is kalam the absolute chief of religious sciences?
A) Because it uses philosophical methods.
B) Because it is the source from which they derive and its judgment permeates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own religious learning, have you ever treated theology (kalam) as optional or secondary to practical rulings? What convinced you that belief must come first?
?
If kalam's judgment permeates all religious sciences, then every conclusion you reach in fiqh or tafsir is ultimately built on your theological foundation. Are you actively building that foundation? What is one concrete step you can take this week to study kalam more seriously?
?
The text says kalam does not derive from any other science, yet many Muslims today treat hadith or fiqh as the ultimate authority. In what ways have you, perhaps unknowingly, given other sciences more weight than kalam? How can you realign your priorities starting now?

Refuting Taftazani: Kalam's Independence
Sanusi refutes Taftazani on kalam's need for divine science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of Taftazani's claim that kalam could be explained in divine science.

Core Idea
Kalam does not need divine science; even if it did, it would be permissible.

Sanusi responds to al-Allama al-Taftazani, who suggested that kalam might be explained in divine science. Sanusi strongly rejects this, arguing that kalam is independent and that any need for other sciences is only in matters not already clarified by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Understand Taftazani's position
Taftazani said it is permissible for kalam to be explained in divine science (al-ilahiyyat) because its subject is Allah.
2
Sanusi's rebuttal
Sanusi says if Taftazani means a need in what the law disagrees, it is false; if in what the law hasn't explained, it is fine.
He cites wisdom as a lost property of the believer.
3
Analyze the example of fiqh and arithmetic
Fiqh needed arithmetic in inheritance; thus the need for a non-shar'i science is acceptable.
This shows the author's view is not absolute prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : مسائله التي المقاصد من الأخر ( لئلا يلزم الدور) ومما قررناه تبين لك أن أحوال المعدوم، والحال ومباحث النظر والدليل مسائل كلامية وتجويز أن تكون مبادي أعلى علوم الشرع مبينة في علم غير شرعي، وتحتاج بذلك إليه مما لا يجترئ عليه إلا فلسفي، أو متفلسف يلحس فضلات الفلاسفة وتشبيه ذلك باحتياج أصول الفقه إلى العربية مما لا يفوه به محصل فإن وجدت في الكتب الكلامية مسائل لا يتوقف : ( مما لا يفوه به محصل بناء على أن العربية من العلوم الشرعية لأن مدونها أهل الشرع، ولا كذلك الإلهي، وقد عرفت أن ذلك مجرد عصبية بقي هاهنا بحث وهو إنه جوز في حواشي مختصر الأصول كما مر كون الكلام، والإلهي محتاجين إلى علم المنطق، ولا يلزم كونه أعلى منهما بناء على أنه لمن يبين فيه مباديهما بل ما يعرض المباديهما، وبذلك يستحق أن يسمى خادماً وآلة لهما، ولا يخفى أن الفرق المذكور تحكم إذ الاحتياج في قوله : ( فهو رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق هذا كما ذكره لكن ها هنا مسألة لا يخلو إنه ذكر صاحب القنية وغيره من الثقات في حق ترتيب الكتب، التنبيه عليها عن الفائدة، وهي بحسب الوضع أن اللغة والنحو نوع واحد فيوضع بعضها فوق بعض والتعبير فوقهما والكلام فوق ذلك، والفقه فوق الكلام والأخبار والمواعظ والدعوات المروية فوق ذلك والتفسير فوق ذلك .
The first observatory - the fifth aim: Its problems which are the aims from the other (to avoid circularity). And from what we have established, it is clear to you that the states of the non-existent, the state, and investigations of reflection and proof are kalam problems. And the allowing that the principles of the highest religious sciences could be explained in a non-religious science, and thereby needing it, is something none dares except a philosopher or pseudo-philosopher who licks the leftovers of the philosophers. And likening that to the need of usul al-fiqh for Arabic is something no scholar would utter. If there are found in kalam books problems that do not depend on establishing beliefs at all, nor definitively warding off doubts, that is from mixing problems of another science with it to increase benefit in the book. For from it (i.e. from kalam) the religious sciences derive, and it does not derive from anything else at all. So it is the chief of the religious sciences absolutely, because its judgment permeates all of them, and no judgment of any of them permeates it. Indeed, the judgment of some of them may permeate others, so that one has a chiefship. His statement: 'And the allowing, etc.' is a refutation of al-Allama al-Taftazani regarding his statement in Sharh al-Maqasid that it is permissible for kalam, assuming its subject is the essence of Allah, to be explained in divine science. That is an ugly condemnation that should not issue from a discerning person, let alone a scholar of the world. Moreover, it is rebutted: if he means that a religious science needs a non-religious science in what the religious law disagrees, it is denied; if he means need in a matter not explained by the law, it is granted but not objectionable if that matter is accepted by law and sound intellect—he brought a sound proof for it, for wisdom is the lost property of the believer, he takes it wherever he finds it. Is this mere partisanship? How, when fiqh needed arithmetic in dividing inheritance and wills, and Hujjat al-Islam in al-Ihya said learning it is a communal obligation. His statement: 'what no scholar utters' is based on Arabic being a religious science because its codifiers are people of the law, not so the divine science. And you have known that is mere partisanship. There remains a discussion: he permitted in the glosses on Mukhtasar al-Usul, as passed, that kalam and divine science need logic without it being higher than them, because it does not explain their principles but rather what pertains to their principles. Thereby it deserves to be called servant and instrument for them. It is not hidden that the mentioned distinction is arbitrary because the need in establishing problems in both cases is necessary, for what pertains to principles—soundness of matter and form—is needed in establishing proofs for them. His statement: 'problems that do not depend on it, like problems of mathematics, motion, rest, qualities, etc.' His statement: 'from mixing, etc.' means it is idle talk unrelated to kalam. His statement: 'may permeate, etc.' like the permeation of the judgment of tafsir and hadith in fiqh. His statement: 'so that one has, etc.' and in it, it follows that grammar and language have chiefship over... His statement: 'so it is the chief of the religious sciences absolutely' as he mentioned, but here is a matter not devoid of benefit: the author of al-Quniya and other trustworthy authorities mentioned regarding the order of books, which is that language and grammar are one type; they place some above others, and expression (or Tafsir?) above them, and kalam above that, and fiqh above kalam, and narrations, sermons and transmitted supplications above that, and tafsir above that.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a composite of assertions and refutations regarding kalam's independence and its rank. It begins with a conclusion that states of non-existent (ma'dum), state (hal), and investigations of reflection and proof are kalam problems. It then strongly rejects the idea that the principles of the highest religious sciences could be taken from a non-religious science, calling it a philosophical excess. It compares the need of usul al-fiqh for Arabic unfavorably to this claim. The text then asserts that kalam is the source from which all religious sciences derive, and it derives from nothing else; thus it is the absolute chief. However, some sciences like tafsir and hadith may have jurisdiction over fiqh, but not over kalam. A long refutation of al-Taftazani follows, arguing that his allowance for kalam to be explained in divine science is an error. The author then addresses the earlier discussion about logic, stating that the distinction that logic only explains what pertains to principles is arbitrary because both cases require the same need. Finally, the passage notes a different ordering of sciences according to some authorities, placing tafsir at the top, then hadith, then fiqh, then kalam, and then language, showing that kalam is not always considered the highest in all contexts.

Key Terms
التفتازاني
العلم الإلهي
الحكمة ضالة المؤمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi give to show that a religious science can need a non-religious science?
A) Usul al-fiqh needing Arabic.
B) Fiqh needing arithmetic for inheritance.
C) Tafsir needing history.
D) Hadith needing biography.

Key Takeaway
Kalam can use other sciences only when the law is silent and the intellect accepts it, as fiqh uses arithmetic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a valuable insight simply because it came from a source you considered 'non-Islamic'? What would it mean for you to treat wisdom as a lost property, regardless of its origin?
?
The lesson shows that even the great scholar Taftazani made an 'ugly condemnation' in Sanusi's view. Is there a scholar or a teaching you have rejected without first understanding their full argument? Will you go back and examine it with fairness?
?
Sanusi uses the example of fiqh needing arithmetic—a practical dependence. In your own journey to understand Islam, what 'arithmetic' have you avoided learning out of fear that it might corrupt your faith? What step will you take today to reclaim that wisdom?

Logic and the Hierarchy of Sciences
Sanusi discusses logic as servant and reports an alternative hierarchy of sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of logic as a servant to kalam and the alternative ordering of sciences proposed by some scholars.

Core Idea
Logic serves kalam; some scholars place tafsir above kalam.

Sanusi addresses two issues: first, that kalam and divine science need logic but logic is not superior; it is a servant. Second, he presents a different hierarchy from al-Quniya, placing tafsir at the top, then hadith, fiqh, kalam, and language. This shows that the absolute chiefship of kalam is not universally agreed upon.

Guided Path
1
Read about logic's role
Sanusi argues that logic is needed but only as a tool, not as a higher science.
He criticizes the distinction as arbitrary.
2
Understand the alternative hierarchy
Note the order: language/nahw, then tafsir (or expression?), then kalam, then fiqh, then hadith/sermons, then tafsir at top.
This contradicts the earlier claim of kalam's absolute chiefship.
3
Reconcile the two views
Sanusi does not resolve the contradiction; he simply reports the other view.
This shows the complexity of ranking sciences.

The Eight Bases: Inclusion vs. Exclusivity
Commentary on al-Amidi's classification and the scope of the eight bases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between claiming that the eight bases encompass all issues and claiming that every base contains only kalam issues.

Core Idea
Inclusion does not imply exclusivity – not every topic in the bases is a kalam issue.

Al-Amidi's Abkar al-Afkar is famous for its systematic classification of kalam issues into eight bases. But a careful reading shows that not every discussion within those bases is itself a theological issue. The commentator here teases apart what the claim of 'inclusion' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the text is a critical commentary on a quote from al-Amidi.
The passage is incomplete, but the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the two claims
Distinguish between the apparent meaning (the eight bases include all issues) and the necessary implication (that every base contains only kalam issues). The commentator accepts the first but denies the second.
The key phrase: 'أما اللزوم فلا' — 'but as for the implication, no.'
3
Understand the objection and rebuttal
The commentator addresses an objection that might confuse the inclusion of topics in the bases with their being kalam issues. Reflect on why the objection is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفي أبكار الأفكار للآمدي تصريح بذلك نقل عنه رحمه الله أن التصريح نظراً إلى ) الظاهر مسلم، وأما اللزوم فلا إذ اللازم عدم خروج المسائل عن تلك القواعد، وأما كون كل قاعدة منها مشتملة عليها فلا، وما قيل من أن تشريك الكل في العنوان أولاً، وتعيين كل منها لبيان ما ببين فيه مع كون البعض من المسائل قطعاً بلا إشارة إلى تمييز بين ما هو منها قطعاً، وبين ما
His statement: (And in Abkar al-Afkar by al-Amidi there is a clear statement of that. It is transmitted from him, may God have mercy on him, that the statement looking to ) the apparent is accepted; but as for the implication, no, because the necessary consequence is that the issues do not exit from those bases. But that every base is inclusive of them, no. And what has been said that the inclusion of all in the title first, and the specification of each to clarify what is clarified in it, while some of the issues are certain without an indication of a distinction between what is certainly from them and what
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a quote from al-Amidi's Abkar al-Afkar. The commentator examines the claim that the eight bases (qawa'id) in that work encompass all issues of the principles (usul). He grants that the apparent meaning is sound, but denies that every base necessarily contains only kalam issues. The necessary consequence is that issues do not escape those bases, but that does not mean each base is fully inclusive. He then addresses a potential objection: one might say that all bases are included under the title 'issues' from the start, and each base is designated to explain what is explained therein, even though some issues are clearly part of the bases without any indication distinguishing them from others. The text breaks off here, but the argument appears to rebut the suggestion that everything in those bases is a kalam issue. The commentator seems to be saying that the mere inclusion of a topic in the eight bases does not automatically make it a theological issue (mas'ala kalamiyya); the classification is more nuanced.

الآمدي
قواعد
مسائل الأصول
تصريح

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All topics discussed within the eight bases of Abkar al-Afkar are necessarily kalam issues.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the eight bases encompass all issues, but that does not mean every base is exclusively about kalam issues; some topics may be methodological or other types.

Key Takeaway
The eight bases encompass all kalam issues, but that does not mean every base is exclusively about kalam issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a topic like the eight bases, do you ever assume that everything within it aligns perfectly with your preconceived notions? Or do you approach it with the humility to accept that inclusion does not mean uniformity? Consider how this principle applies to your own understanding of Islamic theology — where have you been guilty of overextending an implication?
?
The commentator accepts the apparent meaning but denies the implication. In your own reading of religious texts, do you carefully distinguish between what is explicitly stated and what you infer? Have you ever built a belief on an implication that the text does not actually warrant? Take a moment to examine one belief you hold that might be based on implication rather than explicit statement.
?
The passage reminds us that a general category (like the eight bases) can contain diverse elements. Think about the way you categorize people, ideas, or yourself. Are you quick to assume that because something falls under a certain label, it must possess all attributes of that label? How has this tendency affected your relationships or your self-perception? Commit to one area where you will stop assuming exclusivity from inclusion.
