Introduction to the Second Observation: The Five Necessary Investigations
Enumerates five investigations and defends their status as kalam issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five investigations that a mutakallim must undertake and why they are considered essential kalam issues rather than external preliminaries.

Core Idea
The five observations are not just preliminaries but are themselves kalam issues required for establishing doctrines.

The author now moves from the introductory First Observation into the core of his work. He begins by laying out five fundamental investigations that every mutakallim must master. These investigations are not merely preliminary steps; they are integral parts of kalam itself because they are necessary for establishing the doctrines of faith. Let's see what these investigations are and why they matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's main arguments.
Notice the enumeration of five investigations.
2
Identify the five investigations
The passage lists five things the mutakallim must do. Find each one and note how they relate to each other.
These are: (1) quiddity of knowledge, (2) division into necessary/acquired, (3) establishing necessary sciences, (4) states of reflection, (5) path of reflection.
3
Understand why they are kalam issues
The author argues these investigations are not mere preliminaries but are themselves kalam issues. Look for his reasoning: because all acquired propositions needed for doctrines are made part of kalam.
He cites al-Amidi to support the view that these are included among kalam issues.
4
Address the apparent contradiction
The passage then resolves a potential contradiction: the author previously called the First Stand 'preliminaries', but now says the First Observation is the only preliminary. Note how the commentator explains this: the First Observation is an absolute preliminary, while the others are preliminary in one aspect and objectives in another.
This shows the layered nature of kalam's structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني : في تعريف مطلق العلم
من هاهنا في مقاصد علم الكلام، وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمةً للشروع فيه، ولا بد للمتكلم من تحقيق ماهية العلم أولاً، ومن بيان انقسامه إلى ضروري ومكتسب ثانياً، ومن الإشارة إلى ثبوت العلوم الضرورية التي إليها المنتهى ثالثاً، ومن بيان أحوال النظر وإفادته للعلم رابعاً، ومن بيان الطريق الذي يقع فيه النظر ويوصل إلى المطلوب خامساً ، إذ بهذه المباحث يتوصل إلى إثبات العقائد، وإثبات مباحث أخرى تتوقف عليها العقائد وقد عرفت أنه قد جعل جميع ما يتوقف عليه إثبات العقائد من القضايا المكتسبة مقاصد في علمه كيلا يحتاج فيه إلى علم آخر، فالمباحث المذكورة في هذه المراصد الخمسة مسائل كلامية، وفي أبكار الأفكار تصريح بذلك حيث جعله مشتملاً على ثماني قواعد متضمنة لجميع مسائل الأصول. ( قوله : مسائل كلامية من وجه ومباد من وجه فلا ينافيه قوله: الموقف الأول في المقدمات لأن المراد منها ما يتوقف عليه جميع ما عداها إما شروعاً كما في المرصد الأول : أو ، ذاتاً كما في هذه المراصد الخمسة. قوله : (تصريح إلخ) إذ لا يقال : إن الثمانية متضمنة لمسائل الأصول باعتبار تضمن خمسة منها، فلا بد أن تكون القواعد الثلاثة أيضاً متضمنة لمسائله والقول بأن بعض ما يذكر فيها مسائل دون بعض تحكم لم يقل به أحد، فيكون جميع ما يذكر فيها مسائل كلامية، فافهم فإنه زل فيه أقدام.
قوله: (وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمة الشروع فإن قلت: كلام الشارح ها هنا يناقض قول المصنف فيما سبق الموقف الأول في المقدمات، وفيه مراصد فإنه يدل على أن كل المراصد من المقدمة وقول الشارح يدل على أن المقدمة هي المرصد الأول فقط قلت : المرصد الأول مقدمة على الإطلاق، والمراصد الخمسة الباقية مقدمة من وجه، ومقاصد من وجه فمراد الشارح بالمقدمة على الإطلاق أعني مقدمة الشروع، ومراد المصنف أعم من ذلك فلا تناقض .
قوله : ( وفي أبكار الأفكار للآمدي تصريح بذلك نقل عنه رحمه الله أن التصريح نظراً إلى ) الظاهر مسلم، وأما اللزوم فلا إذ اللازم عدم خروج المسائل عن تلك القواعد، وأما كون كل قاعدة منها مشتملة عليها فلا، وما قيل من أن تشريك الكل في العنوان أولاً، وتعيين كل منها لبيان ما ببين فيه مع كون البعض من المسائل قطعاً بلا إشارة إلى تمييز بين ما هو منها قطعاً، وبين ما
The Second Observation: On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. From here onward are the objectives of the science of kalam, and what preceded in the First Observation was an introduction to commencing it. It is necessary for the mutakallim first to investigate the quiddity of knowledge, second to explain its division into necessary and acquired, third to indicate the establishment of necessary sciences which are the ultimate resort, fourth to explain the states of reflection and its yielding of knowledge, and fifth to explain the path in which reflection occurs and which leads to the desired object. For by these investigations one arrives at establishing the doctrines and establishing other investigations on which the doctrines depend. And you have known that he made all the acquired propositions on which the establishment of doctrines depends into objectives in his science so that it does not need another science. Thus the investigations mentioned in these five observations are kalam issues. And in Abkar al-Afkar there is an explicit statement of that, where he made it inclusive of eight principles containing all the issues of the fundamentals. (His statement: 'issues from one aspect and principles from another aspect' does not contradict his statement: 'The First Stand: on the preliminaries', because what is meant by them are things on which all others depend, either for commencement as in the First Observation, or essentially as in these five observations. His statement: 'an explicit statement etc.' — for it is not said that the eight are inclusive of the issues of the fundamentals by virtue of five of them being inclusive, so necessarily the three principles also include its issues. And the claim that some of what is mentioned in them are issues while others are not is arbitrary and no one has said it. Thus all that is mentioned in them are kalam issues, so understand, for feet have slipped therein. His statement: 'And what preceded in the First Observation was an introduction to commencement' — if you say: The words of the commentator here contradict the words of the author in what preceded: 'The First Stand: on the preliminaries' and it contains observations, for it indicates that all the observations are from the preliminary, while the words of the commentator indicate that the preliminary is only the First Observation. I say: The First Observation is an absolute preliminary, and the remaining five observations are preliminary in one aspect and objectives in another aspect. So what the commentator means by 'absolute preliminary' is the preliminary of commencement, and what the author means is more general than that, so there is no contradiction. His statement: 'And in Abkar al-Afkar of al-Amidi there is an explicit statement of that' — it is narrated from him, may Allah have mercy on him, that the explicit statement, upon consideration, is evidently conceded, but as for the implication, it is not, for the necessary consequence is that the issues do not exit from those principles, but that each principle includes them is not conceded. And what is said about including all in the title first, and specifying each to explain what is explained in it, while some of them are certainly issues with no indication to distinguish between what is certainly from them and what is from...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the Second Observation (al-marṣad al-thānī) of the work, which focuses on defining absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq). The author begins by explaining that this observation is part of the main objectives (maqāṣid) of kalam, while the First Observation was merely an introduction to commence the study. He then enumerates five necessary investigations for the mutakallim: (1) investigating the quiddity of knowledge, (2) dividing knowledge into necessary (ḍarūrī) and acquired (muktasab), (3) establishing the necessary sciences, (4) examining the states of reflection (naẓar) and how it yields knowledge, and (5) explaining the path of reflection leading to the desired conclusion. These five observations are themselves kalam issues (masāʾil kalāmiyya), not merely preliminary principles (mabādiʾ), because all of them are necessary for establishing doctrines (ʿaqāʾid) and the author intentionally made them part of kalam to avoid reliance on another science. The passage then addresses a potential contradiction: the author previously called the First Stand (al-mawqif al-awwal) 'preliminaries' (muqaddimāt), which included observations, while the commentator here says the First Observation is the only preliminary. The resolution is that the First Observation is a preliminary in an absolute sense (for commencing), while the remaining five are preliminary in one aspect (as foundations) but also objectives in another. Finally, the passage cites al-Amidi's Abkār al-Afkār, which confirms that the eight principles enumerated therein encompass all issues of the fundamentals, including these observations, thereby affirming their status as kalam issues. The text ends abruptly due to OCR truncation.

الماهية
الضروري
المكتسب
النظر
المقاصد
المقدمات
المسائل الكلامية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think the five observations are merely external preliminaries, not genuine kalam issues.
Correction: The author argues they are kalam issues because they are required to establish doctrines and were deliberately included in the science of kalam to avoid reliance on other disciplines.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the investigations in the five observations considered kalam issues rather than mere preliminaries?
A) Because they are necessary for establishing doctrines and were made part of kalam to avoid reliance on another science.
B) Because the author explicitly calls them 'observations' which is a kalam term.
C) Because they are based on the eight principles of al-Amidi.
D) Because they are all acquired propositions.

Key Takeaway
The five investigations outlined in the Second Observation are not just preliminaries but are themselves kalam issues essential for establishing doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever critically examined the foundations of your own beliefs—the very 'first principles' you take for granted? The mutakallim is commanded to investigate the quiddity of knowledge itself. Are you willing to ask yourself: on what basis do I claim to know anything about Allah? What is 'necessary' and what is 'acquired' in your own faith journey? Do not evade—let the five investigations guide your self‑audit this week.
?
You have just read that reflection (النظر) is the path that leads to the desired object—the truth. But where in your daily life do you actually engage in disciplined reflection about your Creator? Not idle thoughts, but structured reasoning like the one this passage outlines. Will you carve out time today to follow even one of these investigations in your heart, or will you remain satisfied with inherited assumptions?
?
The passage warns that 'feet have slipped' regarding which matters are truly kalam issues and which are preliminaries. In your own search for truth, are you confusing the scaffolding with the building? Have you ever dismissed a foundational question as 'just a preliminary' when it actually determines your entire worldview? Confess to yourself one such question you have neglected, and commit to facing it this week.

Defining Knowledge: Difficulty and Alternative Methods
Al-Ghazali's argument on the difficulty of defining knowledge and the sufficiency of division and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ghazali considered a complete definition of knowledge difficult, and how division and example serve as sufficient alternatives for understanding.

Core Idea
Division and example can provide knowledge even without a full logical definition.

Al-Ghazali famously argued that formulating a precise definition encompassing genus and differentia is extremely difficult, even for sensory objects. How then could we define knowledge, a subtle perception? Yet he insists that we can know what knowledge is through other means: division and example. This lesson explores that argument and its implications for methodology in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once, then its English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the reference to al-Mustasfa.
2
Understand the difficulty of definition
Al-Ghazali states that defining knowledge with a precise expression including genus and essential differentia is difficult, even for most sensory objects.
This establishes why alternative methods are needed.
3
See how division and example work
He then says that division cuts off confusion and example by the outer eye helps grasp its reality. These are the two methods that suffice.
Division and example are not full definitions but still give understanding.
4
Recognize the conditions for a true definition
The passage explains that something can be a way to knowledge without being a definition if it lacks the conditions of being evident in all its individuals and absent from all non-individuals.
This clarifies why division and example are not definitions.
5
Reflect on the common view
Despite the common belief that division yields definitions and example leads to definition by description, neither is absolutely so.
This reinforces the argument that they are sufficient but not strict definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الإمام الغزالي رحمه الله تعالى صرح في المستصفى بأنه يعسر تحديد العلم بعبارة محررة جامعة للجنس والفصل الذاتيين، فإن ذلك متعسر في أكثر الأشياء بل في أكثر المدركات الحسية، فكيف لا يعسر في الإدراكات الخفية. ثم قال: إن التقسيم المذكور يقطع العلم عن مظان الاشتباه، والتمثيل بإدراك الباصرة يفهمك حقيقته. فظهر أن شيءاً قد يكون طريقاً إلى معرفة شيء من غير أن يكون معرفاً له لانتفاء شرائطه، وهو كونه بين الثبوت في جميع أفراده بين الانتفاء عما عداه. وإن اشتهر بينهم من أن القسمة الحقيقية لانطوائها على ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز يعرف منها تعريفات الأقسام، وإن مآل المثال إلى التعريف الرسمي، ليس شيء منهما على إطلاقه.
Know that Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, declared in al-Mustasfa that it is difficult to define knowledge with a precise expression encompassing the genus and the essential differentia, for that is difficult in most things, even in most sense-perceptibles, so how would it not be difficult in the subtle perceptions? Then he said: The aforementioned division cuts off knowledge from the sources of confusion, and the example by perception of the outer eye makes you understand its reality. So it became clear that something may be a way to knowledge of something without being a definition of it due to the absence of its conditions, namely being evident in all its individuals and evident in its negation from what is other than it. And even if it is well-known among them that true division, because it contains what is shared and what is distinctive, yields definitions of the divisions, and that the example leads to a definition by description, neither of them is absolutely that.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage addresses the difficulty of giving a complete definition of knowledge. It cites al-Ghazali from his work al-Mustasfa, where he states that formulating a precise definition that includes the essential genus and differentia is difficult for most things, even for sense-perceptible objects, so it is even more difficult for subtle mental perceptions like knowledge. Nevertheless, al-Ghazali says that the division (qisma) mentioned earlier cuts off knowledge from sources of confusion, and the example of perception of the outer eye makes you understand its reality. The commentary then draws the conclusion that something can be a way to knowledge of something without being a formal definition (ta'rif) because it may lack the necessary conditions, such as being perfectly evident in all its instances and absent from all non-instances. This is despite the common view that true division contains the basis for defining the divisions, and that example leads to a definition by description (rasm). The point is that neither division nor example in this context functions as a full logical definition, but they still provide understanding.

Key Terms
التحديد
الجنس
الفصل الذاتيين
المستصفى
التقسيم
التمثيل
التعريف الرسمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division and example are useless for understanding knowledge.
Correction: Al-Ghazali argues they actually cut off confusion and provide understanding.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ghazali say it is difficult to define knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is a simple concept that cannot be defined
B) Because most things, even sensory objects, are difficult to define with a precise expression
C) Because knowledge has no genus or differentia
D) Because he rejects all definitions

Key Takeaway
Even without a perfect logical definition, knowledge can be sufficiently understood through division and example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed accepting a truth in your faith because you felt you needed a perfect explanation? What would it take for you to trust that division and example are enough?
?
Think of a concept in your life—like 'knowledge' or 'faith'—that you struggle to define. How can al-Ghazali's method of division and example help you appreciate it more deeply, even without a rigorous definition?
?
If you can know something without defining it perfectly, what does that say about your relationship with Allah's attributes? Are you willing to act on that knowledge now, even if your understanding is incomplete?

Method of Learning

Proof by Reduction to Circularity or Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents a reductio proof for necessary knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) proves that some knowledge must be necessary (daruri) rather than purely theoretical (nazari).

Core Idea
If all knowledge were theoretical, acquisition would lead to an impossible loop or endless chain.

Sanusi introduces a second proof for necessary knowledge, moving from intuitive necessity to a logical argument. By assuming the opposite — that every conceptualization and assent is theoretical — he shows that any attempt to acquire knowledge would trigger either a circular dependency or an infinite series, both of which are impossible. Therefore, some knowledge must be foundational.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
The proof begins by assuming that all conceptualizations and assents are theoretical (nazari) — that is, acquired through reasoning rather than self-evident.
2
Trace the dependency chain
If every piece of knowledge is theoretical, then to acquire any one piece, we must depend on another piece, which in turn is theoretical, creating a chain of dependencies.
3
Identify the two impossible outcomes
This chain must either loop back on itself (circularity) or continue infinitely (infinite regress). Both are impossible, so the assumption that all knowledge is theoretical must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإذ لولاه لزم الدور أو التسلسل ) استدلال على المدعى بعد التنزل عن دعوى الضرورة الوجدانية. وحينئذ يكون كل واحد من التصور، وكذا كل واحد من التصديق نظرياً، فإذا حاولنا تحصيل شيء منهما كان ذلك التحصيل مستنداً إلى تصور أو تصديق آخر، وهو أيضاً نظري مستند إلى غيره من التصورات أو التصديقات، فإما أن يدور الاستناد في مرتبة من المراتب أو يتسلسل إلى ما لا يتناهى، وهما يمنعان الاكتساب لأنهما باطلان ممتنعان كما سيأتي، فما يتوقف عليهما كان باطلاً ممتنعاً. ثم ذكر الشارح أن لزوم الدور والتسلسل إنما يتم في التصورات مطلقاً، وفي التصديقات إذا امتنع اكتسابها من التصورات. واعترض عليه بأن التصديق بمناسبة المبادي للمطالب مما لا بد منه، وهو نظري على تقدير نظرية جميع التصديقات، فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل وإن جوزنا اكتساب التصديق من التصورات. والجواب أن اللازم في الاكتساب نفس المناسبة لا العلم بالمناسبة فيجوز أن يكتسب التصديق من تصور يكون مناسباً لذلك التصديق وإن لم يعلم مناسبته له.
His saying: ('And if not for that, circularity or infinite regress would be necessary') is a proof for the claim after descending from the claim of intuitive necessity. And then every single conceptualization and likewise every single assent would be theoretical. So if we attempt to acquire something from them, that acquisition would depend on another conceptualization or assent, which is also theoretical dependent on others among conceptualizations or assents. Then either the dependence circles at some level or regresses infinitely, and both prevent acquisition because they are invalid and impossible as will come, so that which depends on them is invalid and impossible. Then the commentator mentioned that the occurrence of circularity and infinite regress is complete in conceptualizations absolutely, and in assents if their acquisition from conceptualizations is impossible. And an objection was raised against this: that the assent to the appropriateness of premises to the sought conclusions is necessary, and it is theoretical on the assumption that all assents are theoretical, so circularity or infinite regress would occur even if we permit acquisition of assent from conceptualizations. And the answer is that what is necessary in acquisition is the appropriateness itself, not the knowledge of appropriateness, so it is permissible for an assent to be acquired from a conceptualization that is appropriate for that assent even if its appropriateness is not known.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by reductio ad absurdum that some conceptualizations and assents must be necessary (daruri) and not entirely theoretical (nazari). The proof begins by assuming the opposite: if every conceptualization and every assent were theoretical, then when we attempt to acquire any knowledge, that acquisition would depend on another conceptualization or assent, which in turn would depend on yet another, leading either to circular dependency at some level (dawr) or an infinite chain (tasalsul). Both are impossible and prevent acquisition. Therefore, the assumption is false. The commentator then discusses an objection concerning assents: the proof works for conceptualizations absolutely, but for assents, if assents can be acquired from conceptualizations, the dependency chain might stop. However, the objection notes that assent to the appropriateness of premises to conclusions is necessary for any deduction, and if all assents are theoretical, that too would be theoretical, causing the same problem. The answer is that acquisition requires only the objective appropriateness, not the knowledge of it, so an assent can be acquired from a conceptualization without knowing the appropriateness. Thus the proof remains valid.

Key Terms
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
نظري (nazari)
اكتساب (iktisab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to conceptualizations, not assents.
Correction: The proof actually covers both conceptualizations and assents, as the assumption includes both. The commentator later addresses a nuance for assents, but the core proof is general.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would happen if all conceptualizations and assents were theoretical?
A) They would all be certain
B) Acquisition would require circularity or infinite regress
C) They would be self-evident
D) They would be impossible to define

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that at least some knowledge must be self-evident to avoid infinite regress or circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have any necessary, self-evident knowledge that you rely on in your faith, or do you find yourself rationalizing everything, risking infinite regress? What is one belief you hold that you cannot prove without circular reasoning, and how does this lesson challenge you to examine it?
?
If you were asked to prove that God exists, would your proof depend on another proof that eventually circles back or goes on endlessly? How can grounding some knowledge as daruri, as Sanusi shows, give you a firm foundation?
?
In your daily life, are there assumptions you accept without proof (e.g., that your senses are reliable) that are necessary for acquiring other knowledge? How does recognizing these as daruri affect your certainty?

Objection and Answer: Assents and Appropriateness
Objection and response about assents in the proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the proof might fail for assents and the answer that acquisition requires objective appropriateness, not its knowledge.

Core Idea
Assents can be acquired from conceptualizations without knowing the appropriateness.

This passage addresses a subtle challenge: if assents can be derived from conceptualizations, does the proof still hold? The objection points out that even the assent to appropriateness of premises would be theoretical, causing the same problem. Sanusi's answer clarifies that what matters is objective appropriateness, not our awareness of it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection argues that for assents, if we can acquire them from conceptualizations, the proof might break. Moreover, the assent to the appropriateness of premises to conclusions is itself theoretical on the assumption, leading again to circularity/regress.
2
Understand the answer
The answer distinguishes between the appropriateness itself and the knowledge of that appropriateness. Acquisition needs only the objective fit, not our awareness of it.
3
See how the proof remains valid
Since objective appropriateness suffices, the dependency can stop at a conceptualization that naturally fits, avoiding the chain. Thus the original proof stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإذ لولاه لزم الدور أو التسلسل ) استدلال على المدعى بعد التنزل عن دعوى الضرورة الوجدانية. وحينئذ يكون كل واحد من التصور، وكذا كل واحد من التصديق نظرياً، فإذا حاولنا تحصيل شيء منهما كان ذلك التحصيل مستنداً إلى تصور أو تصديق آخر، وهو أيضاً نظري مستند إلى غيره من التصورات أو التصديقات، فإما أن يدور الاستناد في مرتبة من المراتب أو يتسلسل إلى ما لا يتناهى، وهما يمنعان الاكتساب لأنهما باطلان ممتنعان كما سيأتي، فما يتوقف عليهما كان باطلاً ممتنعاً. ثم ذكر الشارح أن لزوم الدور والتسلسل إنما يتم في التصورات مطلقاً، وفي التصديقات إذا امتنع اكتسابها من التصورات. واعترض عليه بأن التصديق بمناسبة المبادي للمطالب مما لا بد منه، وهو نظري على تقدير نظرية جميع التصديقات، فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل وإن جوزنا اكتساب التصديق من التصورات. والجواب أن اللازم في الاكتساب نفس المناسبة لا العلم بالمناسبة فيجوز أن يكتسب التصديق من تصور يكون مناسباً لذلك التصديق وإن لم يعلم مناسبته له.
His saying: ('And if not for that, circularity or infinite regress would be necessary') is a proof for the claim after descending from the claim of intuitive necessity. And then every single conceptualization and likewise every single assent would be theoretical. So if we attempt to acquire something from them, that acquisition would depend on another conceptualization or assent, which is also theoretical dependent on others among conceptualizations or assents. Then either the dependence circles at some level or regresses infinitely, and both prevent acquisition because they are invalid and impossible as will come, so that which depends on them is invalid and impossible. Then the commentator mentioned that the occurrence of circularity and infinite regress is complete in conceptualizations absolutely, and in assents if their acquisition from conceptualizations is impossible. And an objection was raised against this: that the assent to the appropriateness of premises to the sought conclusions is necessary, and it is theoretical on the assumption that all assents are theoretical, so circularity or infinite regress would occur even if we permit acquisition of assent from conceptualizations. And the answer is that what is necessary in acquisition is the appropriateness itself, not the knowledge of appropriateness, so it is permissible for an assent to be acquired from a conceptualization that is appropriate for that assent even if its appropriateness is not known.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by reductio ad absurdum that some conceptualizations and assents must be necessary (daruri) and not entirely theoretical (nazari). The proof begins by assuming the opposite: if every conceptualization and every assent were theoretical, then when we attempt to acquire any knowledge, that acquisition would depend on another conceptualization or assent, which in turn would depend on yet another, leading either to circular dependency at some level (dawr) or an infinite chain (tasalsul). Both are impossible and prevent acquisition. Therefore, the assumption is false. The commentator then discusses an objection concerning assents: the proof works for conceptualizations absolutely, but for assents, if assents can be acquired from conceptualizations, the dependency chain might stop. However, the objection notes that assent to the appropriateness of premises to conclusions is necessary for any deduction, and if all assents are theoretical, that too would be theoretical, causing the same problem. The answer is that acquisition requires only the objective appropriateness, not the knowledge of it, so an assent can be acquired from a conceptualization without knowing the appropriateness. Thus the proof remains valid.

Key Terms
مناسبة (munasaba)
دور (dawr)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
اكتساب (iktisab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is needed for acquisition of an assent from a conceptualization?
A) Knowledge of the appropriateness
B) Objective appropriateness itself
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The proof remains valid because acquisition only requires objective appropriateness, not subjective knowledge of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assent to a truth in your faith, do you sometimes feel uneasy because you cannot fully articulate the reasons? This passage says that objective appropriateness suffices even without your knowing it. Can you trust that God's guidance provides that appropriateness without your being aware of it? How might that change your confidence in what you believe?
?
Think of a time when you hesitated to accept a truth because you could not see the connection between the evidence and the conclusion. According to this lesson, the connection (appropriateness) can exist without your awareness. Will you now be more open to accepting truths even when you don't perceive the link? What would it take for you to act on that?
?
This argument shows that you don't need to know the appropriateness for acquisition to occur. Are you, in your daily life, too reliant on your own awareness and understanding? Or can you humbly accept that much of what you know comes from a source that is beyond your full comprehension? What is one belief you hold that you may need to re-examine in light of this?

The First Weak School: Three Interpretations of Denial
Al-Jurjani analyzes the first weak school into three possible interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible interpretations of the claim that knowledge does not depend on nazar, and which one corresponds to the Ash'ari doctrine.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari position affirms a habitual connection between nazar and knowledge, not a necessary causal one.

Al-Jurjani introduces the first weak school that denies any necessary dependence of knowledge on nazar. He then analyzes their denial into three distinct meanings: the denial of a necessary connection, the denial of causal efficacy (which is the Ash'ari view), and the total denial of any dependence. Understanding these nuances is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on the relationship between reflection and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Context of the weak schools
Recall that the issue is whether knowledge of rational matters necessarily depends on nazar. The first weak school denies this dependence. Read the passage to see how al-Jurjani unpacks this denial.
The weak schools are contrasted with the correct position later.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The passage presents three possible meanings of the denial: (1) no necessary connection, only habitual; (2) knowledge is not brought about by nazar or our power but created by God after nazar; (3) no dependence at all, neither habitual nor necessary. Find each in the text.
These are marked by 'إن أرادوا...' and 'أو أرادوا...'.
3
Recognize the Ash'ari position
The text explicitly states that the second interpretation is the doctrine of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) from the Ash'arites. Note that this affirms a habitual (customary) connection, not a causal one.
This is key: the Ash'aris do not deny that nazar precedes knowledge; they deny that nazar causes knowledge necessarily.
4
Contrast with obstinate denial
The third interpretation is dismissed as mere obstinacy (mukābarah) because it contradicts obvious experience—every rational person knows that their knowledge of disputed issues depends on reflection.
This shows that the Ash'ari view is not extreme; it retains a real connection but attributes it to God's custom.

The third observation - the fourth aim: The weak schools in this issue. ... it is necessarily necessary, for if there remains the possibility of non-necessity, even in the most remote way, it would not be knowledge, and if such is the case, the whole would be necessary. And his critic said: He intended by 'necessary' the meaning of certain, not the self-evident that is independent of reflection, and he called all certainties 'necessary' in agreement with the statement of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. And a group denies that, i.e., its dependence on reflection. These, if they intend by its non-dependence that it (i.e., knowledge) does not depend on reflection necessarily, since there is no intellectual connection between them that necessitates that, but it depends on it habitually; or they intend by it that the knowledge obtained after reflection is not brought about by it, i.e., by reflection, or is not brought about by our power in the way of effect, but by the creation of God in us subsequent to reflection by way of the course of habit — then it is the doctrine of the people of truth from the Ash'arites. And he guarded thereby against what Imam al-Razi chose in al-Muhassal, i.e., the statement of the obligation of knowledge from reflection, not on the basis of generation. And this statement has been attributed to al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn, for they said: the implication of reflection for knowledge is necessary without reflection being a cause or generator. And if they intend by its non-dependence on it that it does not depend on it at all, i.e., neither effect, nor obligation, nor habit, then it is willful obstinacy and opposition to what every rational person finds, that his knowledge of disputed issues depends on his reflection in them. ... He called all certainties 'necessary' indicating the unrestricted application of 'necessary' to both, meaning that our power has no effect. And whoever does not understand falls into confusion. So he said: By his transmission he alludes to the weakness of what occurred in some copies of the book after his statement: 'And some people said, and it is the statement of Imam al-Razi.' And the reason for its weakness is apparent from the words of al-Muhassal and his critic, because both indicate that what is meant by 'necessary' is the meaning of decisive (qat'i), not the opposite of theoretical. For the indication to what has no trace in the commentary has no meaning. His statement: (without) the self-evident, otherwise its division into two parts would not be valid. His statement: (Or they intended...) The difference between the three aspects: in the first, it is a negation of obligatory dependence absolutely, whether it is a cause or not; in the second, it is a negation of causal dependence; and in the third, it is a negation of dependence as a metaphor for negating effect, because effect entails dependence. His statement: (by that), i.e., by his statement 'people of truth.' His statement: 'And some people said, and it is the statement of Imam al-Razi, and the reason for its weakness is apparent from his words in al-Muhassal and the words of his critic, because both indicate that what the Imam meant by 'necessary' is the meaning of involuntary (idṭirārī), not the opposite of theoretical.' His statement: (Or they intended...) The difference between the two intentions is apparent on the surface, because the first clearly indicates the possibility of its occurrence without reflection by way of breaking the habit, and the second does not so indicate it, but on the surface agrees with its intellectual dependence on our reflection even if it does not necessitate it. This much difference does not negate the judgment that each is the doctrine of the people of truth, because the non-indication of something is not an indication of its non-existence. So ponder.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the first weak school (al-madhhab al-awwal) concerning the issue of whether knowledge of rational matters depends necessarily on theoretical reflection (nazar). The passage begins by noting that if knowledge were necessarily entailed by nazar, then all such knowledge would be necessary. A critic then explains that the term 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) here means certain (yaqīnī), not self-evident (badīhī) independent of reflection, and that this usage agrees with Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The weak school is a group that denies any dependence of knowledge on nazar. The author then analyzes their denial into three possible intentions: first, they may mean there is no necessary (wujūbī) causal connection between nazar and knowledge, only a habitual (ʿādatan) connection; second, they may mean that knowledge after nazar is not brought about by nazar itself or by our power, but by God's creation following nazar as a matter of habit (ʿādah) – and this second interpretation is the Ash'ari doctrine (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq); third, they may mean there is no dependence at all—neither habitual nor necessary nor causal—and this is mere obstinacy (mukābarah). The author then notes that al-Ash'ari called all certainties 'ḍarūrī' to indicate that our power has no effect. A textual note follows, pointing out a variant in some copies where the phrase 'and some people said, and it is the statement of Imam al-Razi' appears, and the author explains the weakness of this variant because the term 'ḍarūrī' in that context means 'decisive' (qaṭʿī) or 'involuntary' (iḍṭirārī), not the opposite of theoretical. Finally, the passage clarifies the difference between the first two intentions: the first allows the possibility of knowledge occurring without nazar by breaking habit (kharq al-ʿādah), while the second does not clearly indicate that possibility, but both are compatible with the Ash'ari doctrine because non-indication is not the same as negation.

النظر

المكابرة
أهل الحق
الأشعرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Ash'ari position denies any relationship between nazar and knowledge, treating nazar as completely irrelevant.
Correction: The Ash'ari position affirms a habitual (customary) connection: knowledge is created by God after nazar, so nazar is not the cause but a customary precedent. This is distinct from both necessary causation and total denial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which interpretation of the denial of knowledge's dependence on nazar is attributed to the Ash'ari school (ahl al-haqq)?
A) That knowledge does not depend on nazar necessarily, but only habitually.
B) That knowledge after nazar is created by God subsequent to nazar by way of custom.
C) That knowledge does not depend on nazar at all, neither necessarily nor habitually.
D) That knowledge depends on nazar necessarily through generation (tawlīd).

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school holds that knowledge after nazar is created by God subsequent to nazar by way of habit, not by necessary causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you approach a difficult question in your faith, do you truly invest in reflection, or do you secretly hope that God will hand you the answer without effort? How does the Ash'ari position on habitual connection challenge the laziness of your heart?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth simply because you did not arrive at it through your own reasoning, calling it 'blind following'? Al-Jurjani calls total denial of dependence 'willful obstinacy'—is there an area in your life where you refuse to reflect because you fear what you might discover?
?
The Ash'aris affirm that knowledge after reflection is created by God by habit. Does this make you more grateful for every insight you gain, knowing it is a gift from Him, or does it diminish your sense of responsibility to strive? What will you change in your next study session because of this lesson?

Classification of Rational Possibilities and the First Group
Classifies rational possibilities regarding acceptance of sensory and self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of rational possibilities regarding sensory and self-evident truths, and the position of the first group (the majority).

Core Idea
Rational possibilities reduce to three: accept both, reject both, or accept one.

In this passage from Sanusi's work, we begin the Fourth Observation on establishing necessary sciences. The author first lays out the rational possibilities concerning whether to accept sensory and self-evident truths. He then identifies the first group—those who affirm both—as the majority on the right path. This passage sets the stage for discussing the epistemology of necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Get a sense of the author's classification.
Pay attention to the three possibilities listed.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage mentions three rational possibilities: accepting both, rejecting both, or accepting one without the other. List them.
These are the basic options regarding sensory and self-evident truths.
3
Examine the first group
The first group are those who affirm both sensory and self-evident truths. Note that they are described as the majority on the right path.
This group is the orthodox position.
4
Understand the limitation to four
The commentary explains that the enumeration is limited to four possibilities by this consideration, and other possibilities like accepting parts are dismissed.
This prevents overcomplication.

The possibilities are: accepting both, rejecting both, or accepting one without the other. Then it mentions the first group, which are those who affirm both, and they are the majority on the right path. The commentary then explains that this enumeration of possibilities is limited to these four, and that other possibilities such as accepting some parts are not considered.

The Fourth Observatory: Sensory Knowledge and Its Certainty
Defines the Fourth Observatory, explains the unknown factor in sensory certainty, and refutes skeptics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sensory knowledge requires an unknown additional factor for certainty, and that criticizing sense perception undermines the very sciences of the skeptics.

Core Idea
Sense perception alone is not certain; an unknown rational factor compels certainty.

This lesson introduces the Fourth Observatory, which addresses the necessary sciences grounded in sensory knowledge. The author argues that mere sensation does not yield certainty; an additional rational factor is needed, though its nature is unknown. He then counters skeptics by demonstrating that their own sciences—from Plato to Galen—rely on sense perception, making criticism of the senses self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: definition, then a conditional argument against skeptics, then examples of philosophers.
The passage uses a very condensed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the central claim about sensory knowledge
The author states that sensed things are not certain by mere connection of sensation—there is an unknown auxiliary factor. Why is this important? It refutes the idea that sense perception is automatically reliable.
3
Follow the refutation of skeptics
The author presents a conditional: if skeptics do not accept this interpretation, then their own sciences are based on sense perception. Trace how he lists Plato, Aristotle, Ptolemy, and Galen to show this dependence.
This is a powerful ad hominem argument without being fallacious.
4
Connect to first principles
The passage concludes that even self-evident truths (al-awwaliyyat) arise from sensation in children. Therefore, attacking the senses attacks the foundations of all knowledge.
This shows the breadth of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية ( فتضطره ) أي تلجئ تلك الأمور العقل ) إلى الجزم بما جزم به من الحسيات ( لا نعلم ما هي أي ما تلك الأمور المنضمة إلى الإحساس الموجبة للجزم ( ومتى حصلت لنا وكيف حصلت فلا تكون الحسيات بمجرد تعلق الإحساس بها يقينية، وهذا حق لا شبهة فيه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يريدوا بالقدح في الحسيات ما ذكرناه من التأويل ( فإليها ) أي إلى الحسيات تنتهي علومهم) فيكون القدح الحقيقي فيها قدحا في علومهم التي يفتخرون بها، وذلك لا يتصور ممن له أدنى مسكة، فكيف هؤلاء الأذكياء الأجلاء ؟ وإنما قلنا : بانتهاء علومهم إليها لأن العلم من الإلهي المنسوب إلى أفلاطون مبني على الاستدلال بأحوال المحسوسات المعلومة بمعاونة الحس، وأكثر أصول العلم الطبيعي المنسوب إلى أرسطو كالعلم بالسماء والعالم وبالكون والفساد وبالآثار العلوية، وبأحكام المعادن والنبات والحيوان مأخوذ من الحس وعلم الأرصاد والهيئة المنسوب إلى بطلميوس مبني على الإحساس، وأحكام المحسوسات وعلم التجارب الطبية المنسوب إلى جالينوس مأخوذ من المحسوسات هذا وقد صرحوا بأن الأوليات إنما تحصل للصبيان باستعداد يحصل لعقولهم من الإحساس بالجزئيات فالقدح في الحسيات يؤول إلى القدح في البديهيات
The Fourth Observatory — On Establishing the Necessary Sciences. (So it compels him) i.e., those rational matters force him to the certainty of what he has judged from the sensed things. (We do not know what they are) i.e., what those matters adjunct to sensation that necessitate certainty are. (And when they occur to us and how they occur, then the sensed things are not certain by mere connection of sensation with them, and this is true with no doubt in it. (Otherwise) i.e., if they do not mean by criticizing the sensed things what we mentioned of interpretation, (then to them) i.e., to the sensed things, their sciences end, so the real criticism of them is a criticism of their sciences of which they boast. And that is unimaginable from one who has the least intelligence, so how about these intelligent and noble ones? We only say: their sciences end in them because the divine science attributed to Plato is built on inference from the states of sensed things known with the help of sense; and most of the principles of natural science attributed to Aristotle, like knowledge of the sky and the world, generation and corruption, and meteorological phenomena, and the judgments of minerals, plants, and animals, are taken from sense; and the science of astronomical observations and astronomy attributed to Ptolemy is built on sensation; and the judgments of sensed things and the science of medical experiments attributed to Galen are taken from sensed things. This, and they have stated that the first principles are only acquired for children through a preparedness that occurs for their minds from sensation of particulars. So criticism of the sensed things leads to criticism of the self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the scope of the Fourth Observatory: it is about establishing the necessary sciences. The author explains that sensory knowledge is not certain solely by the act of sensation; there is an additional rational factor that compels certainty, though its exact nature and mode of occurrence are unknown. This is presented as an indisputable truth. The passage then addresses skeptics who deny the reliability of sense perception. It argues that if the skeptics do not accept this interpretation (i.e., that there is an unknown factor), their own sciences depend on sense perception. To illustrate, it lists the sciences of major philosophers: Plato's divine science is based on reasoning from sensed things; Aristotle's natural science relies on sense for topics like cosmology, meteorology, and biology; Ptolemy's astronomy is built on observation; and Galen's medicine is empirical. Furthermore, even the first principles (awwaliyyat) are said to be acquired by children through sensation of particulars. Therefore, criticizing the senses leads to criticizing the self-evident truths (badihiyyat), which would be self-defeating for any rational person, especially the intelligent skeptics.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sense perception alone gives certain knowledge without any additional factor.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that sensed things are not certain by mere sensation; an unknown rational factor is necessary for certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against skeptics who criticize sense perception?
A) Their criticism is based on faulty reasoning.
B) Their own sciences depend on sense perception, so criticizing it undermines their own knowledge.
C) Sense perception is infallible.
D) They have no alternative source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge is not certain by itself; an unknown rational factor is required, and skeptics cannot reject the senses without contradicting their own scientific traditions.

Visual Size Perception via the Angle at the Eye
Explanation of size perception through visual angle and object disappearance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the angle formed at the eye by visual lines determines perceived size, and how medium density and distance affect this angle, leading to the eventual disappearance of objects.

Core Idea
Perceived size is determined by the visual angle, not actual object dimensions.

This passage from a classical work of kalam and natural philosophy explains the geometric basis of visual perception. It addresses common puzzles like why a ring appears large near the eye and why distant objects shrink to a point, and it provides a foundation for understanding how we see the world around us.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the examples used.
The passage includes both the main exposition and commentary in brackets.
2
Identify the key examples
Find the examples of the berry in water, the ring near the eye, and distant objects. For each, ask: what happens to the visual angle and perceived size?
These illustrate how medium and distance affect the angle.
3
Understand the geometric rule
The passage states that for a single magnitude as chord, the angle with shorter sides is larger. Note the condition that this only holds when the sides are equal. Relate this to the ring example.
This rule explains why a near object appears larger.
4
Consider the limit of vision
Trace how the angle narrows with distance until the object appears as a point, then disappears. Understand that disappearance is due to extreme narrowness of the angle, not complete superimposition.
This connects to later discussions in Ilahiyyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية
هذا، فإن تلك الخطوط تنعطف وتميل إلى سهم المخروط عند وصولها إلى ذلك الغليظ، ثم تصل إلى طرفي المرئي فتكون زاوية رأس المخروط ها هنا أكبر منها في الصورة الأولى مع كون المرئي شيئاً واحداً، فيرى في الصورة الثانية أكبر منه في الأولى كما يظهر من هذا الشكل فالخطان الأحمران هما الواصلان إلى طرفي العنبة إذا كانت في الهواء والأسودان هما الواصلان إلى طرفيها إذا كانت في الماء، والزاوية التي بين الأولين أصغر من التي بين الأخيرين فلذلك ترى في الماء أكبر منها في الهواء ) والخاتم المقرب من العين يرى كالحلقة الكبيرة وذلك لكبر الزاوية التي عند الحدقة فإن المقدار الواحد إذا جعل وتر الزاويتين مستقيمتي الأضلاع، فالزاوية التي ضلعاها أقصر كانت أكبر من الزاوية التي ضلعاها أطول ) وبالعكس) أي وترى الكبير صغيراً ( كالأشياء البعيدة ) وسببه صغر تلك الزاوية بحسب بعد المرئي، فكلما كان أبعد كانت الزاوية أضيق إلى أن تتقارب الخطوط الشعاعية جداً، كأن بعضها منطبق على بعض فيرى ذلك المرئي كأنه نقطة، وبعد ذلك ينمحي أثره فلا يرى

قوله : ( فإن تلك الخطوط) أي التي على سطح المخروط، وكذا الخطوط الداخلة فيه ما سوي السهم فإنه ينفذ على الاستقامة في الشفاف المتشابه، وغير المتشابه إلا أن الخطوط الداخلة تتفاوت في الانعطاف بحسب القرب عن السهم وبعدها عنه فعلم من ذلك أن الجزء الذي يقع عليه سهم المخروط على مقداره وما عداه متفاوت في الصغر والكبر بحسب القرب والبعد منه

قوله : ( تنعطف وتميل بقدر تباعدها من مطرحها حال الاستقامة إلى خلاف جهة السهم إن كان ما يلي المرئي أغلظ وإلى جهة السهم إن كان أرق وبسبب هذا تصير الزاوية عند الحدقة أوسع في الأول بصيرورة وتره أطول وأضيق في الثاني لقصر وتره مع اتحاد ضلعي الزاوية فيهما، لأن المفروض عدم التفاوت في جميع التقادير بحسب القرب والبعد، وإلا لكان من الصورة الثانية أعني الخاتم المقرب من العين الخ.

قوله : ( فالزاوية التي ضلعاها أقصر الخ ) هذا إنما يلزم إذا كان الضلعان متساويين وأما إذا لم يكونا متساويين فيجوز أن تكون الزاوية متساوية أو أصغر وإن كان ضلعاها أقصر.

قوله : ( وبعد ذلك أي بعد كونه كالنقطة ينمحي أثره لغاية ضيق الزاوية وصيرورته كالمعدومة وليس ذلك إشارة إلى التقارب جداً حتى يرد أن ما بعد التقارب جداً هو الانطباق فيستفاد منه أن انمحاء الأثر بعد انمحاء الزاوية مع أنه ذكر في بحث الرؤية من الإلهيات أن انمحاء الأثر عند ضيق الزاوية غاية التضييق وصيرورتها كالمعدومة.
The Fourth Observation: On Establishing Necessary Sciences. This: those lines bend and incline toward the axis of the cone when they reach that dense medium, then they reach the two ends of the seen object, so the angle at the vertex of the cone here is larger than in the first case while the object is one, so in the second case it is seen larger than in the first as appears from this figure. So the two red lines are the ones reaching the two ends of the berry when it is in the air, and the two black lines are the ones reaching its two ends when it is in the water, and the angle between the first two is smaller than that between the last two, therefore it is seen in water larger than in the air. And the ring close to the eye is seen like a large circle due to the largeness of the angle at the pupil. For a single magnitude, if made the chord of two right-angled triangles, the angle with shorter sides is larger than the angle with longer sides, and vice versa. That is, a large thing is seen small, like distant objects, and its cause is the smallness of that angle according to the distance of the object. So the farther it is, the narrower the angle becomes until the radial lines converge very much, as if some are superimposed on others, so that object is seen as if it were a point, and after that its trace disappears and it is not seen at all. He said: (those lines) meaning those on the surface of the cone, and likewise the lines inside it other than the axis, for it penetrates straight through similar transparent media and dissimilar ones, but the interior lines vary in bending according to their proximity to or distance from the axis. So it is known from that that the part on which the axis of the cone falls remains on its magnitude, and other parts vary in smallness and largeness according to proximity to or distance from it. He said: (they bend and incline) to the extent of their divergence from their point of origin in a straight line, opposite to the direction of the axis if the medium adjacent to the object is denser, and toward the axis if it is thinner; and because of this, the angle at the pupil becomes wider in the first case due to its chord becoming longer, and narrower in the second due to the shortness of its chord, while the two sides of the angle are equal in both, because the assumption is that there is no variation in all distances according to proximity and distance; otherwise, it would be like the second case, namely the ring close to the eye, etc. He said: (the angle whose sides are shorter, etc.) This only follows if the two sides are equal; but if they are not equal, it is possible that the angle is equal or smaller even if its sides are shorter. He said: (and after that) meaning after it becomes like a point, its trace disappears due to extreme narrowness of the angle and its becoming like nonexistent; and that is not an indication of extreme convergence such that it could be objected that after extreme convergence is superimposition, so it is understood that the disappearance of the trace is after the disappearance of the angle, while he mentioned in the discussion of vision in Ilahiyyat that the disappearance of vision is due to extreme narrowness of the angle and its becoming like nonexistent.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the geometrical basis of visual size perception and the eventual disappearance of objects as they recede. The author begins with the phenomenon that lines reaching the eye from an object form an angle at the cornea (called the vertex angle). When the medium is denser (e.g., water), the lines bend and the angle increases, making the object appear larger—as with a berry in water. Conversely, a ring close to the eye appears large because the angle at the pupil is large. The author then states a geometric rule: for a given chord (the object), the angle with shorter sides is larger, and vice versa. Applying this, distant objects appear small because the angle becomes narrow. As distance increases, the angle narrows until the radial lines nearly superimpose, making the object appear as a point; beyond that, the trace disappears entirely. The commentary elaborates: the lines in question lie on the surface of the cone, and the visual axis (sahm) remains straight, while other lines bend. The reason for the angle's variation is explained: when the medium adjacent to the object is denser, the lines bend away from the axis, lengthening the chord and widening the angle; when thinner, they bend toward the axis, shortening the chord and narrowing the angle. A condition is noted: the geometric rule about side length and angle size holds only when the sides are equal. Finally, the commentary clarifies that the disappearance of the object after it becomes a point is due to the extreme narrowing (not complete superimposition) of the angle, consistent with the discussion of vision in the theological section on divine attributes (Ilahiyyat).

زاوية

حدقة
وتر
ضلعي الزاوية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Visual lines are actual rays emitted from the eye.
Correction: The passage uses a geometrical model for explaining perception; the lines are not physical emissions but represent the path of light or the visual process.

Checkpoint
What determines the perceived size of an object according to this passage?
A) The actual physical size of the object
B) The size of the angle formed at the eye by the visual lines
C) The distance of the object from the eye only
D) The density of the medium between the eye and object

Key Takeaway
The angle at the eye is the decisive factor in visual size perception, influenced by medium, distance, and the geometry of the visual cone.

Optical Theories of Single and Double Vision
Explanations of single/double vision and optic nerve anatomy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical theories explaining single and double vision: the extramission theory of mathematicians and the intromission theory of naturalists, along with the anatomical description of optic nerves.

Core Idea
Single vision requires perfect alignment of the two eyes' images.

This passage presents the foundational explanations for why we normally see one object with two eyes, and why sometimes we see double. The author concisely states the positions of two groups (the mathematicians and naturalists) and then describes the anatomy of the optic nerves according to Galen and others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two theories
The first part presents two explanations for single vision: the mathematicians' view (simultaneous rays) and the naturalists' view (single image at meeting point). Notice how each also explains double vision: non-simultaneous rays or two images at the meeting point.
The terms 'mathematicians' and 'naturalists' refer to philosophical schools in optics.
2
Understand the anatomy of the optic nerves
The passage describes the optic nerves meeting before the eyes and diverging, like two letter dals back-to-back (Galen) or intersecting like a cross. This structure is key to the mechanism of single vision.
The 'meeting point' (ملتقى) is where the two optic nerves join before reaching the brain.
3
Connect anatomy to double vision
Double vision occurs when the two images from the eyes fall on different parts of the common sense (الحس المشترك) due to misalignment. The passage explains that if the conveyances do not meet at the same spot, the object is seen as two.
The common sense is a faculty that combines sensory inputs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيرى واحداً لوقوع الشعاع الخارج من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة عند الرياضيين ولحصول صورة واحدة في الملتقى عند الطبيعيين والإبصار إنما يتم بحصول الصورة فيه بل وفي الحس المشترك لا بمجرد الانطباع في الجليدية وإلا لرئي الشيء الواحد شيئين، فيرى ذلك اثنين لعدم الشعاع من العينين على المرئي دفعة واحدة بل على التعاقب عند الرياضيين وبحصول الصورة في الموضعين من الملتقى لأجل المحاذاتين عند الطبيعيين.

قوله : ( فلان الشعاع الخ يعني أن القمر إذا كان قريبا من الأفق يخرج الشعاع البصري

قوله : ( تتلاقيان قبل وصولهما إلى العينين ثم تتباعدان يعني كهيئة الدالين ظهر أحدهما على ظهر الآخر هذا مذهب جالينوس وقيل التلاقي على سبيل التقاطع الصليبي .

قوله : ( من محاذاتين (الخ فلا يلتقي مؤداهما في الحسن المشترك على موضع واحد، بل موقع أحدهما حينئذ غير موقع الآخر، فينقلان المرئي إلى موضعين منه فيرى اثنين، وفيه بحث فإنه إذا كان قدامنا جسمان أحدهما على مسافة خمسة أذرع والثاني على مسافة ذراع مثلاً، وكان الثاني بحيث لا يحجب الأول عن بصرنا ، فإذا نظرنا إلى الأقرب وجمعنا البصر عليه وقصدنا بالنظر كأنا لا ننظر إلى غيره فإنا نراه في تلك الحالة واحداً ونرى الأبعد اثنين وإذا عكسنا تنعكس القضية فلو كان سبب رؤية الواحد اثنين ما ذكر لزم في الصورة المذكورة أن يكون تركيب العصبتين باقياً بحاله متزايلاً معاً، وهذا غير معقول ولو بالنسبة إلى شيئين.
He said: (so it is seen as one, because of the ray exiting from both eyes falling on the object at once according to the mathematicians, and because of the occurrence of a single image at the meeting point according to the naturalists; and vision is only completed by the occurrence of the image in it, and even in the common sense, not merely by impression on the crystalline humor, otherwise the one thing would be seen as two things. So it is seen as two because of the ray from the two eyes not falling on the object at once but successively according to the mathematicians, and because of the occurrence of the image in two places in the meeting point due to two alignments according to the naturalists. He said: (the ray, etc.) means that when the moon is near the horizon, the visual ray exits. He said: (they meet before reaching the two eyes, then they diverge) meaning like the shape of two dals (د), the back of one against the back of the other; this is the school of Galen, and it is said the meeting is in the manner of a cruciform intersection. He said: (from two alignments, etc.) So their conveyances do not meet in the common sense at one place, but rather, the place of one of them then is other than the place of the other, so they transmit the object to two places in it, and thus it is seen as two. And there is a discussion in this: if there are two objects in front of us, one at a distance of five cubits and the second at a distance of one cubit, for example, and the second does not obstruct the first from our sight, then if we look at the nearer one, focus our vision on it, and intend by our sight as if we are not looking at anything else, then we see it in that case as one, and we see the farther one as two. And if we reverse [the focus], the case is reversed. So if the cause of seeing one as two were what was mentioned, then in the mentioned scenario, the structure of the two nerves would have to remain in its state while being separated together, which is inconceivable, even with respect to two things.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of diplopia (seeing one object as two) and provides a critique. The author begins by presenting two theories: the mathematicians (extramission) say that rays from both eyes strike the object simultaneously for single vision; if not simultaneous, the object is seen as two. The naturalists (intromission) say that a single image forms at the meeting point of the nerves; if images form at two points due to misalignment, double vision results. The commentary then describes the anatomy of the optic nerves according to Galen: they meet before the eyes and then diverge like two letter dals back-to-back; others say they intersect like a cross. The key explanation for double vision is that when the alignment is off, the two images from the two eyes fall on different parts of the common sense, so the object is seen as two. A critical objection (وفيه بحث) is then raised: if we have two objects at different distances, focusing on the nearer one makes it appear single and the farther one double, and focusing on the farther makes the nearer appear double. This contradicts the simple alignment explanation because the nerve structure cannot simultaneously be aligned for one object and misaligned for another. The critique concludes that the proposed cause is insufficient.

C) The optic nerves do not meet
D) The common sense is absent

Key Takeaway
Two classical theories—extramission (mathematicians) and intromission (naturalists)—explain single vision through either simultaneous rays or a single image at the nerve meeting point, and double vision through the failure of these conditions.

The passage teaches that vision is only completed when the image reaches the common sense—not just the eye. In your spiritual life, are you stopping at superficial impressions (like hearing a verse without reflection) or are you letting the truth penetrate your inner faculty until it becomes an integrated conviction? What one step will you take today to move from mere sensory exposure to deep understanding?
?
Think of a disagreement or confusion you have about your faith that feels like 'double vision.' According to this lesson, the cause is misalignment—perhaps you are receiving contradictory inputs from different sources. A Critical Objection to the Alignment Theory
Critical objection to the alignment theory of double vision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a powerful objection to the simple alignment theory of double vision based on focusing at different distances.

Core Idea
Focusing on one object makes others appear double, contradicting fixed nerve alignment.

After presenting the standard explanations, the author raises a critical objection. If we have two objects at different distances and focus on the nearer one, it appears single while the farther appears double. Reversing focus reverses the appearance. This challenges the idea that double vision arises solely from nerve misalignment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The passage describes an experiment with two objects at different distances. Focus on the nearer one makes it single and the farther double; focus on the farther reverses the effect. This is presented as a problem for the alignment theory.
2
Visualize the scenario
Imagine a near object at 1 cubit and a far object at 5 cubits, with the near not blocking the far. When you look at the near object with full focus, it appears single and the far appears double. This is a common experience.
3
Understand the logical consequence
The author argues that if the alignment theory were correct, the nerve structure would have to be simultaneously aligned for the near object and misaligned for the far object, which is impossible. This shows the theory is insufficient.
The nerve structure is fixed relative to the eyes; it cannot adjust differently for different objects at the same time.
4
Reflect on the strength of the objection
This is a strong critique because it uses a simple observable phenomenon to challenge a core assumption. It does not completely disprove the alignment theory but shows it cannot explain all instances of double vision.
The author uses the word 'بحث' indicating a discussion or criticism, not a full refutation.

And there is a discussion in this: if there are two objects in front of us, one at a distance of five cubits and the second at a distance of one cubit, for example, and the second does not obstruct the first from our sight, then if we look at the nearer one, focus our vision on it, and intend by our sight as if we are not looking at anything else, then we see it in that case as one, and we see the farther one as two. And if we reverse [the focus], the case is reversed. So if the cause of seeing one as two were what was mentioned, then in the mentioned scenario, the structure of the two nerves would have to remain in its state while being separated together, which is inconceivable, even with respect to two things.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of diplopia (seeing one object as two) and provides a critique. The author begins by presenting two theories: the mathematicians (extramission) say that rays from both eyes strike the object simultaneously for single vision; if not simultaneous, the object is seen as two. The naturalists (intromission) say that a single image forms at the meeting point of the nerves; if images form at two points due to misalignment, double vision results. The commentary then describes the anatomy of the optic nerves according to Galen: they meet before the eyes and then diverge like two letter dals back-to-back; others say they intersect like a cross. The key explanation for double vision is that when the alignment is off, the two images from the two eyes fall on different parts of the common sense, so the object is seen as two. A critical objection (وفيه بحث) is then raised: if we have two objects at different distances, focusing on the nearer one makes it appear single and the farther one double, and focusing on the farther makes the nearer appear double. This contradicts the simple alignment explanation because the nerve structure cannot simultaneously be aligned for one object and misaligned for another. The critique concludes that the proposed cause is insufficient.

Key Terms
بحث
عصبتان مجوفتان
محاذاتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection completely disproves the alignment theory.
Correction: The objection highlights a specific inconsistency; it does not fully refute the theory but shows it is incomplete.

Checkpoint
What does the author's experiment demonstrate?
A) Double vision never occurs when focusing on a single object
B) The alignment theory cannot explain why focusing on one object makes another appear double
C) The optic nerves are not involved in vision
D) The moon appears larger at the horizon

Key Takeaway
The author's focusing experiment reveals a flaw in the alignment theory: it cannot explain why the same nerve structure produces single vision for one object and double vision for another at a different distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the focusing experiment, your own eyes produce contradictory perceptions from the same physical setup. When have you encountered a similar contradiction in your own understanding of a truth you held firmly? How did you respond to that discomfort?
?
The author points out that the nerve structure cannot be in two states at once. Are there beliefs you hold that require you to accept incompatible truths about your own soul or actions? Will you honestly examine them after this lesson?
?
This objection shows that a seemingly simple phenomenon (double vision) can undermine a whole theory. What area of your life or faith have you assumed is simple, but might actually be more complex? What would it cost you to explore it deeply?

Two Examples of Visual Error: Moon and Squinter
The passage presents two examples of perceptual error: the double moon and the squinter's double vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the moon reflection and squinter examples demonstrate that sense perception can produce errors.

Core Idea
Our eyes can deceive: the same moon appears twice, and a deliberate squint makes one object seem two.

This lesson presents two concrete cases where visual perception goes wrong. The first involves seeing the moon both in the sky and reflected on water; the second involves a person who deliberately squints and sees a single object as double. These examples serve to show that sense perception is not infallible, a key point in establishing the need for rational proofs in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Note the two examples: the moon reflection and the squinter.
The passage is dense; take your time to identify the two examples.
2
Identify the causes of error
For the moon, the error arises from direct and reflected rays. For the squinter, it is due to deviation in the optic nerves.
The squinter example distinguishes between deliberate and natural squinting.
3
Notice the natural vs. deliberate distinction
The text says the natural squinter rarely sees one as two due to habit, while the deliberate squinter does.
This introduces the idea of habit correcting perception.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why are these examples important? They prove that the senses can err, so necessary knowledge cannot rely solely on sense perception.
This is a key argument in kalam for rational proofs.
