سطح الماء إليه أيضاً فيرى مرة في السماء بالشعاع النافذ، ومرة في الماء بالشعاع المنعكس ) وكالاً حول ) أي الذي يقصد الحول تكلفاً ( فإنه يرى الواحد اثنين) بسبب وقوع الانحراف في العصبتين أو في إحداهما ، وأما الأحول الفطري فقلما يرى الواحد اثنين، وذلك لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب ) وبالعكس) أي ويرى الكثير واحداً
ملاصقاً لسطح الماء نافذاً إلى الهواء، فيرى ذلك الشعاع قمر السماء بطريق النفوذ إليه، وقمر الماء بطريق انعكاس الشعاع إليه دفعة واحدة لقربهما فيرى لذلك قمرين.
قوله : ( وينعكس من سطح الماء وذلك لأن وضع قمر السماء عن سطح الماء كوضع قمر الماء، ولذا قالوا : لو رصد ارتفاع قمر السماء بالآلة وانحطاط قمر الماء بها في دائرة الارتفاع لوجد كل واحد منهما مساوياً للآخر، وإنما شرط كونه عند طلوعه مع أنه كذلك عند كونه مرتفعاً لأن ذينك القمرين إنما يريان دفعة واحدة حال كونهما قريبين من الأفق وأما عند تباعدهما فيرى أحدهما بعد الآخر بتقليب الحدقة والالتفات إليه .
(
قوله : (لاعتياده بالوقوف الخ باستعمال الحاستين بالوضع الذي يقع الشعاع الخارج عنهما من محاذاة واحدة فيرى واحداً أما إذا لم يستعمل الحاستين على ذلك الوضع، بل على وضع يعتاده من لا حول له يرى الواحد اثنين، ولذا قال الشارح رحمه الله : فقلما .
قوله : ( أي) الذي يقصد الحول تكلفاً قيل : فحينئذ يكون مغنياً عن حديث الغمز في القمر، لأن ذلك من صور الحول الجعلي، وأنت خبير بأن المقصود تكثير أمثلة الغلط، فلا ضير في التعميم بعد ذكر صورة منه ولا في أن يحمل على غيره من الصور .
قوله : ( وذلك لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب فيه بحث مشهور وهو أن الاعتياد بالوقوف على الصواب لا يدل على أنه يرى الواحد واحداً، فربما يراه اثنين لكن باعتياده المذكور يجزم بأن ما يراه اثنين ،واحد وقد يجاب بأن الإدراكات تتوقف على التفات النفس، فإذا رأت الواحد اثنين وعلم أن الواقع ليس كذلك يعرض عن إحدى الصورتين ولا يلتفت إليها فلا يحصل بسببه إدراك الواحد اثنين، فلا يتجه أن سبب الغلط موجود، فكيف لا يغلط ؟ ويؤيده ما قيل : إن ما يقع عليه شعاع البصر قريب من نصف كرة العالم، وعند الالتفات إلى نقطة لا يدرك إلا تلك النقطة، وأنت خبير بأن اعتقاد أن الواقع ليس كذلك متحقق في الأحول الجعلي أيضاً، بل هو فيه أظهر فينبغي أن لا يرى الواحد اثنين أيضاً، وتحقيق مراد الشارح عندي أن الأحول الفطري ربما يحرف العصبتين من الوضع الخلقي بالنسبة إليه فيجد الوضع الخلقي بالنسبة إلى نوعه، إذ انحراف المنحرف قد يؤدي إلى الاستقامة فمعنى كلامه أن الأحول الفطري لما كان واقفاً على خطأ حكمه بمقتضى حسه حال كونه على الوضع الطبيعي بالنسبة إلى شخصه بحرف العصبتين طالباً لإدراكه بوجه آخر مغايراً لما أدركه أولاً، فيجد الاستقامة وهذا الوجدان صار ملكة له لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب وكيفيته ألا يرى أنه إذا نظر إلى شيء يعتبر وضعه في النظر فمنهم من هو كأنه ينظر بمؤخر عينيه، ومنهم من ينظر بوجه آخر على حسب وجدانه الاستقامة وبه يظهر أن
To the surface of water also, he sees once in the sky by direct ray, and once in the water by reflected ray; and like the squinter—i.e., the one who deliberately squints—he sees the one as two due to deviation occurring in the two optic nerves or in one of them. As for the natural squinter, he rarely sees the one as two, due to his habit of arriving at correctness; and conversely, i.e., he sees the many as one. Adjacent to the surface of water, penetrating into the air, that ray sees the moon in the sky by way of penetration to it, and the moon in the water by way of reflection of the ray to it simultaneously due to their proximity, so he sees two moons. His saying: (And it is reflected from the surface of water) and that is because the position of the moon in the sky relative to the water's surface is like the position of the water-moon, so they said: If one observed the altitude of the sky-moon with an instrument and the depression of the water-moon with it in the circle of altitude, he would find each one equal to the other. And the condition of its being at its rising, although it is likewise when it is high, is because those two moons are seen simultaneously only when they are near the horizon; but when they are distant, one is seen after the other by shifting the pupil and turning to it. His saying: (Due to his habit of arriving at correctness) by using the two senses in a position where the rays emerging from them fall in a single alignment, so he sees one; but if he does not use the two senses in that position, but rather in a position that a non-squinter is accustomed to, he sees the one as two. Hence the commentator, may God have mercy on him, said: 'rarely'. His saying: (i.e., one who deliberately squints) it is said: then this suffices for the discussion of pressing the moon, because that is a form of artificial squint. And you are aware that the purpose is to multiply examples of error, so there is no harm in generalization after mentioning one form, nor in applying it to other forms. His saying: (And due to his habit of arriving at correctness) there is a well-known investigation: habit of arriving at correctness does not prove that he sees the one as one; perhaps he sees it as two but due to that habit he judges that what he sees as two is one. And it may be answered that perceptions depend on the attention of the soul: when it sees the one as two and knows that the reality is not so, it turns away from one of the two images and does not attend to it, so no perception of one as two occurs by that means; thus it does not follow that the cause of error exists, so how does he not err? This is supported by what is said: that what the rays of sight fall upon is close to half of the celestial sphere, and when attending to a point, only that point is perceived. And you are aware that the belief that the reality is not so is also realized in the artificial squinter; indeed it is more apparent in him, so he should not see the one as two either. And the realization of the commentator's intention according to me is that the natural squinter perhaps deviates the optic nerves from the natural position relative to himself, so he finds the natural position relative to his species, since the deviation of the deviator may lead to straightness. The meaning of his speech is that the natural squinter, since he is aware of the error of his judgment by virtue of his sense while in the natural position relative to himself, deviates the optic nerves seeking to perceive it in another way different from what he first perceived, so he finds straightness; and this finding becomes a habit for him due to his habit of arriving at correctness. Its modality is that when he looks at something, he considers his position in looking: among them is one who looks as if from the back of his eyes, and among them is one who looks in another way according to his finding of straightness. And by this it appears that...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage provides two examples of perceptual errors (أغلاط الحس) that are used to show that sense perception is not infallible, which is relevant to proving necessary sciences. The first example concerns the reflection of the moon on water: a person sees the moon both directly in the sky and reflected on the water's surface, giving the illusion of two moons. The explanation involves direct and reflected rays. The second example concerns the squinter (الأحول): a person who deliberately squints sees a single object as two due to the deviation of the optic nerves. By contrast, a natural squinter (الأحول الفطري) rarely makes this mistake because he has acquired the habit of correcting his vision. The commentator (الشارح) then explains the mechanism: the natural squinter, through habit, learns to align his eyes properly or to ignore the false image. He also discusses a well-known investigation (بحث مشهور) about the role of attention (التفات النفس) in perception: if the soul knows the reality, it can disregard the erroneous image. The commentator notes that even an artificial squinter could theoretically correct, but the natural squinter's habit is more ingrained. The passage ends with a description of how the natural squinter adjusts his gaze.

Key Terms
شعاع نافذ
شعاع منعكس
أحول
حول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural squinters always see one object as two.
Correction: The text explicitly states that natural squinters rarely see one as two due to their habit of arriving at correctness.

Checkpoint
Why does the natural squinter rarely see one object as two?
A) Because his nerves are perfectly aligned.
B) Due to his habit of arriving at correctness and attending to the correct image.
C) Because he has a stronger sense of sight.
D) Because he never squints.

Key Takeaway
Visual errors like the double moon and squinter's double vision show that the senses can be mistaken, underscoring the need for rational reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that even your own eyes can deceive you—seeing two moons or double objects. When was the last time you trusted your senses absolutely, only to discover later that you were mistaken? How does that experience challenge the way you judge truth in your daily life? Will you now pause before declaring something 'obviously true' just because you perceived it?
?
The natural squinter, through habit, learns to see correctly despite his physical condition. What habits have you cultivated to protect your heart and mind from spiritual or intellectual illusions? Are there any bad habits that distort your perception of truth? Commit today to one concrete habit that will help you see reality as it is, even when your desires or emotions make it appear otherwise.
?
If the simplest act of seeing can produce error, how much more careful must you be when relying on your own reasoning about matters of faith? Have you ever assumed your understanding of a religious text or teaching was correct simply because it felt right? This lesson warns that our natural faculties are not infallible. Which of your deeply held beliefs are you willing to re-examine in light of this truth?

Habit, Attention, and the Natural Squinter
The passage explains why natural squinters rarely err and discusses the role of habit and attention in perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of habit and the soul's attention in correcting perceptual errors, especially in the natural squinter.

Core Idea
Through habit, the natural squinter learns to attend to the correct image and overcome his visual defect.

The discussion deepens: why does the natural squinter rarely see one as two? The author explains that habit and the soul's attention play crucial roles. He also addresses an objection: if the squinter knows his perception is wrong, why doesn't the artificial squinter also correct? This leads to an analysis of how attention works in perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on habit
Focus on the commentary about the natural squinter's habit. Note that the squinter uses his eyes in a specific alignment to see correctly.
This builds on the earlier distinction between natural and deliberate squinting.
2
Understand the well-known investigation
The passage presents an objection: habit of correctness does not mean he sees one as one; he might see two but judges it as one.
This is a subtle philosophical point about perception vs. judgment.
3
Learn the role of attention
The answer is that perceptions depend on the soul's attention. When the soul knows the truth, it disregards the false image.
This is illustrated by considering how we focus on a point and ignore surroundings.
4
Follow the final resolution
The commentator explains that the natural squinter habitually adjusts his gaze to find the correct alignment, developing a skill so that he sees correctly.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main idea is clear.

By contrast, a natural squinter (الأحول الفطري) rarely makes this mistake because he has acquired the habit of correcting his vision. The commentator (الشارح) then explains the mechanism: the natural squinter, through habit, learns to align his eyes properly or to ignore the false image. He also discusses a well-known investigation (بحث مشهور) about the role of attention (التفات النفس) in perception: if the soul knows the reality, it can disregard the erroneous image. The commentator notes that even an artificial squinter could theoretically correct, but the natural squinter's habit is more ingrained. The passage ends with a description of how the natural squinter adjusts his gaze.

Misconception: That habit of arriving at correctness means the squinter perceives one object as one.
Correction: The passage explains that the squinter may see two but judges them to be one due to habit, and it is the soul's attention that prevents the false perception.

Sensory Errors: The Colored Wheel and the Mirage
The author provides two examples of sensory error: the colored wheel and the mirage, with commentary on their causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two examples of sensory error—the rotating colored wheel and the mirage—and the reasons why our senses can be deceived.

Core Idea
Our senses can blend colors and see non-existent things due to how perception is processed.

In this lesson, we examine two more cases where sensory perception leads to error: a spinning wheel with colored lines that appears as a single color, and the mirage that appears as water. The author explains the mechanisms behind these errors, involving the common sense and the visual ray. We also explore a debate about whether the mirage is truly seeing a non-existent object.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to get the gist of the two examples and the commentary.
Notice that the passage includes commentary within the text, indicated by parentheses.
2
Understand the wheel example
Focus on the first example: the rotating wheel with colored lines. Identify the two reasons given for why the colors appear blended.
The reasons involve the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and the visual ray.
3
Analyze the mirage example
Read the mirage section carefully. Note the objection that the mirage is not truly seeing non-existence because its source (the trembling ray) exists. Then follow the commentator’s response.
The commentator argues there are two errors: seeing the mirage itself as existent and seeing it as water.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how these examples illustrate that sensory error can arise from internal processing (mixing of effects) and from misidentification (confusing similar things).
Also note that the author’s inclusion of the mirage is defended by the commentator.

) كالرحى إذا أخرج من مركزها إلى محيطها خطوط كثيرة ( متقاربة ) في الوضع بألوان مختلفة فإنها إذا دارت ) سريعة جداً ( رؤيت) تلك الألوان الكثيرة ( كاللون الواحد الممتزج ) المؤلف (منها) والسبب في ذلك أن ما أدركه الحس الظاهر يتأدى أولا إلى الحس المشترك، ثم إلى الخيال فإذا أدرك البصر مثلاً لوناً، وانتقل منه بسرعة إلى لون آخر كان أثر اللون الأول باقيا في المشترك عند إدراك اللون الثاني ووصول أثره إليه، فيمتزج الأثران هناك فتراهما النفس لامتزاج أثريهما ممتزجين، ولا تقدر على تمييز أحدهما عن الآخر، وأيضاً لما وقع الشعاع البصري على تلك الألوان بأسرها في زمان قليل جداً لم تتمكن النفس من تمييز بعضها عن بعض، فلذلك رأتها ممتزجة (و) نرى ( المعدوم موجوداً كالسراب ) قيل هذا من اشتباه الشيء بمثله، فإن السراب

قوله : ( ثم إلى الخيال ) ذكره استطراداً ولا مدخل له في الغلط. قوله: (وأيضا الخ الوجه الأول مبني على الامتزاج في الحس المشترك، والثاني على الامتزاج في الباصرة . قوله : (قيل هذا الخ ) اعترض على المصنف بأن السراب ليس مما ذكره لأن السراب ليس معدوماً مطلقاً، أي باعتبار ذاته وباعتبار مأخذه بل مأخذه هو الشعاع المترجرج موجود، إلا أنه اشتبه عند الناظر بالماء بسبب تشابهه به فيكون من اشتباه الشيء بمثله، وعندي أن في السراب غلطين أحدهما رؤية نفسه فإنه أمر مخيل وليس في الخارج إلا الشعاع المترجرج وسبب تخيله ترجرجه كما اعترف به صاحب القيل فهو معدوم من حيث ذاته ويحسب الناظر أنه موجود وهو الذي قصده المصنف ولذا لم يقل : كالسراب يرى ماء وثانيهما رؤيته ماء، وهذا ما ذكره صاحب القيل وهو من اشتباه مخيل بمخيل مثله إذ ليس شيء من السراب والماء موجوداً، ولك
أن تقول : معنى كلام المصنف كالسراب كما في السراب فإنه يرى الماء المعدوم موجودا .

The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences. Like the millstone: if from its center to its circumference many lines close together in position of different colors are drawn, then if it rotates very rapidly, those many colors are seen as a single blended color composed of them. The reason is that what the external sense perceives is first conveyed to the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), then to the imagination (al-khayāl). So if the eye perceives, for example, a color, and moves quickly from it to another color, the effect of the first color remains in the common sense at the time of perceiving the second color and the arrival of its effect; so the two effects mix there, and the soul sees them as blended due to the mixing of their effects, and it cannot distinguish one from the other. Also, because the visual ray falls upon all those colors in a very short time, the soul cannot distinguish some from others, so it sees them as blended. And we see the non-existent as existent, like the mirage (al-sarāb). It is said that this is a case of mistaking a thing for its like, for the mirage... His saying: (That what the external sense perceives, whether the perception is by contact of the ray or by impression.) His saying: (is conveyed) what is meant by conveyance is not transition, because that is impossible for the form, but rather its occurrence in it after occurrence in the external sense. His saying: (then to the imagination) he mentioned it as a digression, and it has no bearing on the error. His saying: (and also) etc. The first explanation is based on mixing in the common sense, and the second on mixing in the seeing faculty. His saying: (it is said) etc. An objection to the author: The mirage is not what he mentioned because the mirage is not absolutely non-existent, i.e., in its essence and in its source; rather its source is the trembling ray which exists, but it is mistaken by the viewer for water due to resemblance, so it is a case of mistaking a thing for its like. In my view, in the mirage there are two errors: one is seeing itself, for it is an imaginary thing and nothing exists externally except the trembling ray, and the cause of its imagining is its trembling, as the one who made the objection admitted; so it is non-existent in terms of its essence, and the viewer supposes it exists—that is what the author intended, and so he did not say 'like the mirage, he sees water.' The second is seeing it as water, and this is what the objector mentioned; it is mistaking an imaginary thing for another imaginary thing like it, since neither the mirage nor water exists. But you may say: the meaning of the author's statement is 'like the mirage' as in the mirage, he sees non-existent water as existent. 'Qallamā' in the wording of the commentary is not for pure negation, although it may be used for that, as Abū 'Alī stated; so reflect, for it is subtle, even if all the observers have been heedless of it. His saying: (then to the imagination) this has no bearing on the main purpose; the intent of mentioning it is to show that the priority of conveyance to the common sense is relative to conveyance to the imagination, not to the external sense. His saying: (from the category of mistaking a thing for its like) as if the one who says this intends to object to the author that he should have mentioned this form in the second aspect of the aspects of sensory errors. And it can be said: he did not mention it there because there is no likeness in reality due to difference in essence; and as for the attribution of non-existence to them, it is because water is non-existent in reality, even if something appears to the sight.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of sensory errors (أغلاط الحس) as part of establishing necessary sciences. It presents two more examples. The first is the millstone (الرحى): when a wheel with many colored lines spins rapidly, the colors appear as a single blended color. The author gives two reasons for this: (1) The external sense transmits impressions to the common sense (الحس المشترك) and then to the imagination; if the eye moves quickly from one color to another, the effect of the first remains and mixes with the second in the common sense, so the soul sees them as blended. (2) The visual ray falls on all colors in a very short time, so the soul cannot distinguish them. The second example is the mirage (السراب), where something non-existent (water) is seen as existent. The commentator addresses an objection: some say the mirage is not a case of seeing non-existent because its source (the trembling ray) exists, and it is merely a confusion of a thing with its like. The commentator argues there are actually two errors: (a) seeing the mirage itself (which is imaginary and non-existent in essence) as existent, and (b) seeing it as water (which is also non-existent). He defends the author's inclusion of the mirage as an example of seeing the non-existent. The passage also contains glosses clarifying that 'conveyance' (التأدي) does not imply spatial motion, and that the mention of imagination is a digression.

Misconception: The mirage is only a case of mistaking one existing thing (refracted light) for another (water).
Correction: Actually, the commentator argues there are two errors: first, seeing the mirage itself (which is non-existent in essence) as existent, and second, identifying it as water. So the mirage involves seeing non-existence as existence, not just misidentification.

The Mirage Illusion Explained
The commentator explains the cause and nature of the mirage illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical cause of the mirage illusion according to the commentator.

Core Idea
The mirage is a real optical effect caused by trembling reflected rays.

Have you ever seen a pool of water on a hot road that vanishes as you approach? This is the mirage. The commentators of the classical tradition explained it not as a mere hallucination, but as a genuine optical phenomenon. Let's read how the commentator al-Isfahani breaks it down.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Don't worry about the physics terms yet—just get a sense of the argument.
Notice the technical term 'tarjarij' (trembling).
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on terms like 'shu'ā' (ray), 'in'ikās' (reflection), and 'tarjarij' (trembling). These are the building blocks of the explanation.
3
Follow the ray's journey
The commentator describes how visual rays from the eye hit the ground and reflect. Trace this path mentally: from eye to ground to eye again.
4
Grasp the reason for the illusion
The reflected rays appear like water because they are trembling and close to the ground, resembling flowing water in subtlety and fluidity.
This resolves the common misconception that the mirage is completely non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله يتراءى للبصر بسبب ترجرج الخ) الترجرج بالراءين المهملتين والجيمين الاضطراب ،والحركة وتحقيقه أن الخطوط الشعاعية لما وصلت إلى سطوح الأجزاء الصقيلة التي الأرض السبخة، انعكست مترجرجة لأن الشعاع المنعكس يكون مترجرجاً كشعاع الشمس المنعكس من الماء على الجدار، ولما كان زوايا الشعاع صغيرة متلاصقة بالأرض لكون وترها بقدر قامة الرائي، تكون زوايا الانعكاس أيضاً، كذلك لوجوب التساوي بين زوايتي الشعاع والانعكاس والشعاع المترجرج الملاصق بالأرض يرى كالماء الجاري على الأرض لمشابهته له وجود اللطافة والسيلان.
His saying: 'it appears to the eye due to tremor etc.' (Al-tarjarij) with two unpointed ra's and two jim's means disturbance and movement. Its verification is that when the radial lines reach the surfaces of the smooth parts of the salty land, they are reflected tremblingly, because the reflected ray becomes trembling like the ray of the sun reflected from water onto a wall. And since the angles of the ray are small and adjacent to the ground because its chord is equal to the height of the viewer, the angles of reflection are likewise, due to the necessity of equality between the two angles of incidence and reflection. And the trembling ray adjacent to the ground is seen as flowing water on the ground due to its resemblance to it in having subtlety and fluidity.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the mirage illusion. The commentator begins by defining 'tarjarij' as disturbance and movement. He then provides a physical analysis: radial lines from the eye reach the smooth surfaces of a salty land and are reflected tremblingly, like sunlight reflected from water. Because the incident angles are small and close to the ground, the reflected angles are equally small. The trembling reflected ray, due to its resemblance to water in subtlety and fluidity, is perceived as a sheet of flowing water. The commentator thus clarifies that the mirage is not a non-existent thing but a real optical effect caused by reflected light.

Key Terms
السراب
الشعاع البصري
الانعكاس
اللطافة
السيلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mirage is a complete illusion with no physical reality.
Correction: The mirage is caused by the trembling reflection of visual rays from the ground, producing a real optical effect resembling water.

Key Takeaway
The mirage is a real optical phenomenon resulting from trembling reflected rays.

Sleight of Hand: Definition and Mechanism
Defines sleight of hand and explains its cause as diversion of attention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sleight of hand is not real magic but a deception achieved by diverting the audience's attention through speed and misdirection, as explained by the commentator.

Core Idea
Sleight of hand works by distraction, not creation.

The commentator begins by defining sleight of hand (shu'badha) as hand-skill that shows a thing other than what it is. He then clarifies that the apparent object has no existence in that place, and explains the real cause: the juggler diverts attention. This lesson unpacks the definition, the mechanism from al-Razi, and a critique of an insufficient earlier explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms like الشعبذة and الأخذ بالعيون.
The passage includes lexical definitions and a detailed explanation from al-Razi.
2
Understand the definition
The passage defines shu'badha as 'lightness of hand' similar to magic. Note the clarification that the illusion has no external existence in that place, though it exists elsewhere.
This counters the misconception that the juggler creates something from nothing.
3
Analyze the mechanism
The commentator quotes al-Razi's explanation: the juggler distracts by showing one action, then quickly performs another elsewhere. Identify the two cooperating factors: preoccupation and speed.
The audience's attention is held by the first action, so the second goes unnoticed.
4
Reflect on the critique
The commentator notes that the earlier explanation only applies when the transition is to something similar, not when it is to something different. Consider why this distinction matters for the completeness of the explanation.
This shows the rigorous approach to causal explanations in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والشعبذة) الشعبذة والشعوذة خفة في اليد بمعنى واحد كالسحر يرى الشيء بغير ما عليه كذا في القاموس وفي شمس العلوم قال الخليل الشعوذة ليست من كلام أهل البادية. قوله مما لا وجود له في الخارج أصلاً لا ذاتاً ولا مأخذاً، ولذا يتعجب الناظرون منه لظهوره عما ينتظرونه، والمراد أنه لا وجود له في المكان الذي رئي فيه لا أنه لا له مطلقاً، فلا يرد أنه إذا كان سببه عدم التمييز بين الشيء، وما يشبهه بسبب سرعة الحركة كان كل منهما موجوداً، وتحقيقه ما ذكره الإمام في التفسير الكبير أن المشعبذ الحاذق يظهر عمل شيء، ويشغل أذهان الناظرين به ويأخذ عيونهم إليه، حتى إذا استغرقهم الشغل بذلك الشيء والتحديق نحوه عمل شيئاً آخر عملاً بسرعة فبقي هذا العمل خفياً لتعاون الشيئين اشتغالهم بالأمر الأول، وسرعة الإتيان بهذا العمل الثاني، وحينئذ يظهر لهم شيء آخر غير ما انتظروه فيعجبون منه، ولو أنه سكت ولم يتكلم بما يصرف الخواطر إلى ضد ما يريد أن يعمله، ولم تتحرك النفوس والأوهام إلى غير ما يريد إخراجه لفطن الناظرون لكل ما يفعله، فهذا هو المراد من قولهم أن المشعبد يأخذ بالعيون لأنه بالحقيقة يأخذ العيون إلى غير الجهة التي يحتال وكلما كان أخذه العيون والخواطر وجذبه لها إلى ما سوى مقصوده أقوى كان أحذق في عمله انتهى، وبهذا ظهر أن بيان الشارح للسبب قاصر فإنه إنما يجري في صورة يكون الانتقال إلى المشابه دون المخالف . قوله : ( وأما بسبب الخ الفرق بين الصورتين أن في الأولى يرى ما يرى في مكان غير الأول، وفي الثانية يرى في المكان الأول
His saying: 'and sleight of hand (al-shu'badha).' Al-shu'badha and al-shu'wadha are lightness of hand, synonymous, like magic, seeing a thing other than what it is. Thus in al-Qamus and in Shams al-'Ulum: al-Khalil said: al-shu'wadha is not from the speech of the desert dwellers. His saying: 'something that has no existence in externality at all, neither in essence nor in source,' and therefore onlookers marvel at it because it appears contrary to what they expect. The intended meaning is that it has no existence in the place where it was seen, not that it has no existence absolutely. So it is not objected that if its cause is the failure of the soul to distinguish between a thing and what resembles it due to speed of movement, then each of them exists. Its verification is what the Imam mentioned in al-Tafsir al-Kabir: the skillful juggler shows an action of something and occupies the minds of onlookers with it and takes their eyes to it, until when they are absorbed in that thing and staring at it, he performs another action quickly, so this action remains hidden due to the cooperation of two things: their preoccupation with the first matter and the speed of performing the second action. Then something other than what they expected appears to them, and they marvel at it. If he remained silent and did not speak in a way that diverts thoughts to the opposite of what he intends to do, and if souls and fancies did not move toward what he intends to produce, the onlookers would perceive everything he does. This is the meaning of their statement that the juggler 'takes the eyes,' because he in reality takes the eyes to a direction other than the one in which he works. The stronger his taking of eyes and thoughts and his drawing them to what is other than his aim, the more skillful he is in his work. End. And by this it is apparent that the commentator's explanation of the cause is deficient, for it applies only to the case of transition to the similar, not to the dissimilar. His saying: 'and as for due to, etc.' — the difference between the two forms: in the first, one sees what is seen in a place other than the first; in the second, one sees in the first place.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines and explains sleight of hand (shu'badha). The commentator first gives lexical definitions: it is lightness of hand like magic, showing a thing other than what it is. Then he clarifies that the apparent object has no external existence in that place, though it is not absolutely non-existent. The verification is from the Imam (al-Razi) in al-Tafsir al-Kabir: the juggler distracts the audience by showing one action and then quickly performs another elsewhere, so the audience's attention is diverted. The commentator then criticizes the earlier commentator's explanation as insufficient because it only covers cases where the transition is to something similar, not to something different. Finally, he notes the difference between two forms of illusion: one where the perception appears in a different place, and one where it appears in the same place.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sleight of hand involves actual magic or creation of objects from nothing.
Correction: The passage explains that the illusion is caused by diversion of attention and speed; the object has no existence in that place but exists elsewhere.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the sleight of hand artist appear to produce something from nothing?
A) He actually creates something new
B) He distracts the audience and performs a quick action elsewhere
C) He uses real magic
D) He relies on shadow play

Key Takeaway
Sleight of hand is an illusion caused by diverting attention and speed, not by creating non-existent objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Illusions of Continuity: The Line and the Circle
Commentator uses two examples to explain perceptual illusions from rapid motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rapid motion creates the illusion of continuous lines and circles through the mechanism of the common sense and the visual ray.

Core Idea
Continuous lines and circles perceived in motion are illusions created by the mind.

The commentator presents two familiar optical illusions — a falling drop appearing as a line and a spinning flame as a circle — to illustrate how sensory perception can be mistaken. By examining the underlying mechanism involving the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) and the visual ray (al-shu'a'), we gain insight into the limits of empirical observation, a topic relevant to theological epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation to understand the exact meaning.
Notice the author's confident tone: he states the illusions as known facts.
2
Identify the two phenomena
Find where the falling drop and the spinning flame are described. Note what is said about their existence: the line and circle have no real existence.
This sets up the puzzle: why do we see something that isn't there?
3
Understand the first reason: common sense
The commentator explains that the eye perceives the moving object in successive positions, but before the image fades from the common sense, the next image arrives, causing the images to connect and appear continuous.
The common sense is the faculty that combines sensory inputs; it retains images briefly.
4
Understand the second reason: the visual ray
Additionally, because the visual ray contacts the moving object in multiple places within a very short time, it is as if the ray contacts all those places at once, producing a continuous impression.
This reinforces the idea of temporal integration.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why a theologian would discuss optical illusions. How does this relate to the reliability of sense perception as a source of knowledge?
This passage is from a discussion that likely warns against trusting sensory appearances without rational scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكالخط لنزول القطرة فإن القطرة إذ نزلت سريعا يرى هناك خط مستقيم، ولا وجود له قطعاً والدائرة لإدارة الشعلة بسرعة فإنها إذا أديرت بسرعة شديدة يرى هناك دائرة من النار ولا وجود لها بلا شبهة. والسبب في هذين أن البصر إذا أدرك القطرة، أو الشعلة في موضع وأداها إلى الحس المشترك، ثم أدركها في موضع آخر قبل أن يزول أثرها عن الحس المشترك اتصل هناك صورتها في الموضع الثاني بصورتها في الموضع الأول، فيرى كما مر ممتداً إما على الاستقامة أو الاستدارة، وأيضاً لما اتصل الشعاع بها في مواضع متعددة في زمان قليل جداً، كان ذلك بمنزلة اتصال الشعاع بها في تلك المواضع دفعة واحدة فيرى لذلك خطاً مستقيماً، أو دائرة.
And like the line from the falling drop: when a drop falls quickly, a straight line is seen there, and it absolutely has no existence. And the circle from the rapid rotation of a flame: when it is rotated very quickly, a circle of fire is seen there, and it has no existence without doubt. The reason in these two is that when the eye perceives the drop or flame in one place and conveys it to the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), then perceives it in another place before its impression fades from the common sense, its image in the second place connects with its image in the first place, so it is seen, as previously, extended either straight or circular. Also, because the ray (al-shu'a') contacted it in multiple places in a very short time, that is like the ray contacting it in those places all at once, so a straight line or a circle is seen accordingly.

Commentary
This passage explains two illusions: a falling drop appearing as a continuous straight line, and a rapidly spinning flame appearing as a circle. The commentator first states the phenomena: the drop creates a line with no real existence, and the spinning flame creates a circle with no real existence. Then he gives the reason: the eye perceives the object in one position and sends it to the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). Before the impression fades, the eye perceives it in another position, and the two images connect, producing the appearance of a continuous line or circle. Additionally, because the visual ray (al-shu'a') contacts the moving object in multiple positions in a very short time, it is as if the ray contacts all those positions simultaneously, resulting in a straight line or circle.

Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the line and circle have no existence ('لا وجود له قطعاً', 'ولا وجود لها بلا شبهة'). They are purely perceptual artifacts arising from the integration of discrete sensations.

Checkpoint

B) The eye retains the image of the drop in earlier positions while it moves
C) The air around it glows
D) The drop leaves a trail

Key Takeaway
Rapid motion produces the illusion of continuous lines or circles because the common sense retains successive images and the visual ray contacts multiple points almost simultaneously.

Illusions of Motion and Rest: Shadow and Ship
The commentator explains why slow motion appears static and why a passenger feels the shore moving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the estimation faculty (al-wahm) interprets motion and rest based on relative position changes, and why we sometimes perceive the opposite.

Core Idea
Motion is not directly sensed; it is inferred from changing positions.

The commentator explains two common perceptual paradoxes: a shadow appears still yet moves with the sun, and a ship passenger feels the shore moving. These cases reveal that motion and rest are judgments made by the soul based on relative changes, not direct sense data. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its implications for epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the two main examples (shadow and ship) and the objection about the shadow's nature.
The passage includes both matn and commentary; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the perceptual illusions
Find where the commentator states that we see the moving as stationary (shadow) and the stationary as moving (shore). Understand the reason: the soul judges based on the degree of change in positions.
3
Understand the role of al-wahm
The commentator explains that motion is not directly perceived but extracted by the estimation faculty from differences in relative positions. If the change is too small or absent, the soul judges stillness.
This is the key epistemological point.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this principle applies to other cases like the horse rider. The commentator shows that when there is clear change and dissimilar parts, true motion is perceived.
Motion and rest are relational, not absolute, in our perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) نرى المتحرك ساكناً وبالعكس أي ونرى الساكن متحركاً ( كالظل يرى ساكناً) أن البصر إذا أدرك الشيء في موضع محاذياً لشيء بعدما أدركه في موضع آخر محاذياً لذلك الشيء، حكمت النفس بالحركة فإذا كانت المسافة في غاية القلة لم تميز النفس بين الموضعين والمحاذاتين، وحكمت بالسكون (وهو متحرك) أبداً لأن الشمس متحركة دائماً إما ارتفاعاً أو انحطاطاً، فلا بد أن يتحرك الظل انتقاصاً أو ازدياداً، فإن قيل : الظل مرتبة من مراتب النور الذي هو عرض فلا يكون متحركاً، قلنا : المقصود أنه أحدهما قطعاً يرى على حالة واحدة ولا يحس بازدياده وانتقاصه مع أنه لا يخلو عن وكراكب السفينة المتحركة يراها ساكنة و يرى الشط الساكن متحركاً، وذلك لأنه لما لم يتبدل وضع الراكب بالنسبة إلى السفينة حسب نفسه، والسفينة ساكنين ولما تبدل محاذاته لأجزاء الشط مع تخيله السكون في نفسه وفي السفينة حسب الشط. قوله : أن البصر الخ يعني أن الحركة ليست بمبصرة بالذات بل ينتزعها الوهم عن أن الشيء المبصر، أو الملموس بتوسط اختلاف أوضاعه بالقياس إلى غيره، فإذا كان تغير الأوضاع مستفاداً من الإحساس حكمت النفس بالحركة وإلا فلا. قوله : ( الظل مرتبة من مراتب الخ فإن النور القائم بالمضيء لذاته يسمى ضوءاً، والقائم بالمضيء بغيره يسمى ظلاً. قوله: (المقصود أنه (الخ يعني ليس المراد بالسكون والحركة النقلة وعدمها بل التغير، وعدم التغير فالمعني أن الظل يرى غير متغير وجوداً وعدماً في أجزاء ما وقع عليه، وهو في الواقع متغير بالوجود والعدم بسبب حركة الشمس، وتبدل محاذاة ما وقع عليه بها وهذا مع ظهوره قد خفي على بعض الناظرين وزل فيه قدمه . قوله : (مع تخيله السكون (الخ لعدم تبدل الأوضاع بينهما، وأما تبدل أوضاعها بالنسبة إلى الماء فلا يحس به أيضاً لتشابه أجزاء الماء، وإنما يحس التبدل بالقياس إلى الشط، فيحسبه متحركاً بخلاف راكب الفرس، فإنه يحس بتبدل أوضاعه بالقياس إلى الفرس بالحركة القسرية، ويحس بتبدل أوضاع الفرس بالقياس إلى الأرض لعدم تشابه أجزائه، وإذا لو عرض له الغفلة بسبب تفكر قلبه في شيء، أو تكلف الغفلة يحسب أن الأرض متحركة إلى خلاف جهة حركة الفرس .
And we see the moving as stationary and vice versa, i.e., we see the stationary as moving: like the shadow seen as stationary: when the eye perceives a thing in a position adjacent to something after having perceived it in another position adjacent to that something, the soul judges movement. But if the distance is extremely small, the soul does not distinguish between the two positions and the two adjacencies, and judges stasis (while it is moving) always, because the sun is always moving either upward or downward, so the shadow must move by decreasing or increasing. If it is said: the shadow is a rank of light, which is an accident, so it cannot move; we reply: the intended meaning is that it is one of them definitively seen in a single state, and its increase and decrease are not sensed, while it is never free from them. And like the passenger of a moving ship: he sees it as stationary and sees the stationary shore as moving. That is because when the passenger's position relative to the ship does not change, he imagines himself and the ship as stationary, and when his alignment with parts of the shore changes, with his imagination of stillness in himself and in the ship, he imagines the shore [moving]. His saying: 'the eye, etc.' means that motion is not perceived by itself, but the estimation (al-wahm) extracts it from the perceived or touched object by means of the difference of its positions relative to another. So if the change of positions is derived from sensation, the soul judges motion; otherwise it does not. His saying: 'the shadow is a rank of light, etc.' — for the light subsisting in the self-luminous is called light (daw'), and that subsisting in what is illuminated by another is called shadow. His saying: 'the intended meaning is that, etc.' — that is, the intended meaning of stillness and motion is not locomotion and its absence, but change and absence of change. So the meaning is that the shadow is seen as unchanging in existence and non-existence in the parts it falls upon, while in reality it is changing in existence and non-existence due to the sun's motion and the change of alignment of what it falls upon. And this, despite its obviousness, has been hidden from some observers, and their foot has slipped in it. His saying: 'with his imagination of stillness, etc.' — due to the absence of change of positions between them. As for the change of their positions relative to the water, it is also not sensed due to the similarity of the parts of water. Rather, the change is sensed relative to the shore, so he reckons it moving. This is unlike the horse rider, who senses the change of his positions relative to the horse by forced motion, and senses the change of the horse's positions relative to the ground due to the dissimilarity of its parts. And if he happens to be inattentive due to his heart's reflection on something, or if he feigns inattention, he reckons the earth moving in the opposite direction of the horse's motion.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage deals with illusions of motion and rest. It begins by stating that we sometimes see the moving as stationary (e.g., a shadow) and the stationary as moving (e.g., a shore). For the shadow, the eye perceives the shadow in adjacent positions, but because the change is very small, the soul judges it to be stationary, whereas it is actually moving due to the sun. An objection is raised: the shadow is an accident, not a body, so how can it move? The answer is that 'motion' here means change (in extension) not locomotion. The commentator then explains that motion is not directly perceived but inferred by the estimation faculty (al-wahm) from changes in position relative to other objects. For the ship passenger, the lack of relative change between himself and the ship leads him to imagine both stationary, while the shore's change relative to him is perceived as the shore moving. A comparison with a horse rider shows that when there is clear change due to forced motion and dissimilar parts, the true motion is perceived.

السكون
الظل
الراكب
السفينة
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That motion is directly perceived by the senses.
Correction: Motion is not directly sensed; it is inferred by the estimation faculty (al-wahm) from changes in relative positions. This is why illusions like the shadow and ship occur.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does a passenger on a moving ship perceive the shore as moving?
A) The ship is actually stationary
B) The passenger's position relative to the ship does not change, so he imagines both stationary, and the shore appears to move

D) The passenger is dizzy

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are relational judgments made by the estimation faculty, not direct sensory perceptions.

The Moon's Apparent Motion: Sensory Error Due to Relative Motion
First example: moon illusion due to relative motion of cloud and observer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent motion of the moon can be mistaken due to the relative motion of clouds or the observer, demonstrating that sensory perception is fallible.

Core Idea
Even the moon's motion can be misperceived; senses mislead without rational analysis.

This lesson introduces the first of several optical illusions presented by the author to prove that our senses are not always reliable. The example of the moon's motion shows how the eye can be tricked by the movement of intervening clouds or by the observer's own movement. By understanding the mechanics of this illusion, you will see why the author argues for the necessity of rational demonstration over mere sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the moon illusion and its commentary. Pay attention to the two distinct scenarios.
The passage includes both the matn and the sharh (commentary).
2
Understand the two scenarios
Identify the two cases: when the cloud moves faster in the moon's direction, and when the observer moves. Note how each leads to a false perception of the moon's motion.
In the first scenario, the moon appears to move east; in the second, it appears to follow the observer.
3
Analyze the cause of error
The commentary explains that the error arises because the eye attributes the change in relative position to the moon, being occupied by its brightness. Recognize that the senses do not automatically correct for relational motion.
This is a key point: sensory information is raw and requires rational interpretation.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this example supports the need for necessary sciences ('ilm al-ḍarūriyya) that are certain and not based on fallible senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية
متحركاً (و) نرى ( المتحرك إلى جهة متحركاً إلى خلافها كالقمر) نراه (سائراً إلى الغيم حين يسير الغيم إليه ) ، فإن القمر يتحرك بحركة الفلك من المشرق إلى المغرب أبداً، فإذا كان بيننا وبينه غيم غير ساتر إياه، ونظرنا إليه نفذ شعاع البصر منا في جزء من أجزاء ذلك الغيم، فإذا فرضنا حركة الغيم من المشرق إلى المغرب أيضاً، كانت هذه الحركة لقرب الغيم منا أسرع في الرؤية من حركة القمر لبعده عنا، فيصير ذلك الجزء الذي كان قد نفذ الشعاع فيه قريباً من القمر، ونفذ الشعاع في جزء آخر قد حاذاه بالحركة، فيقع بين الجزئين قطعة من الغيم فيتخيل أن القمر بحركته إلى المشرق قطع تلك القطعة التي هي بمنزلة المسافة ( وإذا تحركنا إلى جهة رأيناه ) أي القمر ( متحركا إليها ) إن كان هناك غيم رقيق وسببه أن الوضع بيننا وبين القمر يتغير بالنسبة إلى أجزاء الغيم ويقع بيننا وبينه أجزاء منه على التعاقب في جهة حركتنا، فيتخيل أن القمر تحرك إلى تلك الجهة، وقطع قطعة من ذلك الغيم ( وإن تحرك ) القمر ( إلى خلافها ) كما إذا كان حركتنا نحو المشرق فإن القمر متحرك نحو المغرب (و) نرى (الشجر)

قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا الخ فرض حركة الغيم إلى جهة حركة القمر ليظهر غلط الحس ظهوراً تاماً، بخلاف ما إذا فرض حركته مخالفة لحركة القمر في الجهة، فإنه يظهر حركة القمر فيه أسرع من حركته في الصحو لكن الغلط في هذا الاعتبار أخفى من الغلط في الاعتبار الأول. قوله : ( أسرع في الرؤية وإن كان في الواقع حركة القمر أسرع منه بأضعاف لا تحصى. قوله : ( فيتخيل أن القمر (الخ) بناء على تبديل الوضع بينه وبين الغيم، واشتغال الحس بالقمر لكونه ضوء من الغيم، فينسب تغير الوضع إليه فيحسبه متحركاً، فقد اشتبه على البصر حركة الغيم بحركة القمر.
قوله : ( إلى جهة أي مغايرة لجهة حركة القمر سواء كانت مقابلة لها كما إذا تحركتا نحو الشرق أو لا كما إذا تحركتا إلى جهة الشمال أو الجنوب، ثم إذا كانت هذه الحركة سريعة يثبت حركة القمر سريعة وإذا كانت بطيئة فبطيئة.
قوله : ( إن كان هناك غيم) إما متحرك أو غير متحرك إلى خلاف جهة حركتنا، أما إذا كان متحركاً إلى جهة حركتنا فلا يعرض هذا الغلط لعدم وقوع أجزاء الغيم بينه وبيننا على التعاقب في جهة حركتنا .
قوله : ( فيتخيل الخ) لأنه تبدل وضع الغيم بسبب حركتنا ونسب التبدل إلى القمر بناء على اشتغال الحس به .
قوله : ( ونرى الخ هذا إذا كانت رؤية الشجر بتوسط الماء.
قوله : ( ونفذ الشعاع في جزء آخر أي غير ملاق للجزء الأول وإلا لم يقع بين الجزأين المذكورين قطعة من الغيم، وإنما لم يصرح بذلك لظهور أن رؤية حركة القمر لا يكون إلا في هذه الصورة .

المستقيم ( على الشط منتكساً) في الماء وذلك لأن الخطوط الشعاعية المنعكسة من سطح الماء إلى الشجر ، إنما تنعكس إليه على هيئة أوتار الآلة الحدباء المسماة في الفارسية بجنك، فإذا كان الشجر على الطرف الآخر من الماء انعكس الشعاع إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أقرب من الرائي وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أبعد منه، وهكذا وإذا كان الشجر على طرف الرائي كان الأمر في الانعكاس على عكس ما ذكر، ألا ترى أنك إذا سترت سطح الماء من جانبك ستر عنك رأس الشجر في الصورة الأولى، وقاعدتها في الصورة الثانية فيكون الخط الشعاعي المنعكس إلى رأس الشجر أطول تلك الخطوط المنعكسة إلى ما دونه ويكون ما هو أقرب منه أطول مماهو من جميع أبعد منه على الترتيب حتى يكون أقصرها هو المنعكس إلى قاعدة الشجر ، ثم إن النفس لا تدرك الانعكاس لتعودها في رؤية المرئيات بنفوذ الشعاع على الاستقامة، فتحسب الشعاع المنعكس نافذاً في الماء ولا نفوذ هناك؛ إذ ربما لا يكون الماء عميقاً بقدر طول الشجر فتحسب لذلك أن رأس الشجر أكثر نزولاً في الماء لكون الشعاع المنعكس إليه أطول، وكذا الحال في باقي الأجزاء على الترتيب، فتراه كأنه منتكس تحت سطح الماء (و) نرى ( الوجه طويلاً وعريضاً ومعوجاً بحسب اختلاف شكل المرآة ) إذا فرض المرآة كنصف قالب أسطوانة مستديرة، فإن نظر إليها بحيث يكون طولها محاذياً لطول الوجه يرى الوجه فيها طويلاً بقدر طوله قليل العرض، وذلك

قوله : ( إنما تنعكس الخ لوجوب تساوي زاويتي الشعاع والانعكاس وقد بينه الشارح في بحث الأبصار .
قوله : ( كان الأمر (الخ أي في الانعكاس بأن ينعكس إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أبعد من الرائي، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه لوجوب تساوي الزاويتين، وأما الشجر فيرى على الانعكاس كما في الصورة الأولى، سواء كان الرائي قريباً من الشجر أو متصلاً به كما يرى نفسه منتكساً لكون الخط الشعاعي المنعكس إلى رأسه من موضع أبعد ، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه .
قوله : ( طويلاً بقدر طوله فمعنى قوله نرى الوجه طويلاً أنا نراه فنحسبه طويلاً مما هو عليه بسبب قصر عرضه وعريضاً عما هو عليه بواسطة قصر طوله .
قوله : ( على عكس ما ذكر يعني أنه ينعكس إلى رأس الشجرة من موضع أبعد من الرائي، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه وينبغي أن يعلم أن القرب والبعد إنما هو بالنسبة إلى قدم الرائي لا عينه، فإنك إذا حدبت ظهرك وقربت عينك من الماء فلا شك أن الموضع الذي ينعكس الشعاع إلى رأس الشجر، الذي في جانبك قد يكون أقرب إلى عينك وهو ظاهر بالتخيل.
منه
The Fourth Observatory – On Proving Necessary Sciences. (It is seen as) moving, and we see the moving thing toward a direction as moving toward its opposite, like the moon: we see it (moving toward the cloud when the cloud moves toward it). For the moon moves with the motion of the sphere from east to west always. So if there is between us and it a cloud not covering it, and we look at it, the ray of sight from us penetrates a part of that cloud. If we suppose the motion of the cloud from east to west as well, this motion, because the cloud is closer to us, is faster in vision than the motion of the moon because of its distance from us. Then that part in which the ray had penetrated becomes close to the moon, and the ray penetrates another part that has aligned with it by motion, so a piece of cloud falls between the two parts, and it is imagined that the moon, by its motion to the east, cut that piece which is like the distance. (And if we move toward a direction, we see it) i.e., the moon (moving toward that direction) if there is a thin cloud. The reason is that the position between us and the moon changes relative to the parts of the cloud, and parts of it come between us and it successively in the direction of our motion, so it is imagined that the moon moved to that direction and cut a piece of that cloud. (And if the moon moves) (toward its opposite) as when our motion is toward the east, for the moon moves toward the west. (And we see the tree) His words: “(For if we suppose etc.)” – he supposes the motion of the cloud in the direction of the moon’s motion to make the error of sense appear completely, unlike if he supposes its motion opposite to the moon’s motion in direction, then the moon’s motion appears faster in it than its motion in clear weather, but the error in this consideration is subtler than the error in the first consideration. His words: “(faster in vision)” although in reality the moon’s motion is faster than it by countless multiples. His words: “(then it is imagined that the moon etc.)” – based on the change of position between it and the cloud, and the sense being occupied with the moon because it is brighter than the cloud, so it attributes the change of position to it and supposes it moving. Thus the motion of the cloud is confused with the motion of the moon by the sight. His words: “(to a direction)” – i.e., different from the direction of the moon’s motion, whether opposite, as when both move toward the east, or not, as when both move toward north or south. Then if this motion is fast, the moon’s motion is judged fast, and if slow, then slow. His words: “(if there is a cloud)” – either moving or not moving opposite to the direction of our motion. But if it is moving in the direction of our motion, this error does not occur because parts of the cloud do not come between it and us successively in the direction of our motion. His words: “(it is imagined etc.)” – because the position of the cloud changes due to our motion, and the change is attributed to the moon based on the sense being occupied with it. His words: “(And we see etc.)” – this is when the vision of the tree is through water. His words: “(And the ray penetrates another part)” – i.e., not touching the first part, otherwise no piece of cloud would fall between the two mentioned parts. He did not state this explicitly because it is obvious that the vision of the moon’s motion only occurs in this form. The straight (tree) inverted on the bank in the water, because the reflected light rays from the water surface to the tree are reflected to it in the form of the strings of the arched instrument called in Persian “chang”. So if the tree is on the other side of the water, the ray is reflected to the top of the tree from a place closer to the observer, and to what is below its top from a place farther from it, and so on. And if the tree is on the observer’s side, the matter in reflection is the opposite of what was mentioned. Do you not see that if you cover the water surface from your side, it covers from you the top of the tree in the first image, and its base in the second image. So the reflected ray to the top of the tree is the longest of those reflected rays to what is below it, and what is closer is longer than what is farther in order, until the shortest is the one reflected to the base of the tree. Then the soul does not perceive reflection because it is accustomed in seeing visible things to the penetration of the ray straight, so it supposes the reflected ray to be penetrating the water, but there is no penetration; for perhaps the water is not as deep as the length of the tree, so it supposes that the top of the tree is more submerged in the water because the ray reflected to it is longer. The same holds for the remaining parts in order, so it sees it as if it were inverted under the water surface. (And we see the face long, wide, and twisted according to the difference of the mirror’s shape) if the mirror is supposed to be like half a cylindrical mold. Then if one looks into it such that its length aligns with the length of the face, one sees the face in it as long to the extent of its length, with little width. And that is because... His words: “(They are only reflected etc.)” – due to the necessity of equality of the two angles of incidence and reflection, which the commentator has explained in the discussion of vision. His words: “(the matter etc.)” – i.e., in reflection, that it is reflected to the top of the tree from a place farther from the observer, and to what is below its top from a place closer to it, due to the necessity of equality of the two angles. And the tree is seen in reflection as in the first image, whether the observer is close to the tree or connected to it, just as one sees oneself inverted because the reflected ray to one’s head is from a farther place, and to what is below one’s head from a closer place. His words: “(long to the extent of its length)” – meaning his words: “we see the face long” means we see it and we suppose it longer than it is due to the shortness of its width, and wider than it is due to the shortness of its length. His words: “(contrary to what was mentioned)” – meaning it is reflected to the top of the tree from a place farther from the observer, and to what is below its top from a place closer to it. And one should know that nearness and farness are only relative to the observer’s foot, not his eye. For if you curve your back and bring your eye close to the water, there is no doubt that the place from which the ray is reflected to the top of the tree that is on your side may be closer to your eye, and that is apparent by imagination. End.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a series of examples from sensory perception intended to prove that the senses can err, thereby establishing the need for necessary sciences that are rationally certain. The author presents three optical illusions: the apparent motion of the moon due to clouds, the inversion of a tree reflected in water, and the distortion of a face in a curved mirror. Each example is presented with a literal quotation from the original text (the matn) followed by detailed commentary. The first example explains how the moon, which actually moves west, can appear to move east when clouds move faster in the same direction; the commentary emphasizes that the error occurs because the eye is misled by the changing position relative to the cloud and attributes the motion incorrectly. The second example describes why a tree on a riverbank appears inverted in water: the reflected light rays are longer or shorter depending on the angle, and the mind, accustomed to direct vision, misinterprets the reflected rays as if they penetrate the water, leading to the illusion of inversion. The commentary adds the principle of equal angles of incidence and reflection. The third example shows how a cylindrical mirror makes a face appear stretched or distorted, with the commentary explaining that the perceived distortion results from the relative lengths of the reflected rays due to the mirror's curvature. Throughout, the author demonstrates that sensory perception is unreliable and that rational analysis is required to correct these illusions, thus arguing for the necessity of demonstrative knowledge ('ilm al-yaqini) as opposed to mere sensory experience.

Key Terms
العلوم الضرورية
شعاع البصر
الخطأ الحسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The senses always provide accurate information about motion.
Correction: This example shows that apparent motion can be reversed or altered due to relative movement and psychological factors, proving that sensory perception requires rational correction.

Checkpoint
Why does the moon appear to move toward the east in the first example?
A) Because the moon actually reverses direction momentarily.
B) Because the cloud moves faster in the same direction as the moon, causing the observer to attribute the cloud's motion to the moon.
C) Because the observer is moving, not the moon.
D) Because the moon's light is refracted through the cloud.

Key Takeaway
Relative motion between observer, object, and intervening medium can cause sensory illusions that only rational analysis can correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time your senses deceived you—a mirage, an optical illusion, or a misjudgment of distance. How did you discover the truth? Was it through rational analysis or further sensory input? What does that say about your trust in your own senses? Will you now pause before accepting what you see or hear, and ask what rational check is needed? Commit to one area of your life where you will apply this lesson today.
?
The moon illusion shows that even the most reliable sensory observation—the motion of the moon—can be mistaken. What belief do you hold about yourself, your faith, or the world that you have never critically examined? Could it be based on a 'relative motion' of your desires, habits, or cultural assumptions? Before you finish this reflection, write down one such belief and the rational question you will ask to test it.
?
The author argues that rational analysis must correct sensory data. When was the last time you actively used rational thought to override your immediate perception or feeling? If you find it hard to recall, why do you think you rely on feelings more than reason? Does this lesson convict you? Resolve to use reason over impulse at least once today, and reflect tonight on what you learned.

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
The Inverted Tree: Reflection Illusion in Water
Second example: tree inversion due to reflection in water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a tree on a riverbank appears inverted in water due to the geometry of reflected light rays and the mind's habitual interpretation.

Core Idea
Reflection misleads the eye; what appears inverted is due to ray geometry, not reality.

The second example of sensory error involves the reflection of a tree in water. The author explains how the tree appears upside down because the reflected rays from different parts of the tree travel different distances, and the mind, accustomed to direct, straight-line vision, misinterprets these rays as penetrating the water. This illusion further demonstrates that sensory perception requires rational scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the tree reflection and its commentary. Note the detailed explanation of ray paths.
The passage includes the matn and extensive commentary on reflection geometry.
2
Understand the reflection geometry
The rays from the top and base of the tree reflect at different angles, resulting in different path lengths. The ray from the top is longer, making it appear deeper in the water.
The commentary mentions the principle of equal angles of incidence and reflection.
3
Grasp the psychological factor
The soul is accustomed to rays traveling straight, so it interprets the reflected rays as if they penetrate the water, leading to the illusion of inversion.
This is a key point: habitual expectation influences perception.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this illusion underscores the fallibility of senses and the need for rational analysis to correct such errors.

المرصد الرابع - في إثبات العلوم الضرورية
متحركاً (و) نرى ( المتحرك إلى جهة متحركاً إلى خلافها كالقمر) نراه (سائراً إلى الغيم حين يسير الغيم إليه ) ، فإن القمر يتحرك بحركة الفلك من المشرق إلى المغرب أبداً، فإذا كان بيننا وبينه غيم غير ساتر إياه، ونظرنا إليه نفذ شعاع البصر منا في جزء من أجزاء ذلك الغيم، فإذا فرضنا حركة الغيم من المشرق إلى المغرب أيضاً، كانت هذه الحركة لقرب الغيم منا أسرع في الرؤية من حركة القمر لبعده عنا، فيصير ذلك الجزء الذي كان قد نفذ الشعاع فيه قريباً من القمر، ونفذ الشعاع في جزء آخر قد حاذاه بالحركة، فيقع بين الجزئين قطعة من الغيم فيتخيل أن القمر بحركته إلى المشرق قطع تلك القطعة التي هي بمنزلة المسافة ( وإذا تحركنا إلى جهة رأيناه ) أي القمر ( متحركا إليها ) إن كان هناك غيم رقيق وسببه أن الوضع بيننا وبين القمر يتغير بالنسبة إلى أجزاء الغيم ويقع بيننا وبينه أجزاء منه على التعاقب في جهة حركتنا، فيتخيل أن القمر تحرك إلى تلك الجهة، وقطع قطعة من ذلك الغيم ( وإن تحرك ) القمر ( إلى خلافها ) كما إذا كان حركتنا نحو المشرق فإن القمر متحرك نحو المغرب (و) نرى (الشجر)

قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا الخ فرض حركة الغيم إلى جهة حركة القمر ليظهر غلط الحس ظهوراً تاماً، بخلاف ما إذا فرض حركته مخالفة لحركة القمر في الجهة، فإنه يظهر حركة القمر فيه أسرع من حركته في الصحو لكن الغلط في هذا الاعتبار أخفى من الغلط في الاعتبار الأول. قوله : ( أسرع في الرؤية وإن كان في الواقع حركة القمر أسرع منه بأضعاف لا تحصى. قوله : ( فيتخيل أن القمر (الخ) بناء على تبديل الوضع بينه وبين الغيم، واشتغال الحس بالقمر لكونه ضوء من الغيم، فينسب تغير الوضع إليه فيحسبه متحركاً، فقد اشتبه على البصر حركة الغيم بحركة القمر.
قوله : ( إلى جهة أي مغايرة لجهة حركة القمر سواء كانت مقابلة لها كما إذا تحركتا نحو الشرق أو لا كما إذا تحركتا إلى جهة الشمال أو الجنوب، ثم إذا كانت هذه الحركة سريعة يثبت حركة القمر سريعة وإذا كانت بطيئة فبطيئة.
قوله : ( إن كان هناك غيم) إما متحرك أو غير متحرك إلى خلاف جهة حركتنا، أما إذا كان متحركاً إلى جهة حركتنا فلا يعرض هذا الغلط لعدم وقوع أجزاء الغيم بينه وبيننا على التعاقب في جهة حركتنا .
قوله : ( فيتخيل الخ) لأنه تبدل وضع الغيم بسبب حركتنا ونسب التبدل إلى القمر بناء على اشتغال الحس به .
قوله : ( ونرى الخ هذا إذا كانت رؤية الشجر بتوسط الماء.
قوله : ( ونفذ الشعاع في جزء آخر أي غير ملاق للجزء الأول وإلا لم يقع بين الجزأين المذكورين قطعة من الغيم، وإنما لم يصرح بذلك لظهور أن رؤية حركة القمر لا يكون إلا في هذه الصورة .

قوله : ( إنما تنعكس الخ لوجوب تساوي زاويتي الشعاع والانعكاس وقد بينه الشارح في بحث الأبصار .
قوله : ( كان الأمر (الخ أي في الانعكاس بأن ينعكس إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أبعد من الرائي، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه لوجوب تساوي الزاويتين، وأما الشجر فيرى على الانعكاس كما في الصورة الأولى، سواء كان الرائي قريباً من الشجر أو متصلاً به كما يرى نفسه منتكساً لكون الخط الشعاعي المنعكس إلى رأسه من موضع أبعد ، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه .
قوله : ( طويلاً بقدر طوله فمعنى قوله نرى الوجه طويلاً أنا نراه فنحسبه طويلاً مما هو عليه بسبب قصر عرضه وعريضاً عما هو عليه بواسطة قصر طوله .
قوله : ( على عكس ما ذكر يعني أنه ينعكس إلى رأس الشجرة من موضع أبعد من الرائي، وإلى ما تحت رأسه من موضع أقرب منه وينبغي أن يعلم أن القرب والبعد إنما هو بالنسبة إلى قدم الرائي لا عينه، فإنك إذا حدبت ظهرك وقربت عينك من الماء فلا شك أن الموضع الذي ينعكس الشعاع إلى رأس الشجر، الذي في جانبك قد يكون أقرب إلى عينك وهو ظاهر بالتخيل.

Thus the motion of the cloud is confused with the motion of the moon by the sight. His words: “(to a direction)” – i.e., different from the direction of the moon’s motion, whether opposite, as when both move toward the east, or not, as when both move toward north or south. Then if this motion is fast, the moon’s motion is judged fast, and if slow, then slow. His words: “(if there is a cloud)” – either moving or not moving opposite to the direction of our motion. But if it is moving in the direction of our motion, this error does not occur because parts of the cloud do not come between it and us successively in the direction of our motion. His words: “(it is imagined etc.)” – because the position of the cloud changes due to our motion, and the change is attributed to the moon based on the sense being occupied with it. His words: “(And we see etc.)” – this is when the vision of the tree is through water. His words: “(And the ray penetrates another part)” – i.e., not touching the first part, otherwise no piece of cloud would fall between the two mentioned parts. He did not state this explicitly because it is obvious that the vision of the moon’s motion only occurs in this form. The straight (tree) inverted on the bank in the water, because the reflected light rays from the water surface to the tree are reflected to it in the form of the strings of the arched instrument called in Persian “chang”. So if the tree is on the other side of the water, the ray is reflected to the top of the tree from a place closer to the observer, and to what is below its top from a place farther from it, and so on. And if the tree is on the observer’s side, the matter in reflection is the opposite of what was mentioned. Do you not see that if you cover the water surface from your side, it covers from you the top of the tree in the first image, and its base in the second image. So the reflected ray to the top of the tree is the longest of those reflected rays to what is below it, and what is closer is longer than what is farther in order, until the shortest is the one reflected to the base of the tree. Then the soul does not perceive reflection because it is accustomed in seeing visible things to the penetration of the ray straight, so it supposes the reflected ray to be penetrating the water, but there is no penetration; for perhaps the water is not as deep as the length of the tree, so it supposes that the top of the tree is more submerged in the water because the ray reflected to it is longer. The same holds for the remaining parts in order, so it sees it as if it were inverted under the water surface. (And we see the face long, wide, and twisted according to the difference of the mirror’s shape) if the mirror is supposed to be like half a cylindrical mold. Then if one looks into it such that its length aligns with the length of the face, one sees the face in it as long to the extent of its length, with little width. And that is because... His words: “(They are only reflected etc.)” – due to the necessity of equality of the two angles of incidence and reflection, which the commentator has explained in the discussion of vision. His words: “(the matter etc.)” – i.e., in reflection, that it is reflected to the top of the tree from a place farther from the observer, and to what is below its top from a place closer to it, due to the necessity of equality of the two angles. And the tree is seen in reflection as in the first image, whether the observer is close to the tree or connected to it, just as one sees oneself inverted because the reflected ray to one’s head is from a farther place, and to what is below one’s head from a closer place. His words: “(long to the extent of its length)” – meaning his words: “we see the face long” means we see it and we suppose it longer than it is due to the shortness of its width, and wider than it is due to the shortness of its length. His words: “(contrary to what was mentioned)” – meaning it is reflected to the top of the tree from a place farther from the observer, and to what is below its top from a place closer to it. And one should know that nearness and farness are only relative to the observer’s foot, not his eye. For if you curve your back and bring your eye close to the water, there is no doubt that the place from which the ray is reflected to the top of the tree that is on your side may be closer to your eye, and that is apparent by imagination. End.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a series of examples from sensory perception intended to prove that the senses can err, thereby establishing the need for necessary sciences that are rationally certain. The author presents three optical illusions: the apparent motion of the moon due to clouds, the inversion of a tree reflected in water, and the distortion of a face in a curved mirror. Each example is presented with a literal quotation from the original text (the matn) followed by detailed commentary. The first example explains how the moon, which actually moves west, can appear to move east when clouds move faster in the same direction; the commentary emphasizes that the error occurs because the eye is misled by the changing position relative to the cloud and attributes the motion incorrectly. The second example describes why a tree on a riverbank appears inverted in water: the reflected light rays are longer or shorter depending on the angle, and the mind, accustomed to direct vision, misinterprets the reflected rays as if they penetrate the water, leading to the illusion of inversion. The commentary adds the principle of equal angles of incidence and reflection. The third example shows how a cylindrical mirror makes a face appear stretched or distorted, with the commentary explaining that the perceived distortion results from the relative lengths of the reflected rays due to the mirror's curvature. Throughout, the author demonstrates that sensory perception is unreliable and that rational analysis is required to correct these illusions, thus arguing for the necessity of demonstrative knowledge ('ilm al-yaqini) as opposed to mere sensory experience.

Key Terms
الانعكاس
شعاع البصر
الخطأ الحسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tree is actually inverted in the water; the water is acting like a mirror that flips the image.
Correction: The inversion is an illusion caused by the reflection of light rays and the mind's assumption of straight paths. The tree is upright; the water only reflects it, and the apparent inversion is due to the geometry of the reflection.

Checkpoint
Why does the top of the tree appear to be deeper in the water than the base?
A) Because the water is deeper near the top of the tree.
B) Because the ray from the top of the tree is longer than the ray from the base, making it seem farther away.
C) Because the tree is actually upside down in the water.
D) Because the observer's eye is lower than the water surface.

Key Takeaway
Reflection illusions arise from the geometry of light rays combined with the mind's default assumption of straight-line vision, highlighting the need for rational analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about something you perceived with your own eyes, only to find out later that you were wrong. How does this simple reflection illusion challenge the level of trust you place in your senses? What are you going to do next time your senses tell you something is 'obviously' true?
?
Your mind automatically assumes that light travels in straight lines, yet this illusion proves that assumption can lead to error. What other 'straight-line' assumptions do you make about how the world works—about people, about your own abilities, about right and wrong—that might be just as misleading? Will you take a single concrete step this week to question one such assumption?
?
The author uses this illusion to show that rational analysis, not raw sensation, is needed to grasp reality. When have you relied on your feelings or immediate impressions rather than careful reasoning to make an important decision? What might that have cost you? Starting today, how will you intentionally pause and analyze before trusting your gut in matters of your deen or your life?

Face Distortion in a Curved Mirror
Third example: face distortion in a curved mirror.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a cylindrical mirror distorts the appearance of a face, illustrating that sensory perception can be systematically deceived by the shape of reflective surfaces.

Core Idea
Mirror shape determines distortion; what we see is not the true form.

The third example of sensory error involves looking at one's face in a curved mirror. The author explains that depending on the mirror's shape—cylindrical, for example—the face appears elongated or widened. This demonstrates that even the most familiar visual experiences, like seeing one's own face, can be systematically distorted by the physical properties of the reflecting surface, underscoring the need for rational judgment over sensory appearance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its commentary about the mirror distortion.
The passage is brief but contains a clear explanation of the distortion mechanism.
2
Understand the mirror geometry
The mirror is curved like half a cylinder. The alignment of the mirror's length with the face's length makes the face appear longer and narrower.
The curvature affects the reflection angles, stretching or compressing the image.
3
Recognize the perceptual error
The mind accepts the distorted image as accurate because it does not compensate for the mirror's shape.
This shows that sensory perception can be systematically distorted by external factors.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this example, along with the previous ones, builds the case that reason must override sensory data.
